Understanding Logical Subject and Predicate
Author defines logical subject and predicate with analogies from grammar and building.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject and predicate in any Islamic proposition and explain the logical relationship between them.

Core Idea
The subject is the foundation; the predicate is the roof — each proposition depends on their union.

In logic, propositions consist of two essential parts: the subject (mawdu') and the predicate (mahmul). This passage defines these terms by linking them to familiar grammatical and semantic concepts. By understanding the analogy of a building's foundation and roof, you will grasp why these terms are named as they are and how they function in every judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three layers of terminology
The author equates logical 'subject' with semantic 'musnad ilayhi' and grammatical 'mubtada'' or 'fa'il'. List these equivalences for both subject and predicate.
For predicate, logical 'mahmul' corresponds to semantic 'musnad' and grammatical 'khabar' or verb.
2
Analyze the foundation metaphor
Why is the subject called 'mawdu' (placed)? It is the base upon which predicates rest, like a building's foundation. The predicate is 'mahmul' (carried) because it depends on the subject like a roof on a foundation.
Consider the Arabic roots: wad'a (to place) and hamala (to carry).
3
Test with an Islamic example
Apply the definitions to the proposition 'Allah is All-Knowing' (الله عليم). Identify the subject (Allah) and predicate (All-Knowing). Explain that knowledge is an attribute of Allah, so it requires a subject to inhere in.
4
Understand why predicate cannot exist alone
The passage argues that a description needs a described, an action needs an agent. Show that the predicate is meaningless without the subject, just as a roof cannot float without a foundation.
This is crucial for understanding predication in Islamic theology: attributes of Allah are real but not separate from His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الموضوع في اصطلاح المنطقيين هو المعروف في المعاني بالمسند إليه وفي النحو ،بالمبتدأ، أو الفاعل والنائب عن الفاعل . والمحمول (۱) في اصطلاحهم هو المعروف في المعاني بالمسند، وفي النحو بالخبر، أو الفعل . وإنما سمي الموضوع موضوعا لأن المحمول صفة من صفات المحكوم عليه، أو فعل من أفعاله والصفة لابد لها من موصوف، والفعل لابد له من فاعل، فالأساس الذي وضع لإمكان إثبات الصفات أو نفيها المحكوم عليه، ولذا سمي موضوعا، كالأساس للبنيان . وسُمّي الآخر محمولاً لأنه كسقف البنيان، لابد له من أساس يبنى عليه . فلو قلت : زید عالم، أو زيد ضارب، فالعلم صفة زيد، والضرب فعله ولا تمكن صفةٌ بدون موصوف ولا فعل بدون فاعل، فصار المحكوم عليه كأنه وضع أساساً للحكم، فسمي موضوعاً، وسمي ما يسند إليه من صفات وأفعال محمولاً؛ لأنها لا تقوم بنفسها، فلابد لها من أساس تحمل عليه . وإذا عرفت المراد بالتصور والتصديق، وأن كلاً منهما يكون (۱) انظر للأهمية ما سيذكره المؤلف عن الموضوع والمحمول ص ۸۳.
Know that the subject (mawdu') in the terminology of logicians is what is known in semantics as the 'that which is attributed to' (musnad ilayhi) and in grammar as the subject (mubtada') or the agent (fa'il) and its substitute. And the predicate (mahmul) in their terminology is what is known in semantics as the 'attributed' (musnad) and in grammar as the predicate (khabar) or the verb. And the subject is only called 'subject' because the predicate is a description of the described, or an action of the actor, and a description necessarily has a described, and an action necessarily has an agent. So the foundation which is placed to enable the affirmation or negation of attributes is the judged-upon, and therefore it is called subject, like the foundation for a building. And the other is called predicate because it is like the roof of the building; it necessarily has a foundation upon which it is built. So if you say 'Zayd is knowledgeable' or 'Zayd is beating', knowledge is a description of Zayd and beating is his action; a description cannot exist without a described, nor an action without an agent. Thus the judged-upon becomes as if it were placed as a foundation for the judgment, so it is called subject, and what is attributed to it of descriptions and actions is called predicate because they do not subsist by themselves, so they necessarily have a foundation upon which they are carried. And once you have understood the meaning of conception and assent, and that each of them is (1) See for importance what the author will mention about the subject and predicate on page 83.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of the logical terms 'subject' and 'predicate'. The author first equates the logical subject (mawdu') with the semantic term 'musnad ilayhi' (that which is attributed to) and grammatical terms like mubtada', fa'il, etc. The logical predicate (mahmul) is equated with the semantic 'musnad' and grammatical predicates like khabar or verb. Then the author provides a rationale for the names: the subject is called 'subject' (mawdu') because it is the foundation (mawdu' literally means 'placed') for the predicate, which is either a description or an action of the subject. The predicate is called 'mahmul' (carried) because it is like a roof carried by the foundation. The example 'Zayd is knowledgeable' illustrates that knowledge is a description of Zayd, and 'Zayd is beating' shows beating as his action. Without a subject, the predicate cannot exist. So the subject is the basis for the judgment, and the predicate is what is carried upon it. The passage ends by noting that the author has already clarified the meaning of conception and assent, indicating continuity from the previous lesson.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is the foundation of the judgment
B) Because it cannot exist without a subject, like a roof needs a foundation
C) Because it is the same as the grammatical verb
D) Because it is always a description

Key Takeaway
The logical subject and predicate are defined by their functional relationship: the subject supports the predicate, just as a foundation supports a roof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'الصلاة واجبة' (Prayer is obligatory). Identify the subject and predicate, and explain why they are called mawdu' and mahmul.
?
In the Quranic verse 'الله خلق كل شيء' (Allah created everything), identify the subject and predicate. Then explain why the predicate (خلق) cannot stand without the subject (الله).

The Six Types of Indication (Dalala)
Author enumerates the six types of indication by crossing two types of indicator with three modes of indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given indication into one of the six types by distinguishing between verbal/non-verbal indicators and conventional/rational/natural modes, and explain why logicians focus on verbal-conventional indication.

Core Idea
Indication is sixfold, but the logician's path begins with one: the word by convention.

Every sign conveys meaning, but not all signs work the same way. In this passage, the author systematically maps out all possible types of indication (dalala) by crossing the nature of the indicator (verbal or non-verbal) with the mode of signification (convention, reason, or nature). This comprehensive classification is foundational for understanding later logical discussions, but the author also emphasizes that logicians only care about one type: words that signify by convention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of indicator
The indicator (dal) is either verbal (lafzi) or non-verbal (ghayr lafzi). The author asserts that complete induction (istiqra' tamm) proves there are only these two types.
2
Identify the three modes of indication
The indication (dalala) itself occurs in three modes: by convention (wad'), by reason ('aql), and by nature (tab'). These are exhaustive; no fourth mode exists.
Convention: deliberate assignment of a sign to a meaning. Reason: necessary inference (e.g., speech implies a speaker). Nature: habitual association (e.g., crying indicates pain).
3
Multiply to get six types
Multiply the two types of indicator by the three modes: 2 × 3 = 6. This combinatorial method systematically generates all possible types of indication.
4
Enumerate each type with examples
Work through each of the six: 1) verbal-conventional (e.g., 'rajul' for adult male), 2) verbal-rational (speech indicates speaker), 3) verbal-natural (scream indicates calamity), 4) non-verbal-conventional (writing, gesture, finger-counting, markers), 5) non-verbal-rational (artifact indicates maker), 6) non-verbal-natural (red face indicates shyness).
The author provides vivid examples including poetry for natural signs.
5
Note the logician's focus
The author explicitly states that only one type concerns logicians: verbal-conventional indication (dalalat al-lafz wad'an). This is the foundation for defining terms and constructing syllogisms.
Other types may be relevant in other disciplines, but logic starts with conventional language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن أنواع الدلالة محصورة في ستة أقسام لا سابع لها، وإيضاحه أن الدال إما (لفظي ) أو ( غير لفظي)، ولا ثالث لهما، وقد دل الاستقراء التام على أن دلالته ثلاثة أقسام لا رابع لها، وهي : دلالته وضعا دلالته عقلاً، دلالته طبعا . فتضرب حالتي الدال في حالات الدلالة الثلاث، فالمجموع ستة، من ضرب اثنين في ثلاثة : الأول : دلالة اللفظ وضعا كدلالة (الرجل) على الإنسان الكبير الذكر، و(المرأة) على الإنسان الأنثى، وهكذا في دلالة الألفاظ على معانيها المفردة والمركبة . والوضع في الاصطلاح هو : تعيين أمر للدلالة على أمر». الثاني : دلالة اللفظ عقلاً، كدلالته على لافظ به . الثالث : دلالة اللفظ طبعًا، أي عادةً كدلالة الصراخ على مصيبة نزلت بالصارخ ، ودلالة لفظة ( أح) - بالمهملة ـ على ألم بالصدر . الرابع: دلالة غير اللفظ وضعاً كدلالة المُفهمات الأربعة، وهي : الخط، والإشارة، والعقد، والنصب . فالنقوش التي هي الخط تدل على الألفاظ وضعا، وليست لفظا . والمراد بالعقد عقد الأصابع لبيان قدر العدد، فهو يدل على قدر العدد وضعا، وليس باللفظ . والإشارة تدل على المعنى المشار إليه وضعًا، وليست لفظا . والمراد بالنصب : نصب الحدود بين الأملاك، ونصب أعلام الطريق، وهو واضح مما تقدم. الخامس : دلالة غير اللفظ عقلاً، كدلالة المصنوعات على صانعها . السادس : دلالة غير اللفظ طبعاً، أي عادة كدلالة حُمرة الوجه على خجل صاحبه ودلالة صفرة الوجه على وجل صاحبه، أي خوفه، كما قال القائل : تفاحة جمعت لونين خلتهما خَدّي مُحِب ومحبوب قد اعتنقا تعانقا فريا واش فراعهما فاحمر ذا خجلاً واصفر ذا فرقا (۱) ومنه قول مسلم بن الوليد الأنصاري (٢) : إذا شكوتُ إليها الحبّ خفرها شكواي فاحمر خداها من الخجل إذا عرفت هذا فاعلم أن المقصود عند المنطقيين من هذه الأقسام هو واحد فقط، وهو دلالة اللفظ وضعًا، وهي تنقسم عندهم إلي الستة
Know that the types of indication are confined to six divisions, no seventh for it. Its clarification is that the indicator is either (verbal) or (non-verbal), and there is no third for them. Complete induction has shown that its indication is three divisions, no fourth for them: its indication by convention, its indication by reason, its indication by nature. So multiply the two states of the indicator by the three states of indication, the total is six, from multiplying two by three. First: indication of the word by convention, like the indication of 'ar-rajul' for the adult male human, and 'al-mar'ah' for the female human, and so on in the indication of words for their meanings, both simple and compound. Convention in terminology is: assigning something to indicate something. Second: indication of the word by reason, like its indication of its speaker. Third: indication of the word by nature, i.e., custom, like the indication of screaming for a calamity that befell the screamer, and the indication of the word 'akh' (with a dotless ha) for chest pain. Fourth: indication of the non-word by convention, like the indication of the four conveyors: writing, gesture, finger-counting, and markers. For the inscriptions which are writing indicate words by convention, and are not words. By 'finger-counting' is meant the folding of fingers to explain the amount of number, so it indicates the amount of number by convention, and is not a word. Gesture indicates the indicated meaning by convention, and is not a word. By 'markers' is meant erecting boundaries between properties, and erecting road signs, and it is clear from what preceded. Fifth: indication of the non-word by reason, like the indication of artifacts upon their maker. Sixth: indication of the non-word by nature, i.e., custom, like the indication of redness of the face for its owner's shyness, and the indication of yellowness of the face for its owner's fear, i.e., his fright, as the poet said: 'An apple combined two colors I thought them the cheeks of a lover and a beloved who embraced / They embraced, and a spy made them fear, so this reddened in shyness and this yellowed in fear.' And from it is the saying of Muslim ibn al-Walid al-Ansari: 'When I complained to her of love, my complaint made her shy, so her cheeks reddened from shyness.' If you know this, then know that what is intended by the logicians from these divisions is only one, which is the indication of the word by convention, and it is divided according to them into six.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of the types of indication (dalala). The author opens by stating that all types of indication are confined to six categories, with no seventh. The method of arriving at this number is combinatorial: the indicator (dal) is either verbal (lafzi) or non-verbal (ghair lafzi), and the mode of indication is either by convention (wad'), reason ('aql), or nature (tab'). Multiplying the two types of indicator by the three modes yields six. The author then lists each of the six, providing clear examples. The first is verbal indication by convention, e.g., the word 'rajul' indicates an adult male human; this is the standard way words signify meanings. The second is verbal indication by reason, e.g., the sound of speech indicates the existence of a speaker. The third is verbal indication by nature, e.g., a scream naturally indicates distress, and the utterance 'akh' indicates chest pain. The fourth is non-verbal indication by convention, exemplified by the four 'conveyors' (mufhimat): writing (khatt), gesture (ishara), finger-counting ('aqd), and physical markers (nasb). Writing indicates words by convention; finger-counting indicates numbers; gesture indicates meanings; markers indicate boundaries or directions. The fifth is non-verbal indication by reason, e.g., artifacts indicate their maker. The sixth is non-verbal indication by nature, e.g., reddening of the face indicates shyness, while yellowing indicates fear—illustrated with poetic verses. Finally, the author notes that for logicians, only one type is of primary concern: verbal indication by convention (dalalat al-lafz wad'an). This is the basis for all logical analysis of terms and propositions. The passage serves to delimit the scope of logic and to highlight the systematic nature of classification.

Key Terms
دلالة
دال
وضع
عقل
طبع

C) Indication of the word by convention (dalalat al-lafz wad'an)

Key Takeaway
All indication is either verbal or non-verbal, and each operates through convention, reason, or nature, yielding six types; but for the logician, only verbal conventional indication matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Three Types of Necessary Concomitants in Signification
Author classifies necessary concomitants into three types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given necessary concomitant into one of three types (mind+external, mind only, external only) and determine whether logicians or usuliyyun accept it as obligative signification.

Core Idea
Three types of necessary concomitants reveal a disciplinary dispute over obligative signification.

This passage classifies necessary concomitants (lazim) into three categories based on where the necessity holds: in the mind alone, in external reality alone, or in both. Understanding this classification is crucial for grasping how different Islamic sciences define obligative signification (dalala al-iltizam). Logicians restrict it to mental necessity, while jurists and rhetoricians include external-only necessities. The examples of 'four' entailing evenness, blindness entailing sight, and 'crow' entailing blackness illustrate the distinctions clearly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of lazim
Read the first sentence: the author lists three types: (a) necessary in mind and external together, (b) necessary in mind only, (c) necessary in external only. Notice that the first two are grouped together for logicians.
The Arabic terms are: لازم في الذهن والخارج معاً, لازم في الذهن فقط, لازم في الخارج فقط.
2
Map the disciplinary scope of obligative signification
The author states that the first two types are universally called obligative signification (dalala al-iltizam). The third type is called obligative signification only by usuliyyun and bayaniyyun, not by logicians. This shows the difference in how disciplines define the term.
Remember: logicians require the necessity to be inherent in the meaning itself (mental), not just in external reality.
3
Analyze the example of 'four' and evenness
The meaning of 'four' necessarily implies evenness in both mind and external reality. In the mind, understanding 'four' requires understanding evenness (divisible into two equal parts). In external reality, four is actually even. So this is type (a).
Evenness is a necessary concomitant of four by definition.
4
Analyze the example of blindness and sight
Blindness is defined as privation of sight. To understand privation, you must understand what is being deprived – sight. So sight is necessary in the mind when understanding blindness. In external reality, an eye that is blind lacks sight, so sight is not actually present. Thus, sight is a necessary concomitant only in the mind – type (b).
This uses the concept of muqabala al-'adam wa al-malka (opposition of privation and possession).
5
Analyze the example of 'crow' and blackness
In external reality, all crows are black, so blackness is a necessary concomitant externally. But in the mind, one can understand 'crow' without knowing it is black (e.g., imagining a white crow). Therefore, blackness is a necessary concomitant only in the external – type (c). Logicians do not consider this obligative signification.
This example highlights the key disagreement between logicians and other scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولازم في الذهن فقط، ولازم في الخارج فقط . أما الأولان - وهما اللازم في الذهن والخارج معاً، واللازم في الذهن فقط - فتسمى بهما دلالة التزام بالإجماع . وأما الثالث - وهو اللازم في الخارج فقط ـ فلا تسمى به دلالة التزام عند المنطقيين، وإنما تسمى به دلالة التزام عند الأصوليين والبيانيين . مثال اللازم في الذهن والخارج معاً : دلالة الأربعة على الزوجية، التي هي الانقسام إلى متساويين، فيلزم من فهم معنى الأربعة فهم أنها زوج أي منقسمة إلى متساويين فهذا لازم في الذهن كما ذكرنا، ولازم في الخارج أيضاً، والمراد بالخارج هنا: «الواقع في نفس الأمر، فالزوجية لازمة للأربعة في الذهن، وفي الواقع في نفس الأمر. ومثال اللازم في الذهن فقط دون الخارج: لزوم البصر للعمى ؛ لأن معنى العمى بدلالة المطابقة هو سلب البصر، فمعناه المطابقي مركب إضافي، أي مركب من مضاف هو سلب»، ومضاف إليه وهو البصر»، ولا يُعقل سلب البصر حتى يعقل معنى البصر، فظهر أنه لا يفهم أحد معنى العمى حتى يفهم معنى البصر؛ لأن معنى البصر جزء من معنى العمى؛ لأن معنى العمى مركب إضافي، والبصر أحد جزأيه، وهذا اللازم إنما هو في الذهن فقط، لا في الخارج ؛ لأن العين التي اتصفت بالعمى انتفى عنها البصر ضرورة ؛ لما بين العمى والبصر من التنافي؛ المعبر عنه بمقابلة العدم والملكة . ومثال اللازم في الخارج فقط : دلالة لفظ الغراب على السواد؛ يوجد في الخارج غراب إلا هو متصف بالسواد، ولكن هذا لا يفهم من فهم معنى الغراب ؛ لأن من لم ير الغراب قط، ولم يخبره أحد بلونه قد يتصور أن الغراب طائر أبيض، فالسواد إنما يلزم الغراب في الخارج فقط لا في الذهن، فدلالته عليه التزامية عند الأصوليين والبيانيين، وليست كذلك عند المنطقيين .
And a necessary concomitant [lazim] in the mind only, and a necessary concomitant in the external only. As for the first two - which are the necessary concomitant in mind and external together, and the necessary concomitant in the mind only - they are called by them obligative signification (dalala al-iltizam) by consensus. As for the third - which is the necessary concomitant in the external only - it is not called obligative signification according to the logicians, but rather it is called obligative signification according to the usuliyyun (jurisprudents) and bayaniyyun (rhetoricians). Example of the necessary concomitant in mind and external together: the signification of 'four' of evenness, which is divisibility into two equal parts, so from understanding the meaning of four one understands that it is even, i.e., divisible into two equal parts. This is a necessary concomitant in the mind as we mentioned, and also in the external. What is meant by 'external' here is 'the reality in itself'. So evenness is necessary for four in the mind and in the reality in itself. Example of the necessary concomitant in the mind only, not in the external: the necessity of sight for blindness; because the meaning of blindness by matching signification is the privation of sight, so its matching meaning is a relational compound, i.e., composed of a relatum which is 'privation' and a relatum-to which is 'sight'. And the privation of sight is not intelligible until the meaning of sight is understood. So it becomes clear that no one understands the meaning of blindness until he understands the meaning of sight, because the meaning of sight is part of the meaning of blindness; because the meaning of blindness is a relational compound, and sight is one of its two parts. This necessary concomitant is only in the mind, not in the external; because the eye that is characterized by blindness necessarily has sight absent from it, due to the opposition between blindness and sight, expressed as the opposition of privation and possession. Example of the necessary concomitant in the external only: the signification of the word 'crow' of blackness; for in the external there is no crow except it is characterized by blackness, but this is not understood from understanding the meaning of 'crow'; because one who has never seen a crow and no one has told him its color may imagine that a crow is a white bird. So blackness is only necessary for crow in the external, not in the mind. So its signification of it is obligative according to the usuliyyun and bayaniyyun, but not so according to the logicians.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the three types of necessary concomitants (lazim) in signification. The author first states the classification: (1) necessary in mind and external together, (2) necessary in mind only, (3) necessary in external only. He notes that the first two are unanimously called obligative signification (dalala al-iltizam) by logicians, while the third is only called obligative signification by jurists (usuliyyun) and rhetoricians (bayaniyyun). The author then provides an example for each type. For the first type: the signification of 'four' entails evenness, which is divisibility into two equal halves. This evenness is a necessary concomitant of four both in the mind (one understands it upon understanding 'four') and in external reality (four is actually even). For the second type: blindness entails the concept of sight; one cannot understand blindness without understanding sight because blindness is defined as privation of sight. However, in external reality, an eye that is blind lacks sight, so sight is not actually present. Thus, the necessary concomitant (sight) exists only in the mind. For the third type: the word 'crow' entails blackness in external reality (all crows are black), but not in the mind, because someone unfamiliar with crows might imagine them white. Therefore, according to logicians, this is not considered obligative signification because the entailment is not inherent in the meaning itself. The passage carefully distinguishes between mental and external necessity and clarifies the differing definitions of obligative signification across disciplines.

Key Terms
لازم
دلالة التزام
دلالة المطابقة
مقابلة العدم والملكة

Checkpoint
According to the logicians' classification, which type of necessary concomitant is the signification of 'crow' of blackness?
A) Necessary in mind and external
B) Necessary in mind only
C) Necessary in external only
D) Not a necessary concomitant at all

Key Takeaway
A necessary concomitant can be necessary in the mind, in external reality, or both; logicians restrict obligative signification to the first two, while jurists and rhetoricians also include the third.

The First School: Implicative Significations Are Conventional
Author presents first school on implicative significations and its causal justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the causal chain (sabab al-sabab sabab) used to justify that implicit and obligative significations are conventional.

Core Idea
The cause of the cause is a cause: linguistic convention makes matching meaning, which causes implicit and obligative meanings.

In the previous lesson, we established that matching signification is conventional by consensus. Now the author turns to the debated status of implicit and obligative significations. He presents three schools; the first, held by most logicians, argues they are also conventional. The reasoning hinges on a causal principle: the cause of the cause is a cause. Let's unpack that chain step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Match the Arabic terms: dalala al-mutabaqa (matching), dalala al-tadamun (implicit), dalala al-iltizam (obligative). Understand that matching is the whole meaning, implicit is a part of that meaning, and obligative is an external necessary concomitant.
2
Recognize the conventional status of matching
The author states that matching signification is conventional (wadi'iyya) without dispute. This means it is established by linguistic stipulation or convention.
This is the starting point for the causal chain.
3
Trace the causal chain for implicatives
The author argues: linguistic convention (wad') causes understanding of the matching meaning. Understanding the matching meaning then causes understanding of its part (implicit) and its external concomitant (obligative). Thus, the original cause (convention) indirectly causes these understandings.
The principle 'sabab al-sabab sabab' (cause of the cause is a cause) is the logical link.
4
Apply the principle to conclude conventionality
Since the cause (linguistic convention) leads to the effect (understanding matching meaning), and that effect itself causes further effects (understanding implict and obligative), those further effects share the same conventional status as the original effect. Therefore, implicit and obligative significations are also conventional.
This is the first school's reasoning; the author notes it is the view of most logicians.
5
Position this within the three schools
The passage here only details the first school. The author mentions there are three schools total, but the explanation of the other two is in subsequent pages.
Do not assume the other schools; they will be covered later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن دلالة المطابقة وضعية بلا خلاف، وأما دلالة التضمن ودلالة الالتزام ففيهما لأهل العلم ثلاثة مذاهب : الأول : أنهما وضعيتان أيضاً، وعلى هذا القول عامة المنطقيين، ووجهه أن سبب السبب سبب ؛ لأن فهم المعنى المطابقي هو سبب فهم جزئه في ضمن كله وهو دلالة التضمن، وهو سبب فهم اللازم الخارج عن المسمى، وهو دلالة الالتزام، فلما كان وضع اللفظ سبباً لفهم المعنى المطابَقي وفهم المعنى المطابقي سببًا في فهم الجزء في ضمن الكل، وفهم الخارج اللازم، صارت كل من التضمن والالتزام
And know that matching signification (dalala al-mutabaqa) is conventional (wadi'iyya) without dispute. As for implicit signification (dalala al-tadamun) and obligative signification (dalala al-iltizam), there are three schools among the people of knowledge concerning them: First: that they are also conventional. And this is the position of the majority of logicians. Its justification is that the cause of the cause is a cause; because understanding the matching meaning is the cause of understanding its part within its whole, which is implicit signification, and it is the cause of understanding the necessary concomitant external to the named, which is obligative signification. So when the placing of the word is a cause for understanding the matching meaning, and understanding the matching meaning is a cause for understanding the part within the whole and understanding the external necessary concomitant, both implicit and obligative become
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage opens a new topic: the status of implicit (tadamun) and obligative (iltizam) significations as conventional (wadi'iyya) or not. The author states three schools exist among scholars. Only the first school is detailed here: that both implicit and obligative significations are conventional. This is the view of most logicians. The reasoning is based on the principle that 'the cause of the cause is a cause' (sabab al-sabab sabab). The chain of causation is as follows: the linguistic convention (wad' al-lafz) is the cause of understanding the matching meaning (dalala al-mutabaqa). Understanding the matching meaning is then the cause of understanding its part (implicit signification) and also the cause of understanding the external necessary concomitant (obligative signification). Since the original cause leads to these effects indirectly, the effects are also considered conventional. Therefore, implicit and obligative significations share the same conventional status as matching signification. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating that the remaining two schools will follow in later pages. The logical chain presented is a clear example of causal reasoning in Islamic semantics.

Mental vs. External Necessary Implication
Distinction between mental and external necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental and external necessary implications and correctly classify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Not all necessary implications are equal: some are conceptual, others merely empirical.

In this lesson, we explore a key distinction in the theory of signification: the difference between a necessary concomitant that lies in the mind (mental) and one that only occurs in external reality (external). The passage gives two clear examples: blindness necessarily implies sight in the mind, while crow implies blackness only in external reality. Understanding this difference is crucial for evaluating arguments that rely on implicative signification (dalāla iltizāmiyya), especially in uṣūl al-fiqh and logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the signified term (al-madlūl) and its concomitant
For each example, find the word being defined (e.g., 'blindness', 'crow') and the property said to be necessarily implied (e.g., 'sight', 'blackness').
2
Test whether the concomitant is part of the concept
Ask: Can the signified term be understood without understanding the concomitant? If no, it is mental. If yes, it is external. Blindness cannot be understood without sight; crow can be imagined without blackness.
3
Classify the implication as mental or external
Based on the conceptual necessity, label the relationship: mental (lāzim dhihnī) if the concomitant is conceptually required; external (lāzim khārijī) if only empirically co-occurrent.
The passage gives two clear cases: blindness-sight = mental; crow-blackness = external.
4
Recognize the disciplinary disagreement
Note that uṣūliyyūn and bayāniyyūn accept both types as implicative signification, but logicians restrict it to mental implication only. This affects how one uses such implications in argumentation.
This disagreement matters in theological and legal reasoning where certainty is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفهم أحد معنى العمى حتى يفهم معنى البصر؛ لأن معنى البصر جزء من معنى العمى؛ لأن معنى العمى مركب إضافي، والبصر أحد جزأيه، وهذا اللازم إنما هو في الذهن فقط، لا في الخارج؛ لأن العين التي اتصفت بالعمى انتفى عنها البصر ضرورة؛ لما بين العمى والبصر من التنافي؛ المعبر عنه بمقابلة العدم والملكة. ومثال اللازم في الخارج فقط: دلالة لفظ الغراب على السواد؛ يوجد في الخارج غراب إلا هو متصف بالسواد، ولكن هذا لا يفهم من فهم معنى الغراب؛ لأن من لم ير الغراب قط، ولم يخبره أحد بلونه قد يتصور أن الغراب طائر أبيض، فالسواد إنما يلزم الغراب في الخارج فقط لا في الذهن، فدلالته عليه التزامية عند الأصوليين والبيانيين، وليست كذلك عند المنطقيين.
One does not understand the meaning of blindness until one understands the meaning of sight; because the meaning of sight is part of the meaning of blindness; because the meaning of blindness is a relative compound, and sight is one of its two parts. And this necessary implication is only in the mind, not in external reality; because the eye that is described as blindness necessarily has sight negated from it, due to the incompatibility between blindness and sight, expressed as the opposition of privation and possession. An example of necessary implication in external reality only: the indication of the word 'crow' to blackness; there is no crow in external reality except it is described with blackness, but this is not understood from understanding the meaning of 'crow'; because someone who has never seen a crow, and no one has informed him of its color, might imagine that a crow is a white bird. So blackness only accompanies the crow in external reality, not in the mind. So its indication of it is implicative according to the uṣūliyyūn and bayāniyyūn, but not so according to the logicians.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage defines two types of necessary implication (لازم) that a term can have: mental (ذهنی) and external (خارجی). The author first gives the example of blindness and sight: understanding blindness requires understanding sight because blindness is defined relative to the absence of sight. This necessity is mental—it lies in the conceptual relationship. In external reality, blindness and sight cannot coexist; they are opposites (مقابلة العدم والملكة). So the implication is mental only. Next, the example of the word 'crow' and its blackness: in external reality, crows are always black, but the concept of crow does not require blackness; one could think of a white crow. Thus, blackness is an external necessary concomitant, not a mental one. The author notes that uṣūliyyūn and bayāniyyūn (specialists in Islamic jurisprudence and rhetoric) classify this as implicative signification (دلالة التزامية), but logicians do not, because the implication is not based on conceptual necessity but on external reality.

Key Terms

اللازم الخارجي
دلالة التزامية

The distinction between mental and external necessary implication determines whether a concomitant can be considered a true implicative signification in logic.

Three Views on Inclusive and Implicative Signification
Author lists three scholarly opinions on whether inclusive and implicative signification are conventional or rational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three scholarly views on the nature of inclusive and implicative signification and identify their justifications.

Core Idea
The debate: is extra-conformity meaning revealed by convention or by reason?

In the study of signification (dalāla), there is unanimous agreement that conformity (muṭābaqa) is conventional. However, when a word indicates a part of its meaning (inclusion) or an external necessary concomitant (implication), scholars disagree on whether these are also conventional or purely rational. This lesson unpacks the three main positions and their reasoning, a critical distinction for usul al-fiqh and logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
First, note the three types: conformity (dalālat al-muṭābaqa), inclusion (dalālat al-taḍamun), and implication (dalālat al-iltizām). The passage states that conformity is unanimously conventional. Now focus on the two disputed types.
For example, the word 'house' by conformity signifies the entire building; by inclusion signifies a room (part); by implication signifies the builder (external concomitant).
2
Extract the first view: both are conventional
The first view, held by most logicians, argues that inclusion and implication are also conventional. The reasoning is causal: the word (waḍʿ) causes understanding of the conforming meaning, which in turn causes understanding of the part and the concomitant. Since the cause of a cause is a cause, the word is the ultimate cause, making all three conventional.
The principle 'cause of a cause is a cause' (sabab al-sabab sabab) is key to this argument.
3
Extract the second view: both are rational
The second view, held by most rhetoricians (bayāniyyūn), holds that the word is only posited for the conforming meaning. The intellect independently deduces the part and the concomitant from that understanding, so inclusion and implication are rational, not conventional.
This view emphasizes the role of the intellect (ʿaql) in deriving extra-conformity meanings.
4
Extract the third view: inclusion conventional, implication rational
The third view, held by the majority of uṣūliyyūn (legal theorists), takes a middle position. Inclusion is conventional because it is part of the conforming meaning, and a part of the conventional is conventional. Implication is rational because it is external to the named and requires intellectual inference.
This compromise reflects the uṣūlī concern for linguistic precision while acknowledging rational inference for external implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن دلالة المطابقة وضعية بلا خلاف، وأما دلالة التضمن ودلالة الالتزام ففيهما لأهل العلم ثلاثة مذاهب: الأول: أنهما وضعيتان أيضاً، وعلى هذا القول عامة المنطقيين، ووجهه أن سبب السبب سبب؛ لأن فهم المعنى المطابقي هو سبب فهم جزئه في ضمن كله وهو دلالة التضمن، وهو سبب فهم اللازم الخارج عن المسمى، وهو دلالة الالتزام، فلما كان وضع اللفظ سبباً لفهم المعنى المطابَقي وفهم المعنى المطابقي سببًا في فهم الجزء في ضمن الكل، وفهم الخارج اللازم، صارت كل من التضمن والالتزام وضعية؛ لأن السبب الأول في فهمهما الوضع، وسبب السبب سبب. المذهب الثاني: أنهما عقليتان وعليه عامة البيانيين، ووجهه أن اللفظ إنما وضع للمعنى المطابقي، ولكن العقل هو الذي فهم منه الجزء في ضمن الكل، والخارج اللازم. المذهب الثالث: أن دلالة التضمن وضعية ودلالة الالتزام عقلية، ووجهه أن المدلول عليه بالتضمن جزء المدلول المطابقي، والمطابقة وضعية بلا خلاف، وجزء الوضعي وضعي؛ لأن الجزء مندرج في الكل، أما دلالة الالتزام فليست جزء الوضعي، ولكن العقل فهم من المعنى المطابقي لازمه الخارج عن مسماه، وعلى هذا جماهير من الأصوليين.
Know that signification by conformity is conventional without disagreement. As for signification by inclusion and signification by implication, there are three schools among the people of knowledge: First: They are both conventional as well. This is the view of the majority of logicians, and its justification is that the cause of the cause is a cause. Because understanding the conforming meaning is the cause of understanding its part within the whole—which is signification by inclusion—and it is the cause of understanding the necessary concomitant external to the named—which is signification by implication. So since the positing of the word is the cause of understanding the conforming meaning, and understanding the conforming meaning is a cause for understanding the part within the whole and the external necessary concomitant, both inclusion and implication become conventional, because the primary cause for understanding them is the positing (al-waḍʿ), and the cause of the cause is a cause. Second school: They are both rational. This is the view of the majority of bayāniyyūn, and its justification is that the word was only posited for the conforming meaning, but it is the intellect that understands from it the part within the whole and the external necessary concomitant. Third school: Signification by inclusion is conventional, and signification by implication is rational. Its justification is that what is indicated by inclusion is part of the indicated conforming meaning, and conformity is conventional without disagreement, and a part of the conventional is conventional because the part is included in the whole. As for signification by implication, it is not part of the conventional, but the intellect understands from the conforming meaning its necessary concomitant external to the named. This is the view of the majority of uṣūliyyūn.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage enumerates three scholarly positions on the nature of inclusive (تضمن) and implicative (التزام) signification. First, the author reminds that signification by conformity (مطابقة) is unanimously conventional (وضعي). Then he presents the three views. The first view holds that both inclusion and implication are conventional, which is the position of most logicians. Their reasoning is causal: the word (the first cause) causes understanding of the conforming meaning, and that understanding in turn causes understanding of the included part and implied concomitant. Since the cause of a cause is a cause, the word is ultimately the cause of all three, making them all conventional. The second view holds that both are rational (عقليان), the position of most bayāniyyūn (rhetoricians). Their argument: the word is only posited for the conforming meaning; the intellect independently deduces the part and the external concomitant. The third view is a compromise: inclusion is conventional (because it is part of the conforming meaning, and part of what is conventional is conventional), but implication is rational (because it is external to the named and requires intellectual deduction). This third view is held by the majority of uṣūliyyūn (Islamic legal theorists).

دلالة التضمن
دلالة الالتزام

Key Takeaway
The nature of inclusion and implication signification is debated: logicians often deem both conventional, rhetoricians deem both rational, and uṣūliyyūn split the difference, making inclusion conventional and implication rational.

Classifying Utterances: Mufrad and Murakkab
Author defines compound utterance and its exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Arabic utterance as mufrad or murakkab according to the logicians' definition, and explain each exclusion.

Core Idea
Compound utterances have parts that intentionally and purely indicate parts of meaning; everything else is simple.

In this lesson, we dive into the foundational classification of utterances (alfaz) in Islamic logic. The author presents a rigorous definition of compound (murakkab) and then derives the simple (mufrad) by exclusion. Understanding this distinction is crucial because it underlies how logicians treat definitions and arguments. We will walk through the definition step by step, examining each qualifier and what it excludes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the bipartite division
The author states that every utterance is either compound or simple. This is the fundamental division according to the majority of logicians.
2
Memorize the compound definition
The definition is: 'ما دل جزؤه على جزء معناه دلالة مقصودة خالصة' (that whose part indicates a part of its meaning with an intentional pure indication). Break it down into four qualifiers: (1) the utterance has a part that indicates, (2) indicates a part of its meaning, (3) the indication is intentional, (4) the indication is pure.
3
List exclusions for each qualifier
For each qualifier, identify what is excluded: (1) utterances with no parts (prepositions) or parts with no indication (letters in 'Zaid'); (2) utterances like 'Ka'abkam' where parts indicate unrelated meanings; (3) genitive proper names like 'Abdullah' where indication is not intentional; (4) cases like 'Abdullah' used with the intention of servitude but mixed with proper naming.
The author gives concrete Arabic examples for each.
4
Derive the simple utterance
The simple is everything that fails any of the four conditions. This includes prepositions, single letters, unanalyzable words, and proper names like 'Abdullah'.
5
Recognize terminological shifts
The author notes that in other logical discussions (tasawwur/tasdiq), genitive and attributive compounds are considered simple because they do not constitute a complete statement. Be aware that 'mufrad' may have different meanings in different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الألفاظ
المباحث جمع مبحث وهو اسم مكان بمعنى مكان البحث وهو الفحص والتفتيش عن الألفاظ من حيث التركيب والإفراد ونحو ذلك كالكلية والجزئية .
اعلم أولاً أن اللفظ المستعمل في اللغة لا يخلو من أحد أمرين : إما أن يكون مركباً، وإما أن يكون مفرداً، وهذا على قول الجمهور بأن القسمة ثنائية .
وضابط المركب عندهم : هو ما يدل جزؤه على جزء معناه دلالةً مقصودة خالصة».
فخرج بقولهم : ما دل جزؤه» ما لا جزء له أصلاً، كباء الجر ولامه، وما له جزء لا دلالة له على شيء، كبعض حروف (زيد).
وخرج بقولهم : على جزء معناه ماله جزء وله دلالة لكن لا على جزء معناه كـ (كأبكم)، فإن نصفه الأول وهو (أب) يدل على ذاتِ متصفة بالأبوة، ونصفه الأخير وهو (كم) يدل على سؤال عن عدد، أو إخبار بعددٍ كثير، ولكن ليس واحد منهما يدل على جزء المعنى المقصود بالأبكم .
وخرج بقولهم : «مقصودة» العلم الإضافي كـ(عبدالله)؛ فإن جزأه الأول يدل على العبد، والثاني يدل على الخالق - جل وعلا - وهذه الدلالة ليست مقصودة؛ لأن المقصود جعل اللفظين علمًا للشخص مُعيَّنا له عن غيره من الأشخاص.
وخرج بقولهم «خالصة» ما لو قُصد في تسمية الإنسان بـ (عبدالله) مثلاً أنه متصف بالعبودية لله؛ فإن دلالة جزء اللفظ على جزء المعنى حينئذ مقصودة، لكنها غير خالصة من شائبة العلمية .
وبهذا تعلم أن المفرد هو ما لا جزء له أصلاً، كباء الجر ولامه، أو له جزء لا دلالة له على شيء، أو له جزء وله دلالة لكن لا على جزء معناه أو له جزء و له دلالة على جزء معناه ولكنها دلالة غير مقصودة.
أو له جزء وله دلالة على جزء مسماه دلالة مقصودة لكنها غيرُ خالصة من شائبة العلمية .
وقد تركنا بعض المناقشات هنا لعدم اتجاهه عندنا .
وقد عرفت من هذا التعريف الذي ذكروا أن المركب هنا صادق بالمركب الإسنادي نحو (زيد قائم) والمركب الإضافي كـ (غلام زيد)، والمركب التقييدي كـ (الحيوان الناطق)، وأن المفرد هو الاسم أو الفعل أو الحرف، ويدخل في الاسم العلم الإضافي كـ (عبدالله) و (عبد شمس).
وما تقدم في حدهم للتصور والتصديق، يدل على أن المركب الإضافي والمركب التقييدي من أنواع المفرد؛ لأن إدراكهما تصور لا تصديق، والتصور إدراك معنى المفرد، خلافا لما يذكرون هنا .
والظاهر أن بعض الاصطلاحات تختلف في المفرد، فهو في التصور والتصديق : كل ماليس بإسناد خبري تام، وفي مبحث المركب والمفرد يكون له اصطلاح آخر، فيدخل فيه المركب .
Discussions of Utterances. 'Al-Mabahith' is the plural of 'mabhas', which is a noun of place meaning the place of research, which is examination and investigation of utterances from the aspect of composition and simplicity, and so forth, like universality and particularity. Know first that an utterance used in language is not free from one of two things: either it is compound, or it is simple, and this is according to the majority that the division is bipartite. The definition of compound according to them: it is that whose part indicates a part of its meaning with an intentional pure indication. So by their statement 'that whose part indicates' is excluded that which has no part at all, like the preposition 'ba' and its 'lam', and that which has a part that does not indicate anything, like some letters of 'Zaid'. And by their statement 'a part of its meaning' is excluded that which has a part and has indication but not a part of its meaning, like 'Ka'abkam', for its first half which is 'ab' indicates an entity characterized by fatherhood, and its last half which is 'kam' indicates a question about number, or information about a large number, but neither of them indicates a part of the meaning intended by 'Ka'abkam'. And by their statement 'intentional' is excluded the relational proper name like 'Abdullah'; for its first part indicates the slave, and the second indicates the Creator - Glorified and Exalted - and this indication is not intentional, because the intention is to make the two words a proper name for the person, specifying him from other persons. And by their statement 'pure' is excluded that if one intends in naming a person 'Abdullah', for example, that he is characterized by servitude to Allah; for then the indication of the part of the utterance to the part of the meaning is intentional, but it is not pure from the admixture of proper naming. By this you know that the simple is that which has no part at all, like the preposition 'ba' and its 'lam', or has a part that has no indication of anything, or has a part and has indication but not on a part of its meaning, or has a part and has indication on a part of its meaning but that indication is unintentional, or has a part and has indication on a part of its named object with intentional indication but it is not pure from the admixture of proper naming. And we have left some discussions here because they are not sound according to us. And you have known from this definition they mentioned that compound here applies to the predicative compound like 'Zaid is standing', the genitive compound like 'slave of Zaid', and the attributive compound like 'the rational animal', and that the simple is the noun, verb, or particle, and included in the noun is the relational proper name like 'Abdullah' and 'Abd Shams'. And what preceded in their definition of conception and assent indicates that the genitive compound and the attributive compound are types of the simple, because grasping them is conception not assent, and conception is grasping the meaning of the simple, contrary to what they mention here. And it is apparent that some terminologies differ regarding the simple: with respect to conception and assent it is everything that is not a complete predicative statement, while in the topic of compound and simple it has another terminology, and thus the compound enters into it.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of the classification of utterances (alfaz) into compound (murakkab) and simple (mufrad). The author begins by stating that the discussion (mabhas) of utterances covers them from the perspective of composition (tarkib) and simplicity (ifrad), as well as universality and particularity. The key claim is that any used utterance is either compound or simple, following the majority's bipartite division. The core definition is given: a compound utterance is one whose part indicates a part of its meaning with an intentional and pure indication. The author then unpacks each qualifier by showing what it excludes. First, the phrase 'that whose part indicates' excludes utterances with no parts (like prepositions) or whose parts give no indication (like certain letters in 'Zaid'). Second, 'a part of its meaning' excludes utterances like 'Ka'abkam' (deaf-mute), where each part indicates something unrelated to the overall meaning. Third, 'intentional' excludes genitive proper names like 'Abdullah', where the parts indicate their literal meanings (slave, God) but the speaker's intention is to create a proper name, not to convey those meanings. Fourth, 'pure' excludes cases where the intention is present but mixed with the proper-naming aspect, as when someone is named 'Abdullah' with the meaning of being a servant of God, but the name still functions as a proper name. From these exclusions, the simple utterance is defined negatively as anything that fails any of the conditions. The author then clarifies that according to this definition, compound includes three types: predicative (isnadi), genitive (idafi), and attributive (taqyidi). Simple includes nouns, verbs, and particles; genitive proper names like 'Abdullah' fall under nouns and thus are simple. However, the author notes a terminological shift: in the earlier discussion of conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), genitive and attributive compounds were considered simple because they are grasped as a single conception, not as an assent. Thus, 'mufrad' in that context means anything that is not a complete declarative sentence, whereas in the taxonomy of utterances it has a different definition that admits some compounds. This illustrates how logical terminology can vary across different discussions. The passage demonstrates careful conceptual navigation and warns against conflating contexts.

Key Terms
المفرد
المركب
دلالة مقصودة
دلالة خالصة
العلم الإضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, the proper name 'Abdullah' in the utterance classification is considered:
A) Murakkab because its parts indicate 'abd and Allah
B) Mufrad because the indication of its parts is not intentional
C) Murakkab because it is a genitive compound
D) Mufrad because it has no parts

Key Takeaway
A compound utterance's parts must intentionally and purely indicate parts of its meaning; otherwise, the utterance is simple, even if it has parts.

Defining Mufrad: The Five Cases and the Purity Condition
Author defines mufrad with conditions and lists its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five cases of mufrad and determine whether a given term qualifies as mufrad based on the condition of purity (khalisa).

Core Idea
Mufrad is not simply 'no parts'—it depends on how parts indicate meaning, including a purity condition.

In this passage, the author expands on the definition of mufrad by excluding terms where part-indication is 'pure' from proper-noun-ness. He lists five cases that qualify as mufrad. This lesson will walk you through those cases and the crucial condition of khalisa (purity).

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose of the condition 'khalisa'
The author uses the example of 'Abdullah' to show that even when a part indicates a part of the meaning, if the indication is not pure (due to being a proper noun), it is still mufrad.
The word 'Abdullah' has parts 'Abd' and 'Allah' that indicate meaning, but the overall term is a proper noun, so the indication is not pure.
2
Extract the five cases of mufrad
From the passage, identify each case: (1) no parts at all, (2) parts with no indication, (3) parts with indication but not of part of meaning, (4) parts with indication but not intended, (5) parts with indication intended but not pure.
These are disjunctive; a term is mufrad if it satisfies any one of them.
3
Check the definition against examples
Test the examples: ba and lam are mufrad by case 1; 'Abdullah' is mufrad by case 5.
4
Contrast with murakkab
When indication is intended and pure (e.g., 'Abdullah' if it were purely descriptive without proper-noun-ness), it becomes murakkab.
The purity condition is the key differentiator.

And by their saying 'pure' is excluded that which is intended in naming a person 'Abdullah' for example that he is described with servitude to Allah; for then the indication of the part of the word to the part of the meaning is intended, but it is not pure from the taint of proper-noun-ness. And by this you know that the singular is that which has no part at all, like the preposition ba and lam; or has a part with no indication of anything; or has a part with indication but not of a part of its meaning; or has a part with indication of a part of its meaning but that indication is not intended; or has a part with indication of a part of its named thing, intended indication but not pure from the taint of proper-noun-ness. And we have left some discussions here because they are not appropriate according to us. And you have known from this definition that they mentioned that the composite here applies to the predicative composite like 'Zaid standing', and the annexation composite like 'slave of Zaid', and the restrictive composite like 'rational animal'. And that the singular is the noun, verb, or particle, and includes in the noun the annexation proper noun like 'Abdullah' and 'Abd Shams. And what preceded in their definition of conception and assent indicates that the annexation composite and restrictive composite are types of singular; because their apprehension is conception not assent, and conception is apprehension of the meaning of the singular, contrary to what they mention here. And apparently some conventions differ regarding the singular: in conception and assent it is everything that is not a complete predicative statement, and in the topic of composite and singular it has another convention, so the annexation and restrictive composites enter into it, for example.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'mufrad' (singular) by excluding the condition 'khalisa' (pure). The author uses the example 'Abdullah' to illustrate that although it has parts with meaning (Abd and Allah), the intended meaning is not purely descriptive because it functions as a proper noun. The definition of mufrad is then given in five cases: (1) having no parts at all, (2) having a part with no meaning, (3) having a part with meaning but not of the whole meaning, (4) having a part with meaning but the indication is not intended, (5) having a part with meaning intended but not pure from the taint of proper-noun-ness. The passage then notes that according to this definition, murakkab (composite) includes three types: predicative (e.g., 'Zaid standing'), annexation (e.g., 'slave of Zaid'), and restrictive (e.g., 'rational animal'). Mufrad includes nouns, verbs, and particles, with proper nouns like 'Abdullah' and 'Abd Shams being included. A contradiction is observed: earlier in the discussion of tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent), annexation and restrictive composites were considered singular, whereas here they are called composite. The author resolves this by noting that terminology differs between these two contexts. In tasawwur/tasdiq, mufrad is defined as anything that is not a complete predicative statement, while in the topic of composite/singular, it has a different convention that includes annexation and restrictive composites as mufrad.

Key Takeaway
Mufrad is defined by a disjunction of conditions, with the purity condition being the key differentiator from murakkab.

Conventions of Mufrad: Tasawwur/Tasdiq vs. the Topic of Composite/Singular
Author clarifies the difference in convention regarding mufrad between tasawwur/tasdiq and murakkab/mufrad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an annexation or restrictive composite is considered mufrad or murakkab depending on the logical context.

Core Idea
The classification of composites like 'Abdullah' as mufrad or murakkab depends on which convention you follow.

The passage reveals a tension: earlier, in the discussion of tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent), annexation and restrictive composites were treated as mufrad. But here, they are listed as types of murakkab. The author resolves this by noting that different topics use different conventions. This lesson explores that shift and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two contexts
The passage mentions two contexts: (a) the definition of tasawwur and tasdiq, and (b) the topic of murakkab/mufrad.
These contexts have different criteria for what counts as mufrad.
2
Apply the rule from tasawwur/tasdiq
In that context, mufrad is anything that is not a complete predicative statement (isnadi). Therefore, annexation and restrictive composites are mufrad because they do not form a complete sentence.
For example, 'غلام زيد' (slave of Zaid) is not a complete statement, so it is mufrad in this context.
3
Apply the rule from the composite/singular topic
Here, mufrad is defined by the part-indication conditions. Composites with parts that indicate meaning and are intended and pure are murakkab. However, annexation composites like 'Abdullah' have intended indication but not pure, so they are still mufrad in this topic.
The purity condition from the previous lesson applies here.
4
Note the resolution
The author says that terminology differs, so both classifications can be valid in their own context. This prevents confusion.
The key is to be aware of which convention is being used.

The Division of Singular Terms into Universal and Particular
Author divides mufrad into kulli and juz'i and defines kulli.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a universal term and a particular term based on the criterion of preventing sharing as defined in the passage.

Core Idea
A universal term's meaning allows sharing among many; a particular term's meaning does not.

This lesson introduces the foundational division of singular terms (mufrad) into universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i). The author presents an exhaustive division with no third category. Understanding this distinction is crucial for all subsequent logical analysis, especially in defining and classifying concepts in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
The author divides mufrad into exactly two parts: kulli (universal) and juz'i (particular). Recognize that this is an exhaustive division—every singular term falls into one of these categories.
2
Grasp the first definition of kulli
The first definition: the universal is 'that which does not prevent sharing in its meaning.' This means the term can be applied to multiple individuals without contradiction. For example, 'human' can be shared by Zaid, Amr, and Khadija.
The author uses examples like 'human', 'animal', 'man', woman', 'lion'.
3
Apply the definition to examples
Test the given examples: 'human' and 'animal' clearly allow sharing. Consider proper names like 'Zaid'—they prevent sharing (only one Zaid is intended), so they are particular.
The passage implies that any term that prevents sharing is particular.
4
Contrast with particular
The particular is the opposite: it prevents sharing. Though not explicitly defined here, it is implied. Think of terms that refer to a specific individual or instance.
The author will likely define juz'i in the continuation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا علمت هذا فاعلم أن المفرد بالاصطلاح المذكور هنا ينقسم إلى قسمين لا ثالث لهما ; لأنه إما أن يكون كليًا وإما أن يكون جزئيًا . وضابط الكلي في الاصطلاح أنه هو مالا يمنع تعقل مدلوله من وقوع الشركة فيه، كالإنسان والحيوان والرجل والمرأة والأسد ونحو ذلك ; فإنك إذ تعقلت معنى الإنسان لم يمنعك تعقله من وقوع الشركة فيه، فهو قدر مشترك يشترك فيه عمرو وزيد وخالد، وهكذا في باقي الأمثلة . وإن شئت قلت في حد الكلي : هو المفرد الذي لا يمنع تعقل مدلوله من حمله حمل مواطأة على أفراد كثيرة . من والمراد بحمل المواطأة هو حمله عليها بنفسه، من غير احتياج إلى اشتقاق أو إضافة ، فالإنسان مثلاً إذا تعقلت مدلوله لم يمنعك ذلك حمله حمل مواطأة على كثيرين كأن تقول: زيد إنسان، وعمرو إنسان، وخالد إنسان ،وهكذا، وكذلك الحيوان لا يمنعك تعقل مدلوله من حمله حمل مواطأة على كثيرين، كقولك : الإنسان حيوان، والفرس حيوان، والجمل حيوان، وهكذا. وأما إن كان لا يمكن حمله عليها حمل مواطأة، بل حمل اشتقاق أو إضافة، فليس كليًا لها، فليس العلم مثلاً كليا بالنسبة إلى الأشخاص العلماء; لأنك لا تقول : مالك عِلم، والشافعي علم، وإنما يصح في ذلك الحمل بالاشتقاق، كقولك : مالك عالم، والشافعي عالم، أو
And when you know this, know that the singular according to the mentioned convention divides into two parts with no third: because it is either universal or particular. The rule of the universal in terminology is that it is that whose meaning does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it, like 'human', 'animal', 'man', 'woman', 'lion', and so on; because when you conceive the meaning of 'human', its conception does not prevent you from the occurrence of sharing in it, so it is a common capacity shared by Amr, Zaid, and Khalid, and likewise in the remaining examples. And if you wish you may say in the definition of the universal: it is the singular whose meaning does not prevent predication by way of identity (haml muwata'ah) upon many individuals. What is meant by identity predication is predication upon them essentially, without need for derivation or annexation. So for example 'human', when you conceive its meaning, that does not prevent you from predicating it by identity predication upon many, as you say: Zaid is human, Amr is human, Khalid is human, and so on. Likewise 'animal' does not prevent you from conceiving its meaning from predicating it by identity predication upon many, as you say: human is an animal, horse is an animal, camel is an animal, and so on. But if it cannot be predicated upon them by identity predication, but rather by derivation or annexation, then it is not universal for them. So for example 'knowledge' is not universal with respect to the scholars, because you do not say: Malik is knowledge, al-Shafi'i is knowledge, but rather in that case predication by derivation is correct, as you say: Malik is knowledgeable, al-Shafi'i is knowledgeable, or
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage divides mufrad (singular) into two categories: universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i). It then provides two equivalent definitions of kulli. The first definition: a term whose meaning does not prevent sharing (i.e., it can be common to many individuals). Examples given include 'human', 'animal', 'man', 'woman', 'lion'. The meaning of 'human' when conceived does not prevent it from being shared by multiple persons like Amr, Zaid, Khalid. The second definition: a term that can be predicated by haml muwata'ah (identity predication) on many individuals. Haml muwata'ah means predicating the term itself without derivation or annexation. For instance, 'human' is predicated identically on Zaid, Amr, Khalid. 'Animal' is predicated identically on human, horse, camel. In contrast, terms like 'knowledge' cannot be predicated by identity on scholars; one cannot say 'Malik is knowledge'; rather, derivation is used: 'Malik is knowledgeable'. The passage cuts off while discussing another type of predication (likely haml bi al-idafah or haml bi al-ishtiqaq), which would further clarify the boundary of universality.

Defining the Universal through Identity Predication
Author divides mufrad into kulli and juz'i and defines kulli.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a term is universal by checking if it can be predicated by haml muwata'ah on many individuals, and distinguish it from terms requiring derivation.

Core Idea
A universal term can be affirmed of many by simple identity predication, without derivation or annexation.

This lesson deepens the understanding of the universal by focusing on the second definition provided by the author: the universal is a term that can be predicated by haml muwata'ah (identity predication) on many individuals. This test is practical and clarifies why terms like 'knowledge' are not universal in the same sense.

Guided Path
1
Learn the second definition of kulli
The author states: 'If you wish, you may say in the definition of the universal: it is the singular whose meaning does not prevent predication by way of identity (haml muwata'ah) upon many individuals.' This is an operational test for universality.
Haml muwata'ah means the predicate is applied in its essential form, not derived or annexed.
2
Analyze examples of identity predication
For 'human', you can say 'Zaid is human', 'Amr is human', etc. For 'animal', you can say 'Human is animal', 'Horse is animal'. The predicate is unchanged.
This works because these terms are universals.
3
Differentiate from derivation predication
Contrast with 'knowledge': you cannot say 'Zaid is knowledge' (identity), but must say 'Zaid is knowledgeable' (derivation). This shows 'knowledge' is not a universal for individuals who possess it.
The author explicitly uses this contrast to illustrate the boundary.
4
Test universality using the definition
Pick a term and try to predicate it by identity on multiple individuals. If possible, it is universal. If not, it is either particular or requires derivation/annexation.
Remember that a term might be universal in one domain but not another (e.g., 'knowledge' is universal for different types of knowledge but not for individuals).
