The Universal and the Particular: Core Definitions
Author defines universal and particular with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a singular term is universal or particular by applying the definition of kullī and juzʾī and the test of ḥaml muwāṭaʾa.

Core Idea
A universal can be predicated directly on many; a particular cannot.

The author lays out the foundational division of singular terms into universal (kullī) and particular (juzʾī). Using the test of 'predicative predication' (ḥaml muwāṭaʾa), you can determine which category a term belongs to. This distinction is crucial for constructing and evaluating definitions in Islamic disciplines such as uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term's meaning
Consider what the term signifies. For example, 'human' signifies a rational animal.
2
Check if the meaning can be shared by many
Does conceptualizing the meaning prevent it from applying to multiple individuals? For 'human', it does not; for 'Zayd', it does.
3
Apply the test of ḥaml muwāṭaʾa
Can you predicate the term directly on multiple individuals without derivation or annexation? For 'human', you can say 'Zayd is a human', 'Amr is a human'. For 'knowledge', you cannot say 'Zayd is knowledge', but only 'Zayd is knowledgeable' (derivation).
4
Classify as universal or particular
If the term passes both tests (sharing and direct predication), it is universal; otherwise, it is particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا علمت هذا فاعلم أن المفرد بالاصطلاح المذكور هنا ينقسم إلى قسمين لا ثالث لهما؛ لأنه إما أن يكون كليًا وإما أن يكون جزئيًا.
وضابط الكلي في الاصطلاح أنه هو ما لا يمنع تعقل مدلوله من وقوع الشركة فيه، كالإنسان والحيوان والرجل والمرأة والأسد ونحو ذلك؛ فإنك إذ تعقلت معنى الإنسان لم يمنعك تعقله من وقوع الشركة فيه، فهو قدر مشترك يشترك فيه عمرو وزيد وخالد، وهكذا في باقي الأمثلة.
وإن شئت قلت في حد الكلي: هو المفرد الذي لا يمنع تعقل مدلوله من حمله حمل مواطأة على أفراد كثيرة.
والمراد بحمل المواطأة هو حمله عليها بنفسه، من غير احتياج إلى اشتقاق أو إضافة، فالإنسان مثلاً إذا تعقلت مدلوله لم يمنعك ذلك حمله حمل مواطأة على كثيرين كأن تقول: زيد إنسان، وعمرو إنسان، وخالد إنسان، وهكذا، وكذلك الحيوان لا يمنعك تعقل مدلوله من حمله حمل مواطأة على كثيرين، كقولك: الإنسان حيوان، والفرس حيوان، والجمل حيوان، وهكذا.
أما إن كان لا يمكن حمله عليها حمل مواطأة، بل حمل اشتقاق أو إضافة، فليس كليًا لها، فليس العلم مثلاً كليا بالنسبة إلى الأشخاص العلماء؛ لأنك لا تقول: مالك عِلم، والشافعي علم، وإنما يصح في ذلك الحمل بالاشتقاق، كقولك: مالك عالم، والشافعي عالم، أو الإضافة، كقولك: مالك ذو علم، والشافعي ذو علم، فالعلم كلّي بالنسبة إلى الفنون؛ لأنك تقول: النحو علم، والفقه علم، والتوحيد علم، وهكذا؛ لأنه يُحمل عليها حمل مواطأة، وليس العلم كلياً بالنسبة إلى الأشخاص المتصفين به كما بينا.
وتقريب الكلي للذهن: أنه هو كل ما وضع لأكثر من شيء واحد، كالقدر المشترك بين اثنين فصاعداً.
والجزئي (1) هو «ما يمنع تعقل مدلوله من وقوع الشركة فيه».
وهنا واسطة وطرفان: طرف هو جزئي إجماعاً يمنع تعقل مدلوله من وقوع الشركة فيه، وهو العلم بنوعيه، أعني علم الشخص وعلم الجنس؛ لأنك إذا تعقلت معنى (زيد) عَلَمًا لرجل معيّن منعك تعقله من وقوع الشركة فيه؛ لأنه وضع له خاصة ليعينه ويميزه عن غيره من الأشخاص. واعلم أن الفرق بين علم الجنس واسم الجنس يعسر فهمه على كثيرين من طلبة العلم؛ فقولك: (أسامة) علمًا لجنس الأسد لأنه علم فيجوز الابتداء به بدون احتياج إلى مسوغ، ويجوز مجيء الحال منه متأخرة، نحو (هذا أسامة مقبلاً)، ولا يجوز دخول الألف واللام عليه؛ لأنه علم، بخلاف لفظة (أسد) اسما لجنس الأسد؛ فإنها نكرة لا يجوز الابتداء بها إلا بمسوغ ولا تجيء الحال منها متأخرة إلا بمسوغ، ويجوز دخول الألف واللام عليها؛ لأنها نكرة، فيعسر الفرق معرفة.
And if you know this, then know that the singular according to the terminology mentioned here divides into two divisions, no third for them, because it is either universal or particular.
And the control of the universal in terminology is that it is that whose conceptualization of its meaning does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it, like 'human', 'animal', 'man', 'woman', 'lion', and so on; for when you conceptualize the meaning of 'human', your conceptualization of it does not prevent you from the occurrence of sharing in it, so it is a common ground in which 'Amr, Zayd, and Khalid share, and likewise in the remaining examples.
And if you wish, you may say in the definition of the universal: it is the singular whose conceptualization of its meaning does not prevent predicating it with a predicative predication (haml muwāṭa'a) on many individuals.
And by 'predicative predication' is meant predicating it in itself, without need for derivation or annexation. So 'human' for example, if you conceptualize its meaning, that does not prevent you from predicating it with a predicative predication on many, as when you say: Zayd is a human, 'Amr is a human, Khalid is a human, and so on. Likewise, 'animal' does not prevent you from conceptualizing its meaning from predicating it with a predicative predication on many, as you say: the human is an animal, the horse is an animal, the camel is an animal, and so on.
But if it cannot be predicated on them with a predicative predication, but rather with a derivative predication or an annexative predication, then it is not a universal for them. So 'knowledge' for example is not universal with respect to the persons who are knowledgeable, because you do not say: Malik is knowledge, and al-Shafi'i is knowledge. Rather, what is correct in that is the predication by derivation, as you say: Malik is knowledgeable, and al-Shafi'i is knowledgeable, or by annexation, as you say: Malik has knowledge, and al-Shafi'i has knowledge. So 'knowledge' is universal with respect to the sciences because you say: grammar is knowledge, fiqh is knowledge, tawheed is knowledge, and so on, because it is predicated on them with a predicative predication. And 'knowledge' is not universal with respect to the persons characterized by it as we have shown.
And an approximation of the universal to the mind: that it is everything that is placed for more than one thing, like the common ground between two or more.
And the particular (1) is 'that whose conceptualization of its meaning prevents the occurrence of sharing in it.'
Here there is an intermediary and two extremes: an extreme that is particular by consensus, whose conceptualization of its meaning prevents the occurrence of sharing in it, and that is the proper noun (ʿalam) in its two types, I mean the proper noun of a person and the proper noun of a genus (ʿalam al-jins), because if you conceptualize the meaning of 'Zayd' as a proper noun for a specific man, your conceptualization of it prevents you from the occurrence of sharing in it, because it was placed specifically for him to identify him and distinguish him from other persons. And know that the difference between the proper noun of a genus and the common noun of a genus (ism al-jins) is difficult to understand for many students of knowledge. For your saying: 'Usama' as a proper noun for the genus of lions, because it is a proper noun, so it is permissible to begin a sentence with it without need for a permissive (musawwigh), and it is permissible for the state (ḥāl) to come from it delayed, as in 'This is Usama approaching', and the definite article (alif and lām) is not allowed to enter upon it because it is a proper noun, contrary to the word 'asad' as a common noun for the genus of lions, for it is indefinite, so it is not permissible to begin a sentence with it except with a permissive, and the state does not come from it delayed except with a permissive, and the definite article is allowed to enter upon it because it is indefinite, so the difference becomes difficult to know.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the two primary divisions of the singular (mufrad) according to the logicians' terminology: the universal (kulli) and the particular (juz'i). The author begins by stating that the singular divides into exactly these two, with no third. He then provides a clear 'control' (ḍābiṭ) or definition of the universal: it is that whose conceptualization (taʿaqqul) of its meaning does not prevent the occurrence of sharing (shirkah) in it. Examples are given—human, animal, etc.—to illustrate that the meaning is common to many individuals. An alternative phrasing for the definition of the universal is then given: it is a singular whose conceptualization of its meaning does not prevent predicating it with a 'predicative predication' (ḥaml muwāṭaʾah) on many individuals. This is crucial because it distinguishes the universal from terms that can only be predicated derivatively or by annexation. The author explains 'predicative predication' as predicating the word itself in its own form, without derivation (e.g., 'Zayd is a human' uses 'human' directly) or annexation (e.g., 'Zayd has knowledge'). He then uses the example of 'knowledge' to show that a term can be universal in one domain (sciences) but not in another (persons) because it fails the predicative predication test for persons (we say 'Malik is knowledgeable', not 'Malik is knowledge). This sharpens the understanding of how universality is relative to the domain of predication. An approximation (taqrīb) for the universal is then given: it is everything placed for more than one thing, i.e., a common ground between two or more. Then the particular is defined: that whose conceptualization of its meaning prevents the occurrence of sharing. This is the standard definition. Finally, the author discusses an 'intermediary and two extremes'—essentially a classification of terms that are particular by consensus, namely proper nouns ('alam) of two types: proper noun of a person ('alam al-shakhṣ) and proper noun of a genus ('alam al-jins). The proper noun of a person (e.g., Zayd) prevents sharing because it is assigned to a specific individual. The proper noun of a genus (e.g., Usama for lions) is trickier; it is a proper noun for the entire genus, so it acts like a universal in terms of reference but is grammatically and logically particular in that it cannot accept the definite article and has special syntactic properties. The author explains the difference between 'alam al-jins and ism al-jins (common noun of a genus), noting that students often confuse them. The 'alam al-jins is a proper noun (definite by itself), while ism al-jins is indefinite. This distinction is key for understanding the precise logical classification of terms.

Key Terms
كلي
جزئي
حمل مواطأة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a universal (kullī) that can be predicated with ḥaml muwāṭaʾa on individuals?
A) The word 'Usama' when used for the lion genus.
B) The word 'knowledge' when predicated on knowledgeable persons.
C) The word 'human' when predicated on Zayd and 'Amr.
D) The word 'Zayd' when predicated on a specific person.

Key Takeaway
A universal is a singular term whose meaning can be shared by many and can be predicated directly (ḥaml muwāṭaʾa) without derivation or annexation; a particular prevents such sharing and direct predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the term 'ṣalāh' (prayer) from fiqh. Test whether it is universal or particular according to the definition. Can you predicate 'ṣalāh' with ḥaml muwāṭaʾa on multiple individual acts of prayer? Provide specific examples: 'This rakʿah is ṣalāh', 'That rakʿah is ṣalāh'? Explain your reasoning.

Proper Noun of a Genus and Its Logical Status
Author discusses proper noun of a genus and its logical particularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the proper noun of a genus (ʿalam al-jins) and the common noun of a genus (ism al-jins) and explain why the former is logically particular.

Core Idea
A proper noun of a genus is a particular because it prevents sharing in its meaning, even though it applies to many.

The author introduces a subtle type of particular: the proper noun of a genus (ʿalam al-jins). Unlike a common noun, which is indefinite and can accept the definite article, a proper noun of a genus is definite by itself and prevents sharing. This distinction is often confusing for students, but it is essential for accurate logical and grammatical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term as a proper noun or common noun
Check if the term is a proper noun (ʿalam) that designates a specific entity or genus. For example, 'Zayd' is a proper noun for a person; 'Usama' is a proper noun for the lion genus.
2
If proper noun, determine if it is for a person or a genus
A proper noun of a person (ʿalam al-shakhṣ) refers to one specific individual. A proper noun of a genus (ʿalam al-jins) refers to the entire genus as a single named entity.
3
Apply the definition of particular
Does the term prevent sharing in its meaning? For 'Usama', conceptualizing its meaning as the genus of lions prevents it from applying to individual lions? Actually, the author says it is particular by consensus because it prevents sharing: you cannot say 'this is Usama' for each individual lion? But in logic, 'Usama' as a proper noun for the genus functions like a proper name for the whole genus; it cannot be shared among individuals because it denotes the genus as a unity. So it is particular.
The test of ḥaml muwāṭaʾa: can you predicate 'Usama' on multiple individual lions directly? You would not say 'this lion is Usama' as a universal predication, because 'Usama' is a proper name for the genus, not a common noun. So it fails the test.
4
Distinguish from common noun of a genus
Compare with 'asad' (lion) which is a common noun (ism al-jins) and is indefinite. It can be shared and can take the definite article. 'Asad' is a universal; 'Usama' is a particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا علمت هذا فاعلم أن المفرد بالاصطلاح المذكور هنا ينقسم إلى قسمين لا ثالث لهما؛ لأنه إما أن يكون كليًا وإما أن يكون جزئيًا.
وضابط الكلي في الاصطلاح أنه هو ما لا يمنع تعقل مدلوله من وقوع الشركة فيه، كالإنسان والحيوان والرجل والمرأة والأسد ونحو ذلك؛ فإنك إذ تعقلت معنى الإنسان لم يمنعك تعقله من وقوع الشركة فيه، فهو قدر مشترك يشترك فيه عمرو وزيد وخالد، وهكذا في باقي الأمثلة.
وإن شئت قلت في حد الكلي: هو المفرد الذي لا يمنع تعقل مدلوله من حمله حمل مواطأة على أفراد كثيرة.
والمراد بحمل المواطأة هو حمله عليها بنفسه، من غير احتياج إلى اشتقاق أو إضافة، فالإنسان مثلاً إذا تعقلت مدلوله لم يمنعك ذلك حمله حمل مواطأة على كثيرين كأن تقول: زيد إنسان، وعمرو إنسان، وخالد إنسان، وهكذا، وكذلك الحيوان لا يمنعك تعقل مدلوله من حمله حمل مواطأة على كثيرين، كقولك: الإنسان حيوان، والفرس حيوان، والجمل حيوان، وهكذا.
أما إن كان لا يمكن حمله عليها حمل مواطأة، بل حمل اشتقاق أو إضافة، فليس كليًا لها، فليس العلم مثلاً كليا بالنسبة إلى الأشخاص العلماء؛ لأنك لا تقول: مالك عِلم، والشافعي علم، وإنما يصح في ذلك الحمل بالاشتقاق، كقولك: مالك عالم، والشافعي عالم، أو الإضافة، كقولك: مالك ذو علم، والشافعي ذو علم، فالعلم كلّي بالنسبة إلى الفنون؛ لأنك تقول: النحو علم، والفقه علم، والتوحيد علم، وهكذا؛ لأنه يُحمل عليها حمل مواطأة، وليس العلم كلياً بالنسبة إلى الأشخاص المتصفين به كما بينا.
وتقريب الكلي للذهن: أنه هو كل ما وضع لأكثر من شيء واحد، كالقدر المشترك بين اثنين فصاعداً.
والجزئي (1) هو «ما يمنع تعقل مدلوله من وقوع الشركة فيه».
وهنا واسطة وطرفان: طرف هو جزئي إجماعاً يمنع تعقل مدلوله من وقوع الشركة فيه، وهو العلم بنوعيه، أعني علم الشخص وعلم الجنس؛ لأنك إذا تعقلت معنى (زيد) عَلَمًا لرجل معيّن منعك تعقله من وقوع الشركة فيه؛ لأنه وضع له خاصة ليعينه ويميزه عن غيره من الأشخاص. واعلم أن الفرق بين علم الجنس واسم الجنس يعسر فهمه على كثيرين من طلبة العلم؛ فقولك: (أسامة) علمًا لجنس الأسد لأنه علم فيجوز الابتداء به بدون احتياج إلى مسوغ، ويجوز مجيء الحال منه متأخرة، نحو (هذا أسامة مقبلاً)، ولا يجوز دخول الألف واللام عليه؛ لأنه علم، بخلاف لفظة (أسد) اسما لجنس الأسد؛ فإنها نكرة لا يجوز الابتداء بها إلا بمسوغ ولا تجيء الحال منها متأخرة إلا بمسوغ، ويجوز دخول الألف واللام عليها؛ لأنها نكرة، فيعسر الفرق معرفة.
And if you know this, then know that the singular according to the terminology mentioned here divides into two divisions, no third for them, because it is either universal or particular.
And the control of the universal in terminology is that it is that whose conceptualization of its meaning does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it, like 'human', 'animal', 'man', 'woman', 'lion', and so on; for when you conceptualize the meaning of 'human', your conceptualization of it does not prevent you from the occurrence of sharing in it, so it is a common ground in which 'Amr, Zayd, and Khalid share, and likewise in the remaining examples.
And if you wish, you may say in the definition of the universal: it is the singular whose conceptualization of its meaning does not prevent predicating it with a predicative predication (haml muwāṭa'a) on many individuals.
And by 'predicative predication' is meant predicating it in itself, without need for derivation or annexation. So 'human' for example, if you conceptualize its meaning, that does not prevent you from predicating it with a predicative predication on many, as when you say: Zayd is a human, 'Amr is a human, Khalid is a human, and so on. Likewise, 'animal' does not prevent you from conceptualizing its meaning from predicating it with a predicative predication on many, as you say: the human is an animal, the horse is an animal, the camel is an animal, and so on.
But if it cannot be predicated on them with a predicative predication, but rather with a derivative predication or an annexative predication, then it is not a universal for them. So 'knowledge' for example is not universal with respect to the persons who are knowledgeable, because you do not say: Malik is knowledge, and al-Shafi'i is knowledge. Rather, what is correct in that is the predication by derivation, as you say: Malik is knowledgeable, and al-Shafi'i is knowledgeable, or by annexation, as you say: Malik has knowledge, and al-Shafi'i has knowledge. So 'knowledge' is universal with respect to the sciences because you say: grammar is knowledge, fiqh is knowledge, tawheed is knowledge, and so on, because it is predicated on them with a predicative predication. And 'knowledge' is not universal with respect to the persons characterized by it as we have shown.
And an approximation of the universal to the mind: that it is everything that is placed for more than one thing, like the common ground between two or more.
And the particular (1) is 'that whose conceptualization of its meaning prevents the occurrence of sharing in it.'
Here there is an intermediary and two extremes: an extreme that is particular by consensus, whose conceptualization of its meaning prevents the occurrence of sharing in it, and that is the proper noun (ʿalam) in its two types, I mean the proper noun of a person and the proper noun of a genus (ʿalam al-jins), because if you conceptualize the meaning of 'Zayd' as a proper noun for a specific man, your conceptualization of it prevents you from the occurrence of sharing in it, because it was placed specifically for him to identify him and distinguish him from other persons. And know that the difference between the proper noun of a genus and the common noun of a genus (ism al-jins) is difficult to understand for many students of knowledge. For your saying: 'Usama' as a proper noun for the genus of lions, because it is a proper noun, so it is permissible to begin a sentence with it without need for a permissive (musawwigh), and it is permissible for the state (ḥāl) to come from it delayed, as in 'This is Usama approaching', and the definite article (alif and lām) is not allowed to enter upon it because it is a proper noun, contrary to the word 'asad' as a common noun for the genus of lions, for it is indefinite, so it is not permissible to begin a sentence with it except with a permissive, and the state does not come from it delayed except with a permissive, and the definite article is allowed to enter upon it because it is indefinite, so the difference becomes difficult to know.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the two primary divisions of the singular (mufrad) according to the logicians' terminology: the universal (kulli) and the particular (juz'i). The author begins by stating that the singular divides into exactly these two, with no third. He then provides a clear 'control' (ḍābiṭ) or definition of the universal: it is that whose conceptualization (taʿaqqul) of its meaning does not prevent the occurrence of sharing (shirkah) in it. Examples are given—human, animal, etc.—to illustrate that the meaning is common to many individuals. An alternative phrasing for the definition of the universal is then given: it is a singular whose conceptualization of its meaning does not prevent predicating it with a 'predicative predication' (ḥaml muwāṭaʾah) on many individuals. This is crucial because it distinguishes the universal from terms that can only be predicated derivatively or by annexation. The author explains 'predicative predication' as predicating the word itself in its own form, without derivation (e.g., 'Zayd is a human' uses 'human' directly) or annexation (e.g., 'Zayd has knowledge'). He then uses the example of 'knowledge' to show that a term can be universal in one domain (sciences) but not in another (persons) because it fails the predicative predication test for persons (we say 'Malik is knowledgeable', not 'Malik is knowledge). This sharpens the understanding of how universality is relative to the domain of predication. An approximation (taqrīb) for the universal is then given: it is everything placed for more than one thing, i.e., a common ground between two or more. Then the particular is defined: that whose conceptualization of its meaning prevents the occurrence of sharing. This is the standard definition. Finally, the author discusses an 'intermediary and two extremes'—essentially a classification of terms that are particular by consensus, namely proper nouns ('alam) of two types: proper noun of a person ('alam al-shakhṣ) and proper noun of a genus ('alam al-jins). The proper noun of a person (e.g., Zayd) prevents sharing because it is assigned to a specific individual. The proper noun of a genus (e.g., Usama for lions) is trickier; it is a proper noun for the entire genus, so it acts like a universal in terms of reference but is grammatically and logically particular in that it cannot accept the definite article and has special syntactic properties. The author explains the difference between 'alam al-jins and ism al-jins (common noun of a genus), noting that students often confuse them. The 'alam al-jins is a proper noun (definite by itself), while ism al-jins is indefinite. This distinction is key for understanding the precise logical classification of terms.

Key Terms
علم الجنس
اسم الجنس
جزئي

Checkpoint
Why is the word 'Usama' (as a proper noun for the lion genus) considered particular (juzʾī)?
A) Because it refers to a specific individual lion.
B) Because its conceptualization prevents sharing in its meaning, just like a personal name.
C) Because it cannot be used as a subject in a sentence without a permissive.
D) Because it accepts the definite article.

Key Takeaway
The proper noun of a genus (ʿalam al-jins) is a particular because its meaning is fixed to the genus as a whole, preventing sharing; it is distinguished from the common noun of a genus by grammatical properties like definiteness and ability to accept the definite article.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following terms as ʿalam al-jins or ism al-jins, and justify your answer using the grammatical tests provided by the author: (a) 'أسامة' (lion genus), (b) 'أسد' (lion common noun), (c) 'زيد' (personal name).
?
Consider the logical status: why is ʿalam al-jins considered a particular (juzʾī) even though it refers to a whole genus? Use the definition of juzʾī to explain.

intermediate

Distinguishing 'Alam al-Jins from Ism al-Jins
Author defines universal and particular, then contrasts 'alam al-jins and ism al-jins with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between 'alam al-jins (proper noun for a genus) and ism al-jins (common noun) using their logical and grammatical features, and identify correct examples from Arabic texts.

Core Idea
'Alam al-jins treats a universal concept as a mental proper noun, while ism al-jins retains reference to external individuals.

This passage from the Adab al-Bahth tradition clarifies a subtle but crucial logical-grammatical distinction: the difference between a proper noun for a genus (علم الجنس) and a common noun for a genus (اسم الجنس). Many students struggle, but once you grasp whether external individuals are considered or disregarded, the distinction becomes clear. The author first reviews universals and particulars, then applies the framework to these noun types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal and particular extremes
The author defines the universal as 'what is placed for more than one thing' (e.g., 'knowledge' predicated of grammar, jurisprudence, theology) and the particular as 'that whose meaning prevents sharing' (e.g., the proper noun 'Zaid'). 'Alam al-jins sits between these: it behaves like a proper noun but refers to a universal concept.
2
Recall the grammatical differences
'Alam al-jins (e.g., 'Usama' for lion) behaves as a proper noun: it can begin a sentence without a permissive factor, can have a hal (state) after it without permission, and does not take the definite article. Ism al-jins (e.g., 'asad') is indefinite: it requires permission for those constructions and can take the definite article.
3
Grasp the logical distinction
The author states: 'in the proper noun for a genus, the common concept is observed with disregard for individuals, while in the common noun for a genus, the common concept is observed not disregarding its existence in some individuals.' This means 'alam al-jins names the mental meaning alone, while ism al-jins points to the concept while implicitly acknowledging external instances.
4
Apply to examples
Practice with 'Usama' (علم الجنس) vs. 'asad' (اسم الجنس). For 'Usama', you intend the specific mental concept of lion without considering any actual lions; for 'asad', you intend the concept but with an eye on potential external lions. This affects how the terms function in sentences and arguments.

الإضافة، كقولك : مالك ذو علم، والشافعي ذو علم، فالعلم كلّي بالنسبة إلى الفنون؛ لأنك تقول : النحو علم، والفقه علم، والتوحيد علم. وهكذا؛ لأنه يُحمل عليها حمل مواطأة، وليس العلم كلياً بالنسبة إلى الأشخاص المتصفين به كما بينا . وتقريب الكلي للذهن : أنه هو كل ماوضع لأكثر من شيء واحد، كالقدر المشترك بين اثنين فصاعداً. والجزئي هو «مايمنع تعقل مدلوله من وقوع الشركة فيه» . وهنا واسطة وطرفان : طرف هو جزئي إجماعاً يمنع تعقل مدلوله من وقوع الشركة فيه، وهو العَلَم بنوعيه، أعني علم الشخص وعَلَم الجنس؛ لأنك إذا تعقلت معنى ( زيد) عَلَمًا لرجل معيّن منعك تعقله من وقوع الشركة فيه ؛ لأنه وُضع له خاصة ليعينه ويميزه عن غيره من الأشخاص . واعلم أن الفرق بين علم الجنس واسم الجنس يعسر فهمه على كثيرين من طلبة العلم ؛ فقولك : (أسامة) علمًا لجنس الأسد لأنه علم فيجوز الابتداء به بدون احتياج إلى مسوغ، ويجوز مجيء الحال منه متأخرة، نحو (هذا) أسامة مقبلاً) ، ولا يجوز دخول الألف واللام عليه ؛ لأنه علم ، بخلاف لفظة (أسد) اسما لجنس الأسد؛ فإنها نكرة لا يجوز الابتداء بها إلا بمسوغ ولا تجيء الحال منها متأخرة إلا بمسوغ، ويجوز دخول الألف واللام عليها ؛ لأنها نكرة، فيعسر الفرق بين (أسامة) ، علمًا لجنس الأسد، و (أسد) اسماً لجنس وأوضح الفوارق التي فرقوا بها بينهما ـ بل لا يكاد يعقل غيره ـ هو أن علم الجنس روعي فيه القدر المشترك بقطع النظر عن الأفراد، واسم الجنس روعي فيه القدر المشترك لا بقطع النظر عن وجوده في بعض الأفراد . وإيضاحه أن معنى (الأسد) مثلاً شيء واحد، وهو مجموع الحيوانية والافتراسية مثلاً، فالمعنى الذهني الذي هو القدر المشترك بين أفراده شيء واحد لا تعدد فيه، وإنما التعدد في الأفراد الخارجية المشتركة فيه، فوضعوا علَم الجنس لذلك المعنى الذهني، وهو شيء واحد، فشخصوه بالعَلَم في الذهن لا في الخارج، كتشخيص الشخص بعلمه في الخارج، فعَلم الجنس يشخّص مسماه في الذهن لا في الخارج، وعلم الشخص يشخص مسماه في الخارج . وأما لفظة (أسد) فإنهم أرادوا بها المعنى الذهني المشترك الأفراد، بدون قطع النظر عن وجود بعض أفراده الخارجية فيه، التي هي محل التعدد. • وعلى قول من قال : إن اسم الجنس والنكرة شيء واحد فإن اسم الجنس يراعى فيه وجود بعض الأفراد الخارجية في المعنى الذهني، الذي هو القدر المشترك، وهو الفرد الشائع في جنسه . فقطعُ النظر مطلقا عن الأفراد الخارجية التي هي محل التعدد، وقصد تشخيص المعنى الذهني الذي هو القدر المشترك بين الأفراد
Attribution, as when you say: 'Malik is possessor of knowledge', and 'al-Shafi'i is possessor of knowledge' — so knowledge is universal with respect to the sciences, because you say: 'Grammar is knowledge', 'Jurisprudence is knowledge', 'Theology is knowledge', and so on, because it is predicated of them with a univocal predication. And knowledge is not universal with respect to the persons described by it, as we have explained. And the definition of the universal for the mind: it is everything placed for more than one thing, like the common concept between two or more. And the particular is 'that whose meaning prevents the occurrence of sharing in it'. And here there is a middle and two extremes: an extreme that is particular by consensus, whose meaning prevents the occurrence of sharing in it, and it is the proper noun in its two types, namely the proper noun for a person and the proper noun for a genus, because when you conceive the meaning of 'Zaid' as a proper noun for a specific man, its conception prevents the occurrence of sharing in it, because it was placed especially for him to specify him and distinguish him from other persons. And know that the difference between the proper noun for a genus and the common noun for a genus is difficult for many students to understand. For your saying: 'Usama' as a proper noun for the genus of lion, because it is a proper noun, it is permissible to begin with it without need for a permissive factor, and it is permissible for the state (hal) to come after it, like 'this is Usama approaching', and it is not permissible for the definite article alif-lam to enter upon it because it is a proper noun, unlike the word 'asad' as a common noun for the genus of lion, for it is indefinite, so it is not permissible to begin with it except with a permissive factor, and the state does not come after it except with a permissive factor, and it is permissible for the definite article to enter upon it because it is indefinite. So it is difficult to distinguish between 'Usama' as a proper noun for the genus of lion and 'asad' as a common noun for the genus. And the clearest distinction they have made between them — indeed, hardly any other is understood — is that in the proper noun for a genus, the common concept is observed with disregard for individuals, while in the common noun for a genus, the common concept is observed not disregarding its existence in some individuals. Its clarification: the meaning of 'asad' for example is one thing, namely the sum of animality and ferocity for example. So the mental meaning, which is the common concept among its individuals, is one thing with no multiplicity in it, while multiplicity is only in the external individuals sharing in it. So they placed the proper noun for a genus for that mental meaning, which is one thing, and they made it definite by the proper noun in the mind, not in the external, like the specification of a person by his proper noun in the external. So the proper noun for a genus specifies its named entity in the mind, not in the external, while the proper noun for a person specifies its named entity in the external. As for the word 'asad', they intended by it the mental meaning common to individuals, without disregarding the existence of some of its external individuals in it, which are the locus of multiplicity. And according to the one who says that the common noun and the indefinite are one thing, the common noun is observed with the existence of some external individuals in the mental meaning, which is the common concept, and it is the common individual in its genus. So absolute disregard for the external individuals that are the locus of multiplicity, and the intention to specify the mental meaning that is the common concept among the individuals.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and expository discussion of the universal (kulli), particular (juz'i), and the subtle distinction between two types of nouns that appear in logical discourse: 'alam al-jins (proper noun for a genus) and ism al-jins (common noun for a genus).

The author begins by illustrating the universal with examples: knowledge ('ilm) is a universal relative to the sciences (grammar, jurisprudence, theology) because it is predicated of each univocally. However, 'knowledge' is not universal when considered relative to individuals (the persons who possess it), because each person's knowledge is particular. This sets the stage for the formal definition: the universal is 'everything placed for more than one thing', i.e., it can be predicated of many individuals. The particular is 'that whose meaning prevents sharing', meaning it cannot be predicated of more than one.

The author then explains that there is a middle ground between pure universals and pure particulars. Two extremes exist: one is purely particular (like proper nouns for individuals, e.g., 'Zaid'), and the other is purely universal (like the common noun 'asad'). However, 'alam al-jins (like 'Usama' for the genus of lion) is unique: it functions like a proper noun (taking the grammatical rules of proper nouns) but refers to a universal concept (the whole genus). This confuses many students.

To clarify, the author outlines grammatical properties: 'Usama' (a proper noun for a genus) can be used without a permissive factor at the beginning of a sentence, can have a state after it without a permissive factor, and cannot take the definite article. In contrast, 'asad' (a common noun for a genus) requires a permissive factor for such constructions and can take the definite article. These grammatical differences stem from a logical distinction: 'alam al-jins regards the common concept (the mental meaning of 'lion') while disregarding external individuals entirely; it specifies the concept in the mind as if it were a particular. Ism al-jins, however, regards the common concept but not completely disregarding the existence of some external individuals—it implies the potential for multiplicity.

Finally, the author adds that according to some grammarians who consider ism al-jins and indefinites identical, ism al-jins includes the notion of a common individual existing in the genus. The point is that 'alam al-jins is uniquely suited to denote a universal concept as a single mental entity, much like a proper noun specifies a person externally.

This passage guards against the misunderstanding that 'alam al-jins is simply a common noun, highlighting its logical role as a universal given proper noun status.

Key Terms
كلي (kulli): That which is placed for more than one thing, predicated univocally of many. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
جزئي (juz'i): That whose meaning prevents the occurrence of sharing (i.e., cannot be predicated of more than one). [Note: Stable.]
علم الجنس ('alam al-jins): A proper noun for a genus; a term that grammatically behaves like a proper noun but logically denotes the common concept of a genus, disregarding external individuals. [Note: This term is primarily grammatical/logical, but the author uses it to illustrate a logical point about universals and particulars.]
اسم الجنس (ism al-jins): A common noun for a genus; a term that grammatically behaves as a common noun and logically denotes the common concept without disregarding the existence of some external individuals. [Note: Distinguished from 'alam al-jins by the inclusion of consideration of external individuals.]

Definition of the Whole and Its Judgment Condition
Author defines the whole as composed of two or more parts and explains its judgment condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a collective judgment on the whole and a distributive judgment on each part, using the Quranic example.

Core Idea
The whole is defined by its judgment: the predicate applies to the collection, not to each part.

In this passage, the author transitions from discussing universals and particulars to defining the whole (al-kull) and the part (al-juz'). You will learn the precise logical condition that distinguishes a judgment on a composite whole from a judgment on its individual parts. This distinction is critical in Islamic logic for correctly interpreting Qur'anic and fiqhi statements that refer to groups.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and its composition
The author defines 'the whole' as 'what is composed of two or more parts' (ما تركب من جزأين فصاعدا). Note that the definition consists of a genus (composite thing) and a differentia (two or more parts).
This is a real definition (hadd haqiqi).
2
Parse the judgment condition (ضابط)
The author states that the judgment (ḥukm) upon the whole applies 'to its aggregate, not to each of it individually' (على مجموعه لا على جميعه). The Arabic terms 'مجموع' (aggregate) and 'جميع' (each individually) are key.
The condition is that the predicate holds for the whole as a unit, not for each part separately.
3
Apply the condition to the Quranic example
The author cites Quran 69:17: 'And eight will bear the throne of your Lord above them on that day.' The judgment of carrying the throne applies to the eight angels collectively; no single angel carries the entire throne.
If the judgment were distributive, each angel would individually carry the throne, which contradicts the verse.
4
Contrast collective vs distributive judgment
Consider a statement like 'The army is strong.' This is a collective judgment: the army as a whole is strong, not necessarily each soldier. In contrast, 'Every soldier is strong' is distributive. The author's condition forces us to read group statements as collective unless evidence suggests otherwise.
This distinction is essential in usul al-fiqh when interpreting commands addressed to a community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت معنى الكلي والجزئي وبعض تقسيماتهما فاعلم أنا أردنا هنا أن نبيّن معنى الكل والجزء، والكلية والجزئية : اعلم أن الكل في الاصطلاح هو ما تركب من جزأين فصاعدا . وضابطه أن الحكم عليه بالمحمول (۱) إنما يقع على مجموعه لا على جميعه . وإيضاحه أن الحكم يقع عليه في حال كونه مجتمعا، فإذا فرضت تفرقة أجزائه لم يتبع الحكمُ كلَّ واحد منها بانفراده، وإنما يقع عليها مجموعة . ومثاله قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ وَيَحْمِلُ عَرْشَ رَبِّكَ فَوْقَهُمْ يَوْمَذٍ تَنيَةٌ ﴾ [الحاقة ١٧] .
And when you know the meaning of the universal and the particular and some of their divisions, know that we intend here to explain the meaning of the whole and the part, and wholeness and part-hood: Know that the whole in terminology is that which is composed of two or more parts. Its condition is that the judgment upon it by the predicate applies to its aggregate, not to each of it individually. Its clarification is that the judgment applies to it in the state of its being combined, so if you suppose its parts separated, the judgment does not follow each single part individually, but rather applies to them as a group. Its example is the saying of the Exalted: 'And eight will bear the throne of your Lord above them on that day.'
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage transitions from the discussion of universal and particular to defining the whole (kull) and part (juz'). The author states that the whole is defined technically as that which is composed of two or more parts. The key condition (ḍābiṭ) is that a judgment (ḥukm) made upon the whole using a predicate applies to the whole as a collection (majmū'), not to each part individually ('alā jamī'ihi). The clarification adds that the judgment applies when the parts are together; if the parts are separated, the judgment does not follow each part alone but only the assembled group. The example is Quran 69:17: 'And eight will bear the throne of your Lord above them on that day.' The judgment of carrying the throne applies to the eight angels as a composite group, not to each angel individually. If separated, no single angel carries the entire throne.

Distinguishing the Whole from the Universal
Author explains two differences between whole and universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two criteria (predication type and divisibility) to determine whether a given term is a whole (kull) or a universal (kulliy).

Core Idea
A universal is predicated identically of its individuals; a whole is not predicated identically of its parts.

In this passage, the author clarifies a subtle but essential distinction: the whole (al-kull) and the universal (al-kulliy) are not the same. Confusing them leads to errors in predication and division. The author provides two concrete tests—one based on how a term can be predicated, and another based on how it can be divided—that you can apply to any term. This lesson walks you through those tests so you can confidently classify terms as wholes or universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term's structure
Ask: Is this term a collection of parts that together compose it (like a chair composed of wood and nails), or a category that applies to multiple individuals (like 'human' applying to Zayd, Amr, etc.)? This initial question primes you for the tests.
2
Apply the predication test
Can you predicate the term of a part/individual by identical predication (haml muwata'a)? For a universal, 'Amr is human' works—'human' applies identically. For a whole, you cannot say 'The chair is wood' identically; you need relational predication ('possesses wood') or derived predication ('wooden'). Test a new example: 'book'—can you say 'this page is a book'?
If identical predication fails, the term is likely a whole.
3
Apply the division test
Can you divide the term using 'either/or' (e.g., 'animal is either human or horse')? For a universal, yes. For a whole, you cannot use 'either/or' on its parts—you must use 'and' (e.g., 'the chair is wood and nails'). Try dividing 'book' into its parts: pages? chapters?
If 'either/or' is inappropriate, the term is a whole.
4
Compare with the author's examples
The author's examples—'human' (universal) passes both tests, 'chair' and 'tree' (wholes) fail both. Check your own test results against these to verify your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجر بحيوان؛ لأن إثبات الحيوانية للإنسان شامل لكل فرد من أفراده، ونفي الحيوانية عن الحجر شامل لكل فرد من أفراده. وأما الفرق بين (الكل) و (الكلي) فمن جهتين:

الجهة الأولى: أنا قدمنا أن (الكلي) لا يمنع تعقلُ مدلوله من حمله على كثيرين حمل مواطأة، فيجوز حمل الكلي على كل فرد من أفراده حمل مواطأة، كقولك: (عمرو إنسان)، و(زيد إنسان)، و(خالد إنسان) إلخ، فالإنسان كلي وقد صح حمله على كل فرد من أفراده حمل مواطأة كما تقدم إيضاحه.

أما (الكل) فلا يجوز حمله على جزء من أجزائه حمل مواطأة، بل حمل إضافة أو اشتقاق. فالكرسي مثلاً: كلُّ مركب من خشب ومسامير، فلا يجوز أن تقول: الكرسي مسمار ولا أن تقول: الكرسي خشب، ولكن يصح حمله على أجزائه بالإضافة والاشتقاق، فالإضافة كأن تقول: الكرسي ذو مسامير، والاشتقاق كقولك: الكرسي مسمّر. وكالشجرة؛ فإنها كل مركب من جذوع وأغصان، فلا يقال: الشجرة جذوع ولا الشجرة أغصان وإنما يقال: الشجرة ذات جذوع، وذات أغصان مثلاً.

الجهة الثانية: أن (الكلي) يجوز تقسيمه بأداة التقسيم إلى جزئياته، كأن يقول: الحيوان إما إنسان أو فرس، وإما بغل وإما حمار إلخ. بخلاف (الكل)، فلا يجوز تقسيمه إلى أجزائه بأداة التقسيم، فلا يصح أن يقال: الكرسي إما خشب وإما مسامير ولا أن يقال: الشجرة إما جذوع وإما أغصان، وإنما يجوز حمل (الكل) على أجزائه حمل مواطأة العطف خاصة، أعني عطف بعض أجزائه على بعضها، كقولك: الكرسي مسامير وخشب، والشجرة جذوع وأغصان. وأما الجزء فهو ما تركب منه ومن غيره كُلّ، كالمسامير بالنسبة إلى الكرسي وكالخشب بالنسبة إليه وكالجذع بالنسبة إلى الشجرة، والأغصان بالنسبة إليها.

والجزئية في الاصطلاح هي القضية التي حكم بمحمولها على بعض أفراد موضوعها لا كلها سلباً أو إيجاباً، فمثالها موجبة: (بعض الحيوان إنسان)، ومثالها سالبة: (بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان)، وسيأتي إن شاء الله في مبحث القضايا زيادة إيضاح للكلية والجزئية.
The stone is an animal; because the affirmation of animality for a human includes every individual of its individuals, and the negation of animality about a stone includes every individual of its individuals. As for the difference between (whole) and (universal), it is from two aspects:

The first aspect: We have previously mentioned that the universal does not prevent the understanding of its denotation from being predicated of many by way of identical predication. So it is permissible to predicate the universal of every individual of its individuals by identical predication, such as you say: 'Amr is a human', 'Zayd is a human', 'Khalid is a human', etc. So 'human' is a universal and it is correct to predicate it of every individual of its individuals by identical predication as its clarification preceded.

As for the whole, it is not permissible to predicate it of a part of its parts by identical predication, but rather by relational or derived predication. For example, the chair: it is a whole composed of wood and nails. So it is not permissible to say: 'the chair is a nail' nor to say: 'the chair is wood', but it is correct to predicate it of its parts by addition and derivation. The addition such as you say: 'the chair possesses nails', and the derivation such as you say: 'the chair is nailed'. And similarly the tree; for it is a whole composed of trunks and branches. So it is not said: 'the tree is trunks' nor 'the tree is branches', but rather it is said: 'the tree possesses trunks' and 'possesses branches' for example.

The second aspect: That the universal can be divided by the instrument of division into its particulars, such that he says: 'animal is either human, or horse, or mule, or donkey, etc.' Unlike the whole, it is not permissible to divide it into its parts by the instrument of division. So it is not correct to say: 'the chair is either wood or nails', nor to say: 'the tree is either trunks or branches'. But it is permissible to predicate the whole of its parts by identical predication of conjunction specifically, i.e., conjoining some of its parts with others, such as you say: 'the chair is nails and wood', and 'the tree is trunks and branches'. And as for the part, it is that from which and from others a whole is composed, such as nails in relation to the chair, and wood in relation to it, and the trunk in relation to the tree, and the branches in relation to it.

And particularity in terminology is the proposition whose predicate is judged about some individuals of its subject, not all, whether negative or affirmative. So its affirmative example: 'Some animal is human', and its negative example: 'Some animal is not human'. And there will come, God willing, in the section on propositions further clarification of universality and particularity.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of three interrelated logical concepts: the universal (al-kulliy), the whole (al-kull), and the particular proposition (al-juz'iyya). It opens with an introductory remark about the universal 'animal' applying to all humans and denied of all stones, then proceeds to the main topic: the distinction between the whole and the universal in two aspects.

The first aspect concerns predication. The universal can be predicated of each of its individuals by identical predication (haml muwata'a), meaning the same term applies to each individual in the same sense. For example, 'human' is said of Amr, Zayd, and Khalid identically. In contrast, the whole cannot be predicated of its parts by identical predication; one cannot say 'the chair is a nail' or 'the chair is wood'. Instead, the whole is predicated of its parts by relational predication (haml idafa) like 'the chair possesses nails', or by derived predication (haml ishtiqaq) like 'the chair is nailed'. The author gives examples of a chair (composed of wood and nails) and a tree (composed of trunks and branches) to illustrate.

The second aspect concerns division. The universal can be divided into its particulars using the instrument of division (adawat al-taqsim), such as 'animal is either human, horse, mule, or donkey'. The whole, however, cannot be divided into its parts by such division; one cannot say 'the chair is either wood or nails'. Instead, the whole can be predicated of its parts by conjoining the parts with 'and' (haml muwata'at al-'atf), e.g., 'the chair is nails and wood'. The author then defines a part (al-juz') as that from which, along with other parts, a whole is composed.

Finally, the passage defines the particular proposition (al-juz'iyya) in logical terminology: a proposition whose predicate is affirmed or denied of some individuals of its subject, not all. Examples: 'Some animal is human' (affirmative) and 'Some animal is not human' (negative). The author promises further elaboration in the section on propositions.

Throughout, the author uses examples from common physical objects (chair, tree) and from the animal kingdom (human, horse, etc.) to ground abstract logical concepts in recognizable realities. The distinction between whole and universal is crucial for understanding predication and division in logic.

الكل
الكلي
حمل مواطأة
حمل إضافة
حمل اشتقاق

Key Takeaway
Distinguishing the whole (kull) from the universal (kulliy) requires checking whether identical predication and 'either/or' division are possible; if not, the term is a whole.

Defining the Particular Proposition
Author defines particular proposition and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is particular (juz'iyya) or universal based on the quantifier and the scope of the predicate.

Core Idea
A particular proposition judges some individuals of the subject, not all.

After distinguishing the universal from the whole, the author turns to the proposition level, defining particularity (juz'iyya) in logical propositions. This definition is essential for constructing and evaluating syllogisms, as the quantifier ('some' vs 'all') determines the force of the premise. In this lesson, we will break down the definition and learn to recognise particular propositions in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Every categorical proposition has a subject (mawdu') and a predicate (mahmul). For example, in 'Some animal is human', the subject is 'animal' and predicate is 'human'.
2
Check for the quantifier 'some'
A particular proposition explicitly or implicitly indicates that the predicate applies to part of the subject. The Arabic quantifier 'ba'd' (some) is the key. Look for words like 'some' or phrases meaning 'not all'.
The absence of a universal quantifier does not automatically make it particular; context matters.
3
Verify that the predicate is not for all individuals
The definition states: the predicate is judged about some individuals of the subject, not all. So even if the word 'some' is not present, the meaning must be partial. For instance, 'Man is rational' might appear universal, but if intended as 'some men are rational' it would be particular. Always check the intended scope.
The author's examples explicitly include 'some' : 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is not human'.
4
Distinguish from universal propositions
Compare: 'Every animal is human' (false universal) vs 'Some animal is human' (true particular). The universal asserts all, the particular asserts part. Practice by converting a universal into a particular: e.g., 'All scholars are knowledgeable' (universal) becomes 'Some scholars are knowledgeable' (particular) if you want to weaken the claim.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of three interrelated logical concepts: the universal (al-kulliy), the whole (al-kull), and the particular proposition (al-juz'iyya). It opens with an introductory remark about the universal 'animal' applying to all humans and denied of all stones, then proceeds to the main topic: the distinction between the whole and the universal in two aspects.

The first aspect concerns predication. For example, 'human' is said of Amr, Zayd, and Khalid identically. In contrast, the whole cannot be predicated of its parts by identical predication; one cannot say 'the chair is a nail' or 'the chair is wood'. Instead, the whole is predicated of its parts by relational predication (haml idafa) like 'the chair possesses nails', or by derived predication (haml ishtiqaq) like 'the chair is nailed'. The author gives examples of a chair (composed of wood and nails) and a tree (composed of trunks and branches) to illustrate.

The second aspect concerns division. The universal can be divided into its particulars using the instrument of division (adawat al-taqsim), such as 'animal is either human, horse, mule, or donkey'. The whole, however, cannot be divided into its parts by such division; one cannot say 'the chair is either wood or nails'. Instead, the whole can be predicated of its parts by conjoining the parts with 'and' (haml muwata'at al-'atf), e.g., 'the chair is nails and wood'. The author then defines a part (al-juz') as that from which, along with other parts, a whole is composed.

Finally, the passage defines the particular proposition (al-juz'iyya) in logical terminology: a proposition whose predicate is affirmed or denied of some individuals of its subject, not all. Examples: 'Some animal is human' (affirmative) and 'Some animal is not human' (negative). The author promises further elaboration in the section on propositions.

Throughout, the author uses examples from common physical objects (chair, tree) and from the animal kingdom (human, horse, etc.) to ground abstract logical concepts in recognizable realities. The distinction between whole and universal is crucial for understanding predication and division in logic.

القضية
بعض
سلباً أو إيجاباً

Identifying the Four Relations Between Intelligibles
Author defines the four relations with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four relations holds between any two given concepts using their defining conditions.

Core Idea
Between any two concepts, one of only four relations must hold.

This passage introduces the foundational framework for understanding how concepts relate in Islamic logic. You will learn to distinguish equality, disparity, absolute generality, and generality from one aspect using clear criteria and examples from the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the four relations
Read the definitions: equality (always together), disparity (never together), absolute generality (one always includes the other), generality from one aspect (each can exist without the other, but they sometimes meet).
These relations are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
2
Examine the examples given
Look at the examples: human & rational (equality), human & stone (disparity), human & animal (absolute generality), human & white (generality from one aspect).
The author uses these to illustrate the conditions.
3
Apply the conditions to a new pair
Take a pair of Islamic concepts, e.g., 'iman' and 'amal'. Check: Does every iman imply amal? Does every amal imply iman? Determine which condition fits.
Use the 'always meet', 'never meet', or 'sometimes meet' test.
4
Name the relation
Based on the test, assign the correct relation. For iman and amal, every person of iman may not always perform amal, but amal without iman is possible? Actually, debate exists; but for practice, assume absolute generality or generality from one aspect.
Be precise with the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن كل معقولين لابد أن تكون بينهما إحدى نسب أربع لا خامسة لها وهي المساواة والتباين والعموم والخصوص المطلق، والعموم والخصوص من وجه وبرهان الحصر في الأربع أن المعقولين إما ألا يجتمعا البتة، أو لا يفترقا البتة، أو يجتمعا تارة، ويفترقا أخرى . فإن كانا لا يجتمعان البتة فهما المتباينان كالإنسان والحجر ؛ فإن كل ذات ثبتت لها الإنسانية انتفت عنها الحجرية كعكسه، فالنسبة بين الإنسان والحجر التباين . وإن كانا لا يفترقان البتةَ فهما المتساويان كالإنسان والناطق فإن كل ذات ثبتت لها الإنسانية ثبتت لها الناطقية، كعكسه، فالنسبة بين الإنسان والناطق المساواة . وإن كانا يجتمعان تارة ويفترقان أخرى فلهما حالتان : الأولى : أن يكون أحدهما يفارقُ صاحبَه، والآخر لا يمكن أن يفارقه . الثانية : أن يكون كلُّ واحد منهما يفارقُ الآخر في بعض الصور، مع أن المفروض الاجتماع في بعضها .
And know that between any two intelligibles, there must be one of four relations, no fifth to them: equality, disparity, absolute generality and specificity, and generality and specificity from one aspect. The proof of the restriction to four is that the two intelligibles either never meet, or never separate, or meet sometimes and separate at other times. If they never meet, then they are disparate (mutabayinan), like human and stone; for every essence that has humanity, stoneness is negated from it, and vice versa, so the relation between human and stone is disparity. If they never separate, then they are equal (mutasawiyan), like human and rational; for every essence that has humanity, rationality is established for it, and vice versa, so the relation between human and rational is equality. If they meet sometimes and separate at other times, then they have two states: the first: that one of them separates from its companion, and the other cannot separate from it. The second: that each of them separates from the other in some forms, while meeting is assumed in some of them.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the exclusive enumeration of four relations between any two concepts (ma'qulan). The four relations are: 1) equality (musawat), when they always coexist; 2) disparity (tabayun), when they never coexist; 3) absolute generality and specificity (al-umum wa al-khusus al-mutlaq), where one is always present with the other but not vice versa; 4) generality and specificity from one aspect (al-umum wa al-khusus min wajh), where each can exist without the other in some cases, though they sometimes coincide. The proof proceeds by division of possibilities. First, if two concepts never meet, they are disparate (e.g., human and stone). Second, if they never separate, they are equal (e.g., human and rational). Third, if they sometimes meet and sometimes separate, this splits into two subcases: either one always accompanies the other but the other can separate (absolute generality, e.g., human and animal: every human is an animal, but not every animal is human), or both can separate from each other in some cases (generality from one aspect, e.g., human and white: some humans are white, some are not; some white things are human, some are not). The argument shows that no fifth relation exists beyond these four.

تباين
عموم وخصوص مطلق
عموم وخصوص من وجه

The Proof That Only Four Relations Exist
Author presents a proof for the exclusive enumeration of four relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof by division that enumerates the four relations between concepts.

Core Idea
The proof exhausts all possibilities by whether two concepts always meet, never meet, or sometimes meet.

This lesson unpacks the logical demonstration (burhan) that there are exactly four relations. The author uses a binary division: either the two concepts never meet, never separate, or sometimes meet. The third case splits further. Understanding this proof sharpens your ability to classify any pair of concepts systematically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting division
The proof begins: two concepts either never meet, never separate, or sometimes meet (and separate). These three are mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive.
This covers all logical possibilities.
2
Handle the first two cases
Case 1 (never meet) gives disparity. Case 2 (never separate) gives equality. These correspond to clear examples.
The author uses human/stone and human/rational.
3
Analyze the third case
If they sometimes meet, we have two subcases: either one always accompanies the other (absolute generality) or both can exist without the other (generality from one aspect).
This splits based on whether the 'always present' relation is one-directional or both-directional partial.
4
Verify exhaustiveness
Consider if a fifth relation could exist. For example, what about 'always meet but not always separate'? That is covered in third case. Any pairing must fall into one of these four.
The proof shows no other combination is possible.

Two Types of Contrast: Mukhalafa and Muqabala
Author defines two types of contrast and begins subdividing muqabala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tabāyun al-mukhālafa and tabāyun al-muqābala and identify examples of each.

Core Idea
Contrast of difference permits coexistence; contrast of opposition prevents it.

This lesson introduces a foundational distinction in logic: how two distinct realities can relate. The author classifies contrast (tabāyun) into two types: one where the two can coexist peacefully (mukhālafa) and another where they are mutually exclusive (muqābala). Understanding this division is essential for analyzing relationships between terms in Islamic disciplines like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions of tabāyun
Locate the two terms: tabāyun al-mukhālafa and tabāyun al-muqābala. Note that tabāyun itself is one of the four relations (identity, inclusion, partial overlap, contrast).
The author explicitly states: 'and know that contrast is divided into two divisions.'
2
Extract the defining characteristics of each
For mukhālafa: essences are distinct but no extreme incompatibility (can coexist). For muqābala: essences are distinct and there is extreme incompatibility (cannot coexist in one locus at one time).
The key phrase is 'ghāyat al-munāfāh' (extreme incompatibility).
3
Apply the definitions to the given examples
Classify each pair: coldness/whiteness (mukhālafa), sweetness/blackness (mukhālafa), speech/sitting (mukhālafa), blackness/whiteness (muqābala), motion/rest (muqābala). Explain why each belongs to its category.
The examples are from everyday experience but are standard in logic.
4
Differentiate the first two subtypes of muqābala
From the text, distinguish contradictories (naqīḍān: affirmation vs negation, e.g., 'Zayd is standing' vs 'Zayd is not standing') and contraries (ḍiddān: two positive attributes that exclude each other, e.g., black and white). Note the rules: contradictories cannot be both true or both false; contraries cannot coexist but can both be absent.
The passage also lists correlatives and privation-possession, but they are not fully explained due to the cut-off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنها تجري على حكم الأعمين من وجه فلا تكذب في جزئية، ولا تصدق في كلية . فلو قلت : بعض الحيوان (إنسان) أو (بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان)، فكل ذلك صادق، بخلاف الكلّية فإنها كاذبة هنا مطلقاً. كما لو قلت كل حيوان إنسان أو لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان)، فكله كاذب . واعلم أن التباين الذي هو إحدى النسب الأربع ينقسم إلى قسمين وهما : تباين المخالفة، وتباين المقابلة . وضابط تباين المخالفة هو أن الحقيقتين متباينتان في ذاتيهما، ولكنهما ليس بينهما غاية المنافاة، بمعنى أنه يمكن اجتماعهما في ذات واحدة . فحقيقة البرودة مثلاً تُباين حقيقة البياض ؛ لأن كل معنى ثبت له أنه هو البرودة انتفى عنه أنه هو البياض كعكسه، ولكن لا مانع من اجتماع البرودة والبياض في ذات واحدة كالثلج، فهو أبيض بارد . وكالحلاوة والسواد وكالكلام والقعود ؛ فإن حقيقة الحلاوة تباين حقيقة السواد ولكنهما ليس بينهما غاية المنافاة، بمعنى أنه يجوز اجتماعهما في محل واحد كالتمرة السوداء، فهي جامعة بين الحلاوة والسواد، فالنسبة التي بين السواد والحلاوة هي تباين المخالفة . وكذلك حقيقة الكلام فإنها تباين حقيقة القعود، ولكن لا مانع من أن يكون الإنسان الواحد قاعدًا متكلما في وقت واحد . وأما تباين المقابلة فضابطه أن تكون الحقيقتين متباينتين في ذاتيهما وأن بينهما غاية المنافاة، بمعنى أنه لا يمكن اجتماعهما في محل واحد في وقت واحد كالسواد والبياض، والحركة والسكون . وتباين المقابلة ينقسم إلى أربعة أقسام وهي : ١ - المقابلة بين النقيضين . ٢ - المقابلة بين الضدين . ٣ - المقابلة بين المتضائفين . ٤ ـ المقابلة بين العدم والملكة. أما مقابلة النقيضين فهي المقابلة بين السلب والإيجاب ـ أعني النفي والإثبات ، كقولك : زيد قائم (الآن)، (زيد ليس بقائم الآن) . فالمقابلة بين هذا النفي والإثبات مقابلة نقيضين، وهي واحد من أنواع تباين المقابلة . وضابط النقيضين أنهما لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، بل لابد من أحدهما وجود وعدم الآخر، والمقابلة بين الزوج والفرد مثلاً من مقابلة الشي ومساوي نقيضه؛ لأن الفرد مساو لـ(ليس بزوج)، كعكسه. وأما المقابلة بين الضدين فهي المقابلة بين أمرين وجوديين بينهما غاية المنافاة، لا يتوقف إدراك أحدهما على إدراك الآخر . كالسواد والبياض والحركة والسكون ونحو ذلك؛ فإن النقطة
Then they [the particulars] follow the ruling of the two universals from one aspect, so they are not false in a particular, nor true in a universal. So if you say 'Some animal is a human' or 'Some animal is not a human', all that is true, unlike the universal which is absolutely false here, as if you say 'Every animal is a human' or 'No animal is a human', all of that is false. And know that contrast (tabāyun), which is one of the four relations, is divided into two divisions: contrast of difference (tabāyun al-mukhālafa) and contrast of opposition (tabāyun al-muqābala). The rule of contrast of difference is that two realities are mutually distinct in their essences, but there is no extreme incompatibility between them, meaning that they can coexist in one entity. For example, the reality of coldness is different from the reality of whiteness; because every meaning established to be coldness excludes it being whiteness and vice versa, but there is no prevention of the coexistence of coldness and whiteness in one entity like snow, for it is white and cold. And like sweetness and blackness, and like speech and sitting: the reality of sweetness is different from the reality of blackness, but there is no extreme incompatibility between them, meaning that it is permissible for them to coexist in one locus, like the black date, for it combines sweetness and blackness. So the relation between blackness and sweetness is contrast of difference. Similarly, the reality of speech is different from the reality of sitting, but there is no prevention that one single human be sitting and speaking at the same time. As for contrast of opposition, its rule is that two realities are mutually distinct in their essences and that there is extreme incompatibility between them, meaning that they cannot coexist in one locus at one time, like blackness and whiteness, and motion and rest. And contrast of opposition is divided into four divisions: 1. Opposition between contradictories. 2. Opposition between contraries. 3. Opposition between correlatives. 4. Opposition between privation and possession. As for opposition of contradictories, it is the opposition between negation and affirmation – I mean negation and assertion – like your saying 'Zayd is standing (now)', 'Zayd is not standing now'. So the opposition between this negation and affirmation is opposition of contradictories, and it is one of the types of contrast of opposition. The rule of contradictories is that they neither coexist nor vanish together, but rather one must exist and the other be absent. And the opposition between even and odd, for example, is of the type opposition of a thing and the equivalent of its contradictory; because odd is equivalent to 'not even', and vice versa. As for opposition between contraries, it is the opposition between two existential things between which there is extreme incompatibility, without the perception of one depending on the perception of the other, like blackness and whiteness, motion and rest, and the like; for the point...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage defines two main types of logical contrast (tabāyun) and then subdivides the second type. The author first explains that the relation between two terms can be one of four: identity, inclusion, partial overlap, or contrast. Contrast itself is divided into two: contrast of difference (mukhālafa) and contrast of opposition (muqābala). In contrast of difference, the two realities are essentially distinct but can coexist in the same subject. Three examples are given: coldness and whiteness (snow is both white and cold), sweetness and blackness (a black date is both sweet and black), and speech and sitting (a person can sit and speak simultaneously). In contrast of opposition, the two realities cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time, like blackness/whiteness or motion/rest. This type is further divided into four subtypes, which are standard in Aristotelian logic: 1) contradictories (naqīḍān) – one is the negation of the other, e.g., 'Zayd is standing' vs 'Zayd is not standing'; they cannot both be true and cannot both be false. 2) contraries (ḍiddān) – two positive attributes that exclude each other, e.g., black and white; they cannot coexist but can sometimes be absent together (unlike contradictories). 3) correlatives (mutalāyifān) – terms that imply each other, e.g., father and son. 4) privation and possession (ʿadam wal-malaka) – the presence and absence of a quality in a subject capable of it, e.g., sight and blindness. The text fully defines contradictories and begins to define contraries, giving examples of black/white and motion/rest, but cuts off at 'the point...'. The commentary should stick to the text; note the examples all come from sensible qualities and actions, accessible to everyday experience.

Key Terms
التباين
تباين المخالفة
تباين المقابلة
النقيضان
الضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Coldness and whiteness
B) Blackness and whiteness
C) Sweetness and blackness
D) Speech and sitting

Key Takeaway
Contrast of difference allows coexistence; contrast of opposition forbids it, and the latter has four subtypes: contradictories, contraries, correlatives, and privation-possession.

The Seven Types of Definitions (Mu'arrafat)
Author classifies the seven types of definitions (mu'arrafat).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven types of definitions and identify which type a given definition belongs to.

Core Idea
Every definition must gather all instances and exclude others; there are seven distinct forms.

This passage introduces the technical concept of a 'definer' (mu'arraf) in Islamic logic. It establishes two essential conditions — comprehensiveness and exclusivity — and then categorises all definitions into seven types. Mastering this classification is the first step to evaluating any definition in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditions of a valid definer
The passage states that a definer (mu'arraf) must be 'jami'' (gathering all individuals of the defined term) and 'mani'' (preventing any non-instance from entering). These are expressed as 'tard' (prevention) and 'aks' (gathering).
The terms 'tard' and 'aks' have different meanings in later logic; here they refer specifically to the two conditions.
2
Memorize the seven types of definers
The author lists seven types: complete hadd (genus + differentia), incomplete hadd (genus only), complete rasm (proper accidents), incomplete rasm (accidents only), verbal definition (tafsir al-lafz), division (qisma), and example (mithal).
Complete hadd and complete rasm are the most common in Islamic sciences.
3
Apply the classification to examples
The passage gives examples: 'rational animal' for human (a complete hadd) and 'whinnying animal' for horse (a complete rasm). Identify why each fits its type.
Rational animal uses genus (animal) and differentia (rational); whinnying animal uses a proper accident (whinnying) which is coextensive with horse.

Section on the aims of concepts. Know that the aims of concepts are the 'definers' (mu'arrafat) in the form of the active participle. The rule of the definer (active participle) is 'that which gathers all individuals of the defined (passive participle), such that no individual of it falls outside, and prevents all others from entering into it.' So every gatherer and preventer is a definer. And know that gathering and preventing are what are expressed in terminology as tard and 'aks: preventing is tard, and gathering is 'aks. An example of a gatherer and preventer is the definition of man as 'rational animal', and the definition of horse as 'whinnying animal'. And know that the relation between the definer (active participle) and the defined (passive participle) [if it is equality, then it is a gatherer and preventer, like the two mentioned examples; for rational animal is equal to man, and whinnying animal is equal to horse. And know that the types of definers (active participle) are seven: complete hadd, incomplete hadd, complete rasm, incomplete rasm, verbal, division, and example. (1) In the printed edition: 'بان'.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines the technical concept of a definer (mu'arraf) and lists its types. It begins by stating that the aims of concepts are the definitions (mu'arrafat) in the active participle sense. The definer must satisfy two conditions: it must gather (jami') all individuals of the defined term and prevent (mani') any outsiders from entering. These correspond to the technical terms tard (prevention) and 'aks (gathering). Examples: 'rational animal' for human and 'whinnying animal' for horse. If the relation between definer and defined is exact equality (musawat), the definition is proper (jami' mani'). Finally, it enumerates seven types: complete hadd (by genuine genus and differentia), incomplete hadd (genus only), complete rasm (by proper accidents), incomplete rasm (by accidents only), verbal definition (tafsir al-lafz), division (qisma), and example (mithal). The footnote indicates a variant reading 'ban' in the printed text.

The Criterion of جامع مانع and Seven Types of Definitions
Author defines جامع مانع and lists seven types of definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a definition satisfies the جامع مانع condition and classify it into one of the seven types.

Core Idea
A correct definition must be both جامع and مانع – exactly equivalent to the defined term.

In this passage, the author establishes the foundational criterion for any proper definition in logic: it must be جامع مانع, meaning it includes every instance of the defined term and excludes everything else. He also lists seven types of definitions that will be detailed later. Mastering this criterion is essential for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand active and passive forms
The terms المعرّف (definer) and المعرَّف (defined) are key. The definer is the phrase doing the defining; the defined is the concept being defined. Keep these distinct.
2
Grasp the جامع مانع condition
The definer must encompass all individuals of the defined (جامع) and prevent any outsiders from entering (مانع). Memorize these two conditions.
3
Learn طرد and عكس
In this context, طرد means prevention (مانع) and عكس means inclusion (جامع). Note the technical shift from common Arabic usage.
4
Apply the equivalence test
Check if the definer is exactly equivalent (مساو) to the defined. If it is broader or narrower, it fails the جامع مانع condition.
Example: 'rational animal' is equivalent to human.
5
Memorize the seven types
The author lists: complete definition, incomplete definition, complete description, incomplete description, verbal definition, division, and example. These will be explained in subsequent lessons.

Chapter on the objectives of concepts. Know that the objectives of concepts are the definitions (المعرفات) in the active participle form. The rule of the definer (active) is: that which encompasses all individuals of the defined (passive) such that no individual of it falls outside, and prevents everything else from entering it. So every encompassing preventer is a definer. And know that encompassing and preventing are what are expressed in terminology as طرد (exclusion) and عكس (inclusion). So prevention is طرد, and encompassing is عكس. An example of the encompassing preventer is defining human as 'rational animal', and defining horse as 'whinnying animal'. And know that the relation between the definer (active) and the defined (passive) is that if it is equivalent, then it is an encompassing preventer, like the two mentioned examples; indeed 'rational animal' is equivalent to human, and 'whinnying animal' is equivalent to horse. And know that the types of definers (active) are seven: complete definition, incomplete definition, complete description, incomplete description, verbal definition, division, and example.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of a definition (المعرّف) in logic. The author first explains that the goals of concepts (مقاصد التصورات) are the definitions. He then gives a precise rule: a definition must be جامع (comprehensive) covering all instances of the defined term, and مانع (exclusive) preventing any non-instances from entering. This dual condition is expressed by the terms طرد and عكس: طرد means preventing (منع) non-members, and عكس means encompassing (جمع) all members. Examples are given: defining human as 'rational animal' (حيوان ناطق) encompasses all humans and excludes non-humans; defining horse as 'whinnying animal' (حيوان صاهل) does the same for horses. The passage then highlights that for a definition to be correct, the definer must be exactly equivalent (مساو) to the defined; if it is broader or narrower, it fails the جامع مانع condition. Finally, the author enumerates the seven types of definitions that will be explained: complete definition (حد تام), incomplete definition (حد ناقص), complete description (رسم تام), incomplete description (رسم ناقص), verbal definition (لفظي), division (قسمة), and example (مثال). This enumeration sets the stage for the detailed explanation that follows.
