The Five Types of Definitions in Islamic Logic
Author explains the first five types of definitions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between the five types of definitions (complete/incomplete hadd, complete/incomplete rasm, and lafzi) using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Definitions in Islamic logic are either essential (حد) or descriptive (رسم), each complete or incomplete based on genus proximity.

In this passage, the author systematically categorises the ways quiddities (ماهيات) are defined. This is crucial for evaluating definitions used in fiqh, kalam, and usul al-fiqh. Understanding these types sharpens your ability to judge whether a definition is precise or loose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and differentia
In the given definition, locate the broader category (genus) and the distinguishing feature (differentia or property). For example, in 'rational animal', 'animal' is the genus and 'rational' is the differentia.
2
Determine the proximity of the genus
Check if the genus is the proximate (closest), intermediate, or remote. Proximate genus gives a tighter definition.
Example: 'animal' is proximate for human; 'body' is remote.
3
Check if the differentia is essential or accidental
If the differentia is essential (جزء ذاتي), it forms a حَدّ; if it is a property (خاصة), it forms a رَسْم.
Essential: 'rational'; accidental: 'laughing'.
4
Classify the definition type
Combine the above: complete hadd = proximate genus + essential differentia; incomplete hadd = distant genus or differentia alone; complete rasm = proximate genus + property; incomplete rasm = distant genus or property alone; lafzi = synonym.
Use the classification given in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحد التام هو تعريف الماهية بالجنس القريب والفصل كتعريف الإنسان بأنه الحيوان الناطق.
والحد الناقص: هو تعريف الماهية بالفصل مع الجنس المتوسط أو البعيد، أو بالفصل وحده. كتعريف الإنسان بأنه «النامي الناطق»، أو «الجسم الناطق»، أو «الجوهر الناطق»، أو تعريفه بأنه «الناطق».
والرسم التام هو تعريفها بالخاصة مع الجنس القريب، كتعريف الإنسان بأنه الحيوان الضاحك، أو الحيوان الكاتب.
والرسم الناقص: هو التعريف بالخاصة مع الجنس المتوسط أو البعيد، أو الخاصة وحدها، كتعريف الإنسان بأنه «النامي الضاحك أو الكاتب»، أو «الجسم الضاحك أو الكاتب»، أو «الجوهر الضاحك أو الكاتب»، أو تعريفه بأنه «الضاحك» فقط، أو «الكاتب» فقط.
واللفظي: هو تعريف اللفظ بلفظ مرادف له أشهر منه، كتعريف الغضنفر بأنه الأسد، وتعريف الزخيخ بأنه النار، وكتعريف الخيتعور بأنه السراب وكل زائل مُضْمَحِل، ومنه قوله:
كل أنثى وإن بدا لك منها آية الحبّ حبُّها خيتعور أي زائل مضمحل غير دائم.
وأما القسمة: فكقولك في تعريف العلم: الاعتقاد إما جازم وإما
The complete definition (حد تام) is the definition of a quiddity by the proximate genus and differentia, like defining human as 'rational animal'. The incomplete definition (حد ناقص) is the definition of a quiddity by the differentia together with the intermediate or remote genus, or by the differentia alone; like defining human as 'growing rational', or 'body rational', or 'substance rational', or defining it as 'rational'. The complete description (رسم تام) is the definition of it by a property together with the proximate genus, like defining human as 'laughing animal', or 'writing animal'. The incomplete description (رسم ناقص) is the definition by a property together with the intermediate or remote genus, or the property alone; like defining human as 'growing laughing or writing', or 'body laughing or writing', or 'substance laughing or writing', or defining it as 'laughing' only, or 'writing' only. The verbal definition (لفظي) is the definition of a word by a synonymous word more famous than it, like defining الغضنفر (lion) as الأسد, and defining الزخيخ (fire) as النار, and defining الخيتعور (mirage) as السراب and everything that vanishes and disappears. From it is his saying: 'Every female, even if her sign of love appears to you, her love is a mirage' i.e., vanishing, disappearing, not permanent. As for division (قسمة): like your saying in defining knowledge: belief, either certain or ...
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage continues from the previous enumeration, providing detailed explanations of the first five types of definitions. The author proceeds in order: First, the complete definition (الحد التام) is when you define something by its proximate genus (الجنس القريب) and its differentia (الفصل). Example: human = rational animal; 'animal' is the genus, 'rational' is the differentia. Second, the incomplete definition (الحد الناقص) uses the differentia with a more distant genus (intermediate or remote) or just the differentia alone. For example, defining human as 'body rational' uses a remote genus (body instead of animal). Third, the complete description (الرسم التام) uses a property (خاصة) instead of the differentia, along with the proximate genus. Example: human = laughing animal; laughing is a property of humans, not the essence. Fourth, the incomplete description (الرسم الناقص) uses a property with a distant genus or the property alone. Fifth, the verbal definition (اللفظي) uses a more famous synonym to explain an obscure word, with examples from Arabic: الغضنفر (a lion) defined as الأسد, الزخيخ (fire) as النار, الخيتعور (mirage) as السراب. The author quotes a line of poetry to illustrate. Finally, he begins the sixth type, division (القسمة), with an incomplete example: defining knowledge as belief that is either certain or ... (the text cuts off). This passage systematically lays out the different ways terms can be defined, from the most precise (حد تام) to the looser descriptions.

Key Terms

الحد الناقص
الرسم التام
الرسم الناقص
اللفظي
القسمة

Key Takeaway

Definition by Division, Example, and Linguistic Roots
Author explains definition by division, example, and the linguistic meaning of hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply definition by division and definition by example in the context of Islamic definitions, and explain why the term 'hadd' is used for definition.

Core Idea
Definition in Islamic logic can be achieved by dividing a genus or by giving a paradigm example, rooted in the idea of preventing error.

This passage introduces two practical methods of definition—division (qisma) and example (mithal)—and then traces the word 'hadd' to its linguistic meaning of prevention. Understanding these methods deepens your ability to construct and evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences such as Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand definition by division
The author starts with the genus 'belief' (i'tiqad) and divides it stepwise: certain vs. non-certain, then certain into conforming vs. non-conforming, then conforming into stable without doubt vs. otherwise. The intersection of the positive branches (certain, conforming, stable) yields the definition of knowledge.
Each division excludes a specific type of non-knowledge.
2
Identify the exclusions
Note what each negative branch removes: 'non-certain' removes opinion, doubt, and illusion; 'non-conforming' removes false belief (e.g., philosophers' belief in the eternity of the world); 'not stable' removes the ijtihad of a correct mujtahid because it is subject to change.
This shows how definition by division sharpens the concept.
3
Understand definition by example
The author provides paradigm instances: for knowledge, he gives 'apprehension like the apprehension that one is half of two' (rational) and 'like the apprehension of sight' (sensory). For parts of speech, he gives 'noun like Zayd' and 'verb like daraba'.
Example definitions rely on the listener's prior knowledge of the paradigm.
4
Learn the linguistic meaning of hadd
Hadd in language means prevention. The author cites Nabigha Dhubyan's line where God tells Sulayman to 'prevent them from error'. This connects to the logical term: definitions prevent the mind from erring about the true nature of things.
This etymology reinforces the purpose of definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسمة: فكقولك في تعريف العلم : الاعتقاد إما جازم وإما غير جازم، والجازم إما مطابق أو غير مطابق، والمطابق إما ثابت لا يقبل التشكيك بحال، وإما ألا يكون كذلك. فخرج عن القسمة «اعتقاد جازم مطابق ثابت لا يقبل التشكيك بحال»، وهو العلم. وخرج بالجازم غير الجازم، كالظن والشك والوهم. وخرج بالمطابق غير المطابق وهو الاعتقاد الفاسد، كاعتقاد الفلاسفة قدم العالم. وخرج بالثابت الذي لا يقبل التشكيك بحال اجتهاد المجتهد المصيب؛ لأن الاجتهاد قابل للتغيير والتشكيك. وأما المثال: فكقولك في تعريف العلم هو «إدراك كإدراك أن الواحد نصفُ الاثنين، وأن الكل أكبر من الجزء». وإدراك البصيرة الذي هو كإدراك الباصرة»، وكقولك: «الاسم كزيد» و«الفعل كضرب». واعلم أن الحد في اللغة المنع ، ومنه قول نابغة ذبيان : ولا أرى فاعلاً في الناس يُشْبِهُهُ ولا أُحاشي من الأقوام من أحدِ إلا سليمان إذ قال الإله له قم في البرية فاحددها عن الفنَدِ
As for division: it is like your saying in defining knowledge: belief is either certain or not certain, and the certain is either conforming or not conforming, and the conforming is either stable not admitting doubt in any respect, or not like that. So from the division results 'certain conforming belief stable not admitting doubt in any respect', which is knowledge. And by 'certain' comes out the non-certain, like opinion, doubt, and illusion. And by 'conforming' comes out the non-conforming, which is false belief, like the philosophers' belief in the eternity of the world. And by 'stable not admitting doubt in any respect' comes out the ijtihad of the correct mujtahid, because ijtihad is subject to change and doubt. As for the example: it is like your saying in defining knowledge: 'an apprehension like the apprehension that one is half of two, and that the whole is greater than the part', and the apprehension of insight which is like the apprehension of sight, and like your saying: 'the noun like Zayd' and 'the verb like daraba'. And know that 'hadd' in language means prevention, and from it is the saying of Nabigha Dhubyan: 'I see no one among people resembling him, nor do I exclude anyone, except Sulayman when God said to him: Arise among the people and prevent them from error.'
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage explains two additional methods of definition: division (qisma) and example (mithal), and then gives the linguistic origin of the term 'hadd'. The author demonstrates definition by division using the concept of knowledge ('ilm). He starts with the genus 'belief' (i'tiqad) and divides it into certain (jāzim) and non-certain. The certain is divided into conforming (mutābiq) and non-conforming. The conforming is divided into stable (thābit) not admitting doubt (lā yuqbil al-tashkīk) and otherwise. The intersection of all the positive divisions yields the definition: 'a certain, conforming, stable belief that admits no doubt' — this is knowledge. Then he explains what each exclusion removes: non-certain includes opinion (zann), doubt (shakk), and illusion (wahm); non-conforming includes false belief (i'tiqād fāsid) like the philosophers' belief in the eternity of the world; non-stable includes the ijtihad of a correct mujtahid because it is subject to change. Thus, by negative exclusions, the definition is sharpened. Next, definition by example (mithāl) gives a paradigm case to convey the concept, like defining knowledge by saying 'apprehension' and giving examples: 'the apprehension that one is half of two' (for rational knowledge) and 'like the apprehension of sight' (for sensory knowledge). Similarly, one can define 'noun' by the example 'Zayd' and 'verb' by 'daraba'. Finally, the author notes the linguistic meaning of 'hadd' as 'prevention' (man'), citing a line from Nabigha Dhubyan where God tells Sulayman to 'prevent them from error' (fa-ḥdidhā 'an al-fanad). This etymology is relevant because definitions serve to prevent the mind from erring about the true nature of things.

Key Terms
القسمة
المثال
الحد (لغة)
العلم

Checkpoint

A) It emphasizes that definitions are short.
B) It suggests that definitions prevent the mind from error.
C) It indicates that definitions are derived from the Arabic word for 'boundary'.
D) It shows that definitions are arbitrary.

Key Takeaway
Definition by division and by example are complementary methods used in Islamic logic to produce precise definitions, and the term 'hadd' itself reminds us that definitions prevent error.

Hadd: Definition through Essential Parts
Author defines hadd linguistically and technically, with its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in a given hadd and determine whether the definition is complete.

Core Idea
A complete hadd must include all essential parts of the quiddity: both genus and differentia.

In logic, a definition (hadd) is not just any description; it is a precise differentiation of a thing's essence using its essential parts. This lesson unpacks the linguistic origin of hadd as 'prevention' and its technical meaning as a composition of genus and differentia. You will learn why a definition that omits one of these is deficient.

Guided Path
1
Trace the linguistic root of hadd
Find the word 'hadd' in the passage and its meaning in the verse. Note that it means 'prevention'.
2
Extract the technical definition
Locate where the author states 'tamyiz al-mahiyya bi-ajza'iha al-dhatiyya'. This is the core definition of hadd.
3
Identify the essential parts
The passage says the quiddity is composed of genus (jins) and differentia (fasl). Understand that the genus is more general, the differentia is coextensive.
4
Assess completeness
Read the condition for a complete hadd: it must include all essential parts. If only the differentia or a partial genus is given, it is deficient (naqis).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الحد في اللغة المنع، ومنه قول نابغة ذبيان:
ولا أرى فاعلاً في الناس يشبهه ولا أحاشي من الأقوام من أحدِ
إلا سليمان إذ قال الإله له
قم في البرية فاحددها عن الفنَدِ
فقوله: (فاحددها) أي امنعها.
وسمي الحد حدا لأنه يمنع أفراد المحدود من الخروج، ويمنع غيرها من الدخول.
وهو عندهم تمييز الماهية بأجزائها الذاتية؛ لأنها متركبة عندهم من جنس وفصل، والجنس عندهم جزؤها الذي هو أعم منها، والفصل عندهم هو جزؤها المساويها كما تقدم.
وإن لم يشتمل الحد على جميع الذاتيات كالتعريف بالفصل وحده أو مع الجنس المتوسط أو البعيد فهو عندهم ناقص؛ لعدم اشتماله على جميع الذاتيات.
والرسم أصله العلامة، ومنه رسوم الدار، أي علاماتها، كقوله: وهل عند رسم دارس من معول
وإنما سموه رسما لأن التعريف فيه بالخاصة، وهي من علامات الذات المختصة بها الخارجة عن ماهيتها عندهم كما تقدم إيضاحه.
And know that hadd (definition) in language means prevention; from it is the saying of Nabigha al-Dhubyani: 'I do not see anyone among people resembling him, nor do I exclude anyone, except Sulayman when God said to him: Rise among the people and define them (fahdidha) from error.' His saying '(fahdidha)' means 'prevent them.' And it was called hadd because it prevents the individuals of the defined from leaving, and prevents others from entering. And it is according to them the differentiation of the quiddity by its essential parts; because the quiddity according to them is composed of genus and differentia. The genus according to them is its part that is more general than it, and the differentia according to them is its part that is coextensive with it, as previously mentioned. If the definition does not encompass all essential properties, like definition by the differentia alone or with the intermediate or distant genus, then it is according to them incomplete because it does not encompass all essential properties. And rasm originally means sign, from it 'rusum al-dar' meaning its marks, as his saying: 'Is there at a decaying trace someone to console?' And they named it rasm because the definition is by the proprium, which is among the distinctive marks of the essence that are outside the quiddity according to them, as previously clarified.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage explains the linguistic and technical meanings of hadd and rasm. Linguistically, hadd means prevention, illustrated by a verse from Nabigha where 'fahdidha' means prevent people from error. Technically, hadd is defined as the differentiation (tamyiz) of a quiddity by its essential parts (ajza' dhatiyya) because the quiddity is composed of genus (jins) and differentia (fasl). The genus is the broader part, the differentia is the coextensive part. If the definition does not include all essential parts—for instance, if it uses only the differentia or the differentia with an intermediate or distant genus—it is deficient (naqis). Rasm, by contrast, originally means a sign or mark (like the traces of a dwelling). Technically, rasm is a definition that uses the specific property (khassa) of the thing, which is a distinctive mark of the essence but not part of the quiddity. So the difference is that hadd captures the essence, while rasm captures an accident that distinguishes.

جنس
فصل
ماهية

Key Takeaway
A complete hadd (definition) must differentiate the quiddity by including all its essential parts, namely the genus and differentia.

Rasm: Definition through Proprium
Author explains the linguistic and technical meaning of rasm as definition by proprium.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish rasm from hadd and identify when a definition uses an accidental property rather than an essential part.

Core Idea
Rasm defines something by a distinctive accidental property (khassa), not by its essential parts.

Not every definition captures the essence. Sometimes we describe something by its distinctive marks, like the traces of a dwelling. This lesson explores rasm, the technical term for such description. You will learn how rasm differs from hadd and see examples of accidental definitions.

Guided Path
1
Find the linguistic meaning of rasm
Look for the origin of rasm in the passage. It means 'sign' or 'mark', as in rusum al-dar (traces of a house).
2
Extract the technical definition of rasm
The passage states that the definition (ta'rif) in rasm is by the specific property (khassa). This is an accidental property that is unique to the thing.
3
Contrast rasm with hadd
Note that hadd uses essential parts (genus and differentia), while rasm uses non-essential accidents. This is the key distinction.
4
Recognize the role of rasm in logic
Rasm still serves to distinguish the thing from others, but it does not reveal its essence. It is a descriptive definition.

The First Condition of Definitions: Coextensiveness (Muttarid)
Author presents the first condition for all definitions: coextensiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether any given definition satisfies the condition of coextensiveness (muttarid) by checking if it includes all instances of the definiendum and excludes all non-instances.

Core Idea
A valid definition must cover exactly its definiendum, no more, no less.

Every definition, whether a complete definition (hadd) or a description (rasm), must meet several conditions. The very first condition is that the definiens (the defining phrase) must be 'muttarid' – coextensive with the definiendum (the thing being defined). This means the definiens applies to every single instance of the definiendum and to nothing else. Without this condition, the definition would be either too broad or too narrow, leading to logical errors. In this lesson, we will unpack what coextensiveness means and how to verify it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiens and definiendum
Locate the phrase that is doing the defining (المعرف باسم الفاعل) and the thing it is supposed to define (المعرف باسم المفعول). For example, in 'Human is a rational animal', 'rational animal' is the definiens and 'human' is the definiendum.
2
List all instances of the definiendum
Think of every individual or case that falls under the definiendum. For 'human', this includes every human being that exists, has existed, or will exist.
3
Check if the definiens applies to all instances
Verify that the definiens is true for every item in the list from step 2. If any instance of the definiendum does not satisfy the definiens, the definition fails the coextensiveness condition (it is too narrow).
4
Check if the definiens applies to any non-instance
Consider things that are not instances of the definiendum. If the definiens applies to any of them, the definition fails the coextensiveness condition (it is too broad).
5
Conclude whether the definition is muttarid
If the definiens covers all and only the instances of the definiendum, the definition is coextensive (muttarid). Otherwise, it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن جميع المعرفات يشترط فيها شروط :
الأول : أن يكون المعرف - باسم الفاعل ـ مطردا، أي مانعا من دخول غير المعرف - باسم المفعول.
And know that all definers have conditions: First: the definer (active participle) must be coextensive, i.e., preventing the entry of what is not the defined (passive participle).
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage states the first condition for any valid definition: the definiens (the part that does the defining, 'المعرف' as active participle) must be coextensive (mutard) with the definiendum (the thing being defined, 'المعرف' as passive participle). Coextensiveness means that the definition applies to all and only the things that are the definiendum. In other words, it prevents any non-member from entering under the definition, and it includes every member. This condition is essential for a definition to be accurate and not too broad or too narrow.

Key Terms
معرف (باسم الفاعل)
معرف (باسم المفعول)
مطرد

D) It is not muttarid because it is too narrow.

Key Takeaway
The first condition of a valid definition is that the definiens (المعرف) must be coextensive (مطرد) with the definiendum (المعرف), neither too broad nor too narrow.

Conditions of a Valid Definition: Muttarid and Mun'akis
Author defines hadd and rasm and explains the two conditions for valid definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a definition satisfies the conditions of muttariq (coextensive existence) and mun'akis (coextensive non-existence), and classify definitions as hadd or rasm.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be both muttariq (preventing non-members) and mun'akis (including all members).

In this lesson, we explore the two fundamental conditions for any definition in Islamic logic: al-ṭard and al-'aks. The author clarifies that ṭard is existence-concomitance—whenever the definition exists, the defined exists—and 'aks is non-existence-concomitance—whenever the definition is absent, the defined is absent. These ensure the definition is neither too broad nor too narrow. Using the example of 'human' defined as 'rational animal', we see how these conditions are applied and why common errors like defining 'human' as 'animal' or 'animal' as 'rational' fail.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish ḥadd and rasm
Identify the two types of definitions: ḥadd uses essential parts (genus + differentia); rasm uses non-essential properties (khāṣṣa). Note that ḥadd derives from 'prevention'.
The passage emphasizes that ḥadd prevents non-members from entering and members from exiting.
2
Understand ṭard (existence-concomitance)
The definition must be such that whenever it exists, the defined exists. This prevents non-members from being included. Example: 'rational animal' ⇒ whenever it exists, human exists, so it excludes horses.
The author explicitly corrects those who reverse ṭard and 'aks.
3
Understand 'aks (non-existence-concomitance)
The definition must be such that whenever it is absent, the defined is absent. This ensures all members are included. Example: defining animal as 'rational' fails because when 'rational' is absent, animal may still exist (e.g., horse).
This condition is about 'comprehensiveness' as the author says.
4
Apply both conditions to test a definition
Given a definition, check: (1) If the definition holds, does the defined hold? If yes, it is muttariq. (2) If the definition does not hold, does the defined not hold? If yes, it is mun'akis. Only if both are true is the definition valid.
Use the passage's examples: 'human = animal' fails 1; 'animal = rational' fails 2.
5
Summarize the two conditions
A valid definition must be fully coextensive with the defined term: it must have the same extension in existence and non-existence. This is the classic checker for definitions.
This summary is taken from the author's conclusion: ṭard and 'aks are 'concomitance in existence and non-existence'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقوله : (فاحددها) أي امنعها . وسُمّي الحد حدًا لأنه يمنع أفراد المحدود من الخروج، ويمنع غيرها من الدخول . وهو عندهم تمييز الماهية بأجزائها الذاتية» ؛ لأنها متركبة عندهم من جنس وفصل، والجنس عندهم جزؤها الذي هو أعم منها، والفصل عندهم هو جزؤها المساويها كما تقدم (1) وإن لم يشتمل الحد على جميع الذاتيات كالتعريف بالفصل وحده أو مع الجنس المتوسط أو البعيد فهو عندهم ناقص؛ لعدم اشتماله على الذاتيات . جميع والرسم أصله العلامة، ومنه رسوم الدار، أي علاماتها، كقوله : وهل عند رسم دارس من معول (۲) وإنما سموه رسماً لأن التعريف فيه بالخاصة، وهي من علامات الذات المختصة بها الخارجة عن ماهيتها عندهم كما تقدم إيضاحه (۳) واعلم أن جميع المعرفات يشترط فيها شروط : الأول : أن يكون المعرف - باسم الفاعل ـ مُطَّرِداً، أي مانعا من دخول غير المعرَّف - باسم المفعول .. الثاني : أن يكون منعكسا أي جامعاً لجميع أفراده، والتحقيق أن الطرد هو المنع ، والعكس هو الجمع ، خلافا لمن عكس ذلك . وإيضاح أن الطرد هو المنع والعكس هو الجمع هو أن تعلم أولاً أن الطرد في الاصطلاح هو الملازمة في الثبوت»، وقضيته : كلّما وجد الحدّ وُجد المحدود. والعكس في الاصطلاح الملازمة في الانتفاء»، وقضيته : كلَّما انتفى الحد انتفى المحدود. فإذا صدقت قضية كلما وُجد الحد وُجد المحدود) لزم منع غير المحدود من الدخول قطعًا، كقولك : كلما وجد الحيوان الناطق وجد الإنسان)، فهذا الحد مطّرد ، أي مانع من دخول غير الإنسان . فإن اختلت [قضية ] (۱) الملازمة في الثبوت اختل المنع، فلو قلت: (كلما وجد الحيوان وجد (الإنسان) فهذا ليس بصحيح؛ لأن وجود الإنسان لا يلزم من وجود الحيوان، فلو عرفت الإنسان بأنه حيوان كان الحد غير مانع من دخول غير الإنسان كالفرس، وإنما كان غير مطّرد - أي غير مانع ـ لاختلال الملازمة في الثبوت . جمع وكذلك الملازمة في الانتفاء إن اختلت لزم من ذلك اختلال جميع أفراد المحدود. فلو قلت مثلاً كلما انتفى الناطق انتفى (الحيوان فهذا ليس بصادق . ولذلك لو عرّفت الحيوان بأنه الناطق كان غیر جامع لجميع أفراد الحيوان؛ لأن منها ماليس ناطقا .
His saying: 'fa-ḥaddid-hā' means 'prevent them'. It is called ḥadd (definition) because it prevents the individuals of the defined from exiting, and prevents others from entering. According to them, it is 'distinguishing the essence by its essential parts' because, according to them, it is composed of genus and differentia. Genus, according to them, is its part that is more general, and differentia is its part that is equal to it, as previously explained. If the definition does not include all essential parts—like a definition by differentia alone or with an intermediate or remote genus—then, according to them, it is incomplete due to lack of inclusion of all essential parts. 'Rasm' originally means 'mark', from which is derived 'rusūm al-dār' (the marks of a house), as in the verse: 'And is there at a worn-out abode any answer?' They call it rasm because the definition is by means of a property (khāṣṣa), which is among the marks of the essence specific to it, external to its quiddity according to them, as previously clarified. Know that all definitions require conditions: First, that the definer (active participle) be mutṭarid, i.e., preventing the entry of other than the defined (passive participle). Second, that it be mun'akis, i.e., comprehensive of all its individuals. The verification is that ṭard is prevention, and 'aks is inclusiveness, contrary to those who reversed that. Clarification that ṭard is prevention and 'aks is inclusiveness requires you first to know that ṭard in terminology is 'the concomitance in existence' and its proposition is: 'Whenever the definition exists, the defined exists.' And 'aks in terminology is 'the concomitance in non-existence' and its proposition is: 'Whenever the definition is absent, the defined is absent.' So if the proposition 'Whenever the definition exists, the defined exists' is true, then it necessarily prevents the entry of other than the defined absolutely, as in your saying: 'Whenever rational animal exists, human exists.' This definition is mutṭarid, i.e., preventing the entry of other than human. If the concomitance in existence fails, prevention fails. For example, if you say: 'Whenever animal exists, human exists,' this is not correct, because the existence of human does not follow from the existence of animal. So if you define human as 'animal', the definition is not preventing the entry of other than human, such as horse. It is only not mutṭarid—i.e., not preventing—due to the failure of concomitance in existence. Similarly, if concomitance in non-existence fails, this results in failure to include all individuals of the defined. For example, if you say: 'Whenever the rational is absent, animal is absent,' this is not true. Thus, if you define animal as 'rational', it would not be comprehensive of all individuals of animal, because among them are those that are not rational.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines the central terms of definition theory: ḥadd (definition proper) and rasm (description). It begins by explaining the etymology of ḥadd, which derives from 'prevention'—the definition prevents the individuals of the defined from being mistaken for others and vice versa. Ḥadd, according to the logicians, distinguishes the essence (māhiyya) by its essential parts (jins and faṣl). Genus is the more general part, species-differentia the equal part. If a ḥadd lacks some essential parts, it is incomplete (nāqiṣ). Rasm, by contrast, uses non-essential properties (khawāṣṣ) to mark the essence. The passage then introduces two essential conditions for any valid definition: (1) al-muṭṭarid (coextensive) meaning it prevents non-members from entering; (2) al-mun'akis (converse) meaning it includes all members. The author clarifies a terminological dispute: some use 'ṭard' for inclusion and 'aks' for prevention, but the correct view is that ṭard is prevention (the condition of existence: whenever the definition exists, the defined exists) and 'aks is inclusion (the condition of non-existence: whenever the definition is absent, the defined is absent). Examples from the definition of human illustrate this: 'rational animal' is mutṭarid (prevents non-human) and mun'akis (includes all human). Conversely, defining human as 'animal' violates ṭard (horse also is animal), and defining animal as 'rational' violates 'aks (non-rational animals are excluded). Thus, the two conditions ensure that the definition is neither too wide nor too narrow.

Key Terms
الحد
الرسم
الطرد
العكس

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must satisfy two conditions: muttariq (existence-concomitance) and mun'akis (non-existence-concomitance), ensuring it is neither too broad nor too narrow.

Test whether the definition of 'salat' (prayer) as 'specific words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' satisfies ṭard and 'aks. Consider whether every instance of this description is a valid salat (ṭard) and whether every valid salat falls under this description ('aks).

Examine a definition of 'fasting' (sawm) as 'abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual activity from dawn to sunset with intention'. Check if this definition is muttariq (prevents non-fasting acts) and mun'akis (includes all valid fasts).

The Second Condition of Definition: Tard and 'Aks
Author defines tard and 'aks as necessary concomitance conditions for a sound definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tard and 'aks in a definition and verify whether a given definition satisfies both conditions using the criterion of necessary concomitance.

Core Idea
A sound definition must prevent non-instances (tard) and include all instances ('aks) through necessary concomitance.

This passage establishes the second condition for a correct definition: it must be both muttard (coextensive) and mun'akis (converse). The author clarifies the precise meanings of tard (prevention) and 'aks (comprehensiveness), linking them to necessary concomitance in existence and non-existence. Understanding this condition is crucial for constructing definitions that are neither too broad nor too narrow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiens and definiendum
In any definition, pick out the term being defined (definiendum) and the defining expression (definiens). For example, in 'man is rational animal', definiendum is 'man', definiens is 'rational animal'.
2
Test the tard condition (prevention)
Check the proposition: 'Whenever the definiens exists, the definiendum exists.' If this holds for all instances, the definition is muttard—it prevents non-definiendum from being included. For 'rational animal' and 'man', this holds, so it prevents non-men like horses.
If tard fails, the definition is too broad (includes non-instances).
3
Test the 'aks condition (comprehensiveness)
Check the proposition: 'Whenever the definiens is absent, the definiendum is absent.' If this holds, the definition is mun'akis—it includes all instances. For 'animal' as a definition of 'man', this fails because when 'animal' is absent, 'man' is absent (true) but also when 'animal' is present, 'man' may not be present (false). The 'aks condition is about absence: if the definiens is absent, the definiendum must be absent. For 'rational animal' and 'man', it holds.
If 'aks fails, the definition is too narrow (excludes some instances).
4
Apply both conditions to sample definitions
Test definitions from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'ṣalāt is specific words and actions beginning with takbīr and ending with taslīm'. Check tard: does anything that is not ṣalāt ever satisfy this? Check 'aks: does every ṣalāt satisfy this?
Practice with more examples to internalize the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن يكون منعكسا أي جامعاً لجميع أفراده، والتحقيق أن الطرد هو المنع ، والعكس هو الجمع ، خلافا لمن عكس ذلك . وإيضاح أن الطرد هو المنع والعكس هو الجمع هو أن تعلم أولاً أن الطرد في الاصطلاح هو الملازمة في الثبوت»، وقضيته : كلّما وجد الحدّ وُجد المحدود. والعكس في الاصطلاح الملازمة في الانتفاء»، وقضيته : كلَّما انتفى الحد انتفى المحدود. فإذا صدقت قضية كلما وُجد الحد وُجد المحدود) لزم منع غير المحدود من الدخول قطعًا، كقولك : كلما وجد الحيوان الناطق وجد الإنسان)، فهذا الحد مطّرد ، أي مانع من دخول غير الإنسان . فإن اختلت [قضية ] (۱) الملازمة في الثبوت اختل المنع، فلو قلت: (كلما وجد الحيوان وجد (الإنسان) فهذا ليس بصحيح؛ لأن وجود الإنسان لا يلزم من وجود الحيوان، فلو عرفت الإنسان بأنه حيوان كان الحد غير مانع من دخول غير الإنسان كالفرس، وإنما كان غير مطّرد - أي غير مانع ـ لاختلال الملازمة في الثبوت . وكذلك الملازمة في الانتفاء إن اختلت لزم من ذلك اختلال جميع أفراد المحدود. فلو قلت مثلاً كلما انتفى الناطق انتفى (الحيوان فهذا ليس بصادق . ولذلك لو عرفت الحيوان بأنه الناطق كان غیر جامع لجميع أفراد الحيوان؛ لأن منها ماليس ناطقا .
The second [condition]: that it be converse, i.e., comprehensive of all its individuals. The investigation is that tard is prevention and 'aks is comprehensiveness, contrary to whoever reversed that. And clarification that tard is prevention and 'aks is comprehensiveness is that you first know that tard in terminology is the necessary concomitance in existence, and its proposition: Whenever the definiens exists, the definiendum exists. And 'aks in terminology is the necessary concomitance in non-existence, and its proposition: Whenever the definiens is absent, the definiendum is absent. So if the proposition 'Whenever the definiens exists, the definiendum exists' is true, it necessitates the prevention of what is not the definiendum from entering absolutely, as you say: 'Whenever the rational animal exists, man exists.' So this definition is muttard, i.e., preventer of entry of what is not man. If the [proposition] of the necessary concomitance in existence is defective, the prevention is defective. So if you said: 'Whenever the animal exists, man exists' — this is not correct because the existence of man does not necessarily follow from the existence of animal. So if you defined man as animal, the definition would not prevent the entry of what is not man, like the horse. It is only non-muttard — i.e., non-preventive — due to the defect in the necessary concomitance in existence. Similarly, if the necessary concomitance in non-existence is defective, it necessitates defect in [comprehensiveness of] all individuals of the definiendum. So if you said, for example, 'Whenever the rational is absent, the animal is absent' — this is not true. And therefore if you defined animal as the rational, it would be non-comprehensive of all individuals of animal because among them is what is not rational.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines the second condition for a sound definition: that it be both muttard (coextensive) and mun'akis (converse). The author clarifies that 'tard' means 'prevention' (man') — the definition prevents what is not the definiendum from entering its scope — and ''aks' means 'comprehensiveness' (jam') — the definition includes all instances of the definiendum. Some scholars reversed these terms, but the author asserts the correct usage. He then explains the logical foundation: tard corresponds to the necessary concomitance in existence: if the definiens exists, the definiendum must exist. This yields the proposition: 'Whenever the definiens exists, the definiendum exists.' If this holds, the definition is 'preventive' — e.g., 'rational animal' for 'man': whenever rational animal exists, man exists, so no non-man (like horse) can satisfy the definition. Conversely, ''aks' corresponds to necessary concomitance in non-existence: if the definiens is absent, the definiendum is absent. This yields: 'Whenever the definiens is absent, the definiendum is absent.' If this fails, the definition does not encompass all instances. For example, defining 'animal' as 'rational' fails because when 'rational' is absent (as in a horse), 'animal' may still be present — so the definition is not comprehensive. The author illustrates both failures to reinforce the two aspects.

Key Terms
الطرد
العكس
مطّرد
منعكس

D) That the definition is clear and unambiguous.

Key Takeaway
A sound definition must be both muttard (preventing non-instances) and mun'akis (including all instances), which correspond to necessary concomitance in existence and non-existence respectively.

The Four Relationships Between Definiens and Definiendum
Author enumerates four relationships (equality, broader, narrower, overlap/disjoint) and their effect on definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four relationships between definiens and definiendum is present in a given definition and determine whether the definition is comprehensive and preventive.

Core Idea
Only equality makes a definition both comprehensive and preventive.

In this passage, the author explains the fundamental relationships between the definiens (what is used to define) and the definiendum (what is being defined). Understanding these four types is crucial for evaluating any definition's adequacy. We'll see how only one of these relationships yields a proper definition that is both comprehensive (jami') and preventive (mani').

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
In any definition, first locate the term being defined (definiendum) and the expression used to define it (definiens). For example, in 'man is an animal', 'man' is the definiendum and 'animal' is the definiens.
2
Determine the extension relationship
Compare the extension (set of individuals) of the definiens and definiendum. Is it exactly equal? Is the definiens broader? Is it narrower? Or do they overlap partially or completely differ? This is the key analytical step.
Use examples from the passage: defining man as animal (broader), animal as rational (narrower), man as white (overlap), man as stone (disjoint).
3
Check jami' (comprehensiveness)
A definition is jami' if it includes all instances of the definiendum. Ask: Does the definiens cover every case of the definiendum? If not, it fails comprehensiveness.
Example: 'Animal' includes all men, so it is jami' for man; but 'Rational' does not cover all animals, so it is not jami' for animal.
4
Check mani' (preventiveness)
A definition is mani' if it excludes everything that is not the definiendum. Ask: Does the definiens apply only to the definiendum, or does it also include other things? If it includes non-instances, it fails preventiveness.
Example: 'Animal' includes horses, so it is not mani' for man; 'Rational' excludes non-animals, so it is mani' for animal.
5
Conclude validity
Only if both conditions are satisfied (jami' and mani') is the definition valid (mutard mun'akis). This occurs only when the definiens and definiendum are exactly coextensive (musawat).
The author states that tard and 'aks are necessary conditions for every definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل إيضاح هذا أن النسبة بين الحد والمحدود إن كانت المساواة كان جامعا مانعا . وإن كان الحد أعم من المحدود كان جامعاً غير مانع، كتعريف الإنسان بأنه الحيوان . وإن كان الحد أخص من المحدود كان مانعاً غير جامع، كتعريف الحيوان بأنه الناطق ؛ فإنه مانع من دخول غير الحيوان؛ إذ لا ناطق إلا وهو حيوان، ولكنه غير جامع ؛ لأن من أفراد الحيوان ماليس بناطق . وإن كانت النسبة بينهما العموم والخصوص من وجه أو التباين كان غير جامع ولا مانع كتعريف الإنسان بأنه الأبيض، أو بأنه الحجر، كما لا يخفى . والمراد بالحد هنا مطلق التعريف فيدخل فيه جميع المعرفات، والحاصل أن كل مانع من دخول غير المحدود جامع لجميع أفراد المحدود فإنه هو المطّرد المنعكس، والاطراد والانعكاس شرطان في كل تعريف .
And the gist of clarifying this is that the relationship between the definiens and the definiendum: if it is equality, it is comprehensive and preventive. If the definiens is broader than the definiendum, it is comprehensive but not preventive, like defining man as animal. If the definiens is narrower than the definiendum, it is preventive but not comprehensive, like defining animal as the rational; for it prevents entry of non-animal, since no rational exists except it is animal, but it is not comprehensive because among the individuals of animal is what is not rational. If the relationship between them is overlapping general-and-specific (from one aspect) or complete disparity, it is neither comprehensive nor preventive, like defining man as white, or as stone, as is not hidden. And what is meant by 'hadd' here is absolute definition, so it includes all definitions. And the conclusion is that every definition that prevents entry of what is not the definiendum and is comprehensive of all individuals of the definiendum is the muttard mun'akis. And tard and 'aks are conditions in every definition.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage summarizes the four possible relationships between the definiens (hadd) and the definiendum (mahdud), showing which yield a proper definition. The author states that only when the definiens is exactly equal (musawi) to the definiendum does the definition become both comprehensive (jami') and preventive (mani'), i.e., muttard and mun'akis. If the definiens is broader (a'am) — e.g., defining 'man' simply as 'animal' — it includes all men but also includes horses and other animals, so it is not preventive. If the definiens is narrower (akhass) — e.g., defining 'animal' as 'rational' — it excludes non-animals (stones, etc.) but fails to cover irrational animals, so it is not comprehensive. If there is overlap ('umum wa khusus min wajh) like 'white' for man (some whites are men, some are not; some men are white, some are not), or complete disparity (tabayun) like 'stone' for man, the definition fails both conditions. The author then clarifies that the term 'hadd' here is used broadly for any definition (ta'rif), not only the strict logical definition (hadd haqiqi). The lesson concludes by reaffirming that tard and 'aks are necessary conditions for every definition.

Key Terms
جامع مانع (jami' mani'): A definition that includes all instances of the definiendum and excludes everything else.
المساواة (al-musawat): Equality in extension between definiens and definiendum, required for a correct definition.
الحد (al-hadd): Here used broadly to mean any definition (ta'rif), not just the technical 'hadd' (genus and differentia).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Human is a rational being.

Key Takeaway
A correct definition must be exactly coextensive with the definiendum, being both comprehensive (including all instances) and preventive (excluding all non-instances).

Third Condition: Definiens Must Be Clearer
Author states the third condition: definiens must be clearer than definiendum, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a definition violates the condition that the definiens be clearer than the definiendum, using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
A definition that is not clearer than its term fails its purpose.

This lesson presents the third condition for a valid definition in Islamic logic: the definiens must be more apparent (azhar) and clearer (awdah) to the listener than the definiendum. If the definiens is equally obscure or more obscure, it fails to clarify and defeats the purpose of definition. We will learn how to apply this condition and evaluate definitions from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum (mu'arraf) and definiens (mu'arif)
In any definition, locate the term being defined and the term(s) used to define it. For example, in 'ghadanfar is asad', the definiendum is 'ghadanfar' and the definiens is 'asad'.
The definiens is the active participle (ism fa'il) and the definiendum is the passive participle (ism maf'ul).
2
Assess the relative clarity of definiendum and definiens
Ask: Is the definiens more familiar and obvious to the intended audience than the definiendum? In the example, 'asad' is a common word for lion, while 'ghadanfar' is rare. Hence it satisfies the condition.
Clarity is relative to the hearer, not absolute. A definition that works for expert may not suit a beginner.
3
Check for equality or greater obscurity
The definiens must not be equal in clarity or more hidden. For instance, defining 'zawj' (even) as 'what is not fard' (not odd) uses a term equally familiar; thus it violates the condition.
Yes, the example in the text is incomplete but the point is clear: the definition does not enlighten.
4
Apply the condition to real Islamic examples
Test definitions from fiqh, usul, or kalam. For instance, if someone defines 'riba' as 'increase without countervalue', check whether 'increase without countervalue' is clearer than 'riba' to the listener.
If not, the definition fails the third condition and should be replaced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is not permissible for the definiens (as active participle) to be equal to the definiendum in clarity or more hidden than it. And an example of equality: defining 'zawj' (even) as 'what is not odd' (fard), and its converse, and defining...

An example of equality is defining 'zawj' (even number) as 'what is not odd' (laysa fardan) and its converse. This is problematic because both terms are of similar familiarity — the definition does not clarify. The Four Logical Relations Between Definiens and Definiendum
Author explains the four relations between definiens and definiendum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any pair of definiens and definiendum into one of the four relations: equality, generality, specificity, or partial overlap/opposition.

Core Idea
The definiens must exactly match the definiendum in extension no more, no less.

In logic, a definition is not just a phrase; it is a relationship of meaning between two terms. The author explains four possible relationships: when the definiens equals, is broader, is narrower, or partially overlaps with the definiendum. Only the first yields a correct definition.

Guided Path
1
Isolate definiens and definiendum
In each example, first identify the defining expression (haddu) and the term being defined (mahdud). For 'human is animal', the definiens is 'animal', the definiendum is 'human'.
2
Compare extensions
Determine the set of all things to which the definiens applies and the set of all things to which the definiendum applies. Are they equal? Does one contain the other? Or do they partially overlap?
Use mental examples from Islamic sciences, like definiendum 'salah' and definiens 'ritual prayer'.
3
Identify the relation
If the sets are equal, it is equality (musawa). If definiens set is larger, it is a'amm. If smaller, it is akhass. If they partially overlap or are disjoint, it is umum min wajh or tabayun.
The author gives examples: animal vs human, rational vs animal, white vs human, stone vs human.
4
Classify the definition's quality
For equality, the definition is comprehensive and exclusive (jami' mani'). For a'amm, it is comprehensive but not exclusive. For akhass, exclusive but not comprehensive. For the others, neither.
This is the key: only equality yields a proper definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل إيضاح هذا أن النسبة بين الحد والمحدود إن كانت المساواة كان جامعا مانعا . وإن كان الحد أعم من المحدود كان جامعاً غير مانع، كتعريف الإنسان بأنه الحيوان . وإن كان الحد أخص من المحدود كان مانعاً غير جامع، كتعريف الحيوان بأنه الناطق ؛ فإنه مانع من دخول غير الحيوان؛ إذ لا ناطق إلا وهو حيوان، ولكنه غير جامع ؛ لأن من أفراد الحيوان ماليس بناطق . وإن كانت النسبة بينهما العموم والخصوص من وجه أو التباين كان غير جامع ولا مانع كتعريف الإنسان بأنه الأبيض، أو بأنه الحجر، كما لا يخفى . والمراد بالحد هنا مطلق التعريف فيدخل فيه جميع المعرفات، والحاصل أن كل مانع من دخول غير المحدود جامع لجميع أفراد المحدود فإنه هو المطّرد المنعكس، والاطراد والانعكاس شرطان في كل تعريف .
The summary of clarifying this is that the relationship between the definition and the defined: if it is equality, it is comprehensive and exclusive. If the definition is more general than the defined, it is comprehensive but not exclusive, like defining 'human' as 'animal'. If the definition is more specific than the defined, it is exclusive but not comprehensive, like defining 'animal' as 'rational'; for it excludes non-animal, since there is no rational except it is an animal, but it is not comprehensive because among individuals of animal there is what is not rational. If the relationship between them is general-specific from one aspect or opposition, it is neither comprehensive nor exclusive, like defining 'human' as 'white' or as 'stone', as is not hidden. And by 'definition' here is meant absolute definition, so it includes all definers. The conclusion: every definer that excludes what is not the defined and includes all individuals of the defined is the one that is universal and convertible. Universality and convertibility are two conditions in every definition.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage discusses the essential conditions for a proper definition (hadd). The author first presents the logical relationship between the definition (hadid — the defining phrase) and the defined (mahdud — the term being defined). There are four possible relationships: equality (musawa), where the definition and the defined exactly correspond; then the definition is both comprehensive (jami') — it includes all instances of the defined — and exclusive (mani') — it excludes everything else. Second, if the definition is more general (a'amm) than the defined, it is comprehensive but not exclusive; for example, defining 'human' as 'animal' includes all humans but also includes non-human animals. Third, if the definition is more specific (akhass), it is exclusive but not comprehensive; defining 'animal' as 'rational' excludes non-animals but misses non-rational animals. Fourth, if the relationship is general-specific from one aspect (umum min wajh) or total opposition (tabayun), the definition is neither comprehensive nor exclusive; e.g., defining 'human' as 'white' or 'stone' fails to cover all humans and includes non-humans. The author then clarifies that by 'definition' here he means any defining expression. The main point is that a proper definition must have two characteristics: ittarad (comprehensiveness) and in'ikas (exclusiveness). Al-ittarad means the definition applies to every individual of the defined. Al-in'ikas means it applies only to the defined. These two together ensure the definition is both comprehensive and exclusive, which are necessary for a correct definition.

Key Terms
الاطراد
الانعكاس
الحد
المحدود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A sound definition must be equal in extension to the definiendum; otherwise it fails one or both conditions.

Classify each as equality, generality, specificity, or partial overlap/opposition.

The Two Conditions of a Proper Definition: Ittarad and In'ikas
Author introduces ittarad and in'ikas as necessary conditions for every definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions of ittarad (comprehensiveness) and in'ikas (exclusiveness) to evaluate any definition.

Core Idea
A definition must include all members of the definiendum and exclude all non-members.

After discussing the four relations, the author distills the essential requirements: any correct definition must have two properties – ittarad (comprehensiveness) and in'ikas (exclusiveness). This lesson shows how these conditions correspond to the equality relation.

Guided Path
1
Define ittarad and in'ikas
Itarad means the definiens applies to every individual of the definiendum (jami'). In'ikas means the definiens applies only to the definiendum (mani'). Together they ensure the definition is both comprehensive and exclusive.
In Usul al-Fiqh, these terms are used to test the soundness of definitions of legal terms.
2
Map to the four relations
Equality satisfies both ittarad and in'ikas. Generality satisfies ittarad but not in'ikas. Specificity satisfies in'ikas but not ittarad. Partial overlap/opposition satisfies neither.
Thus only the equality relation yields a proper definition.
3
Apply to an example
Take the definition of 'salah' as 'specified acts of worship beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'. Does it include all salah? (ittarad) Does it exclude non-salah? (in'ikas) Test it.
If it fails either, the definition is defective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل إيضاح هذا أن النسبة بين الحد والمحدود إن كانت المساواة كان جامعا مانعا . وإن كان الحد أعم من المحدود كان جامعاً غير مانع، كتعريف الإنسان بأنه الحيوان . وإن كان الحد أخص من المحدود كان مانعاً غير جامع، كتعريف الحيوان بأنه الناطق ؛ فإنه مانع من دخول غير الحيوان؛ إذ لا ناطق إلا وهو حيوان، ولكنه غير جامع ؛ لأن من أفراد الحيوان ماليس بناطق . وإن كانت النسبة بينهما العموم والخصوص من وجه أو التباين كان غير جامع ولا مانع كتعريف الإنسان بأنه الأبيض، أو بأنه الحجر، كما لا يخفى . والمراد بالحد هنا مطلق التعريف فيدخل فيه جميع المعرفات، والحاصل أن كل مانع من دخول غير المحدود جامع لجميع أفراد المحدود فإنه هو المطّرد المنعكس، والاطراد والانعكاس شرطان في كل تعريف .
The summary of clarifying this is that the relationship between the definition and the defined: if it is equality, it is comprehensive and exclusive. If the definition is more general than the defined, it is comprehensive but not exclusive, like defining 'human' as 'animal'. If the definition is more specific than the defined, it is exclusive but not comprehensive, like defining 'animal' as 'rational'; for it excludes non-animal, since there is no rational except it is an animal, but it is not comprehensive because among individuals of animal there is what is not rational. If the relationship between them is general-specific from one aspect or opposition, it is neither comprehensive nor exclusive, like defining 'human' as 'white' or as 'stone', as is not hidden. And by 'definition' here is meant absolute definition, so it includes all definers. The conclusion: every definer that excludes what is not the defined and includes all individuals of the defined is the one that is universal and convertible. Universality and convertibility are two conditions in every definition.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage discusses the essential conditions for a proper definition (hadd). The author first presents the logical relationship between the definition (hadid — the defining phrase) and the defined (mahdud — the term being defined). There are four possible relationships: equality (musawa), where the definition and the defined exactly correspond; then the definition is both comprehensive (jami') — it includes all instances of the defined — and exclusive (mani') — it excludes everything else. Second, if the definition is more general (a'amm) than the defined, it is comprehensive but not exclusive; for example, defining 'human' as 'animal' includes all humans but also includes non-human animals. Third, if the definition is more specific (akhass), it is exclusive but not comprehensive; defining 'animal' as 'rational' excludes non-animals but misses non-rational animals. Fourth, if the relationship is general-specific from one aspect (umum min wajh) or total opposition (tabayun), the definition is neither comprehensive nor exclusive; e.g., defining 'human' as 'white' or 'stone' fails to cover all humans and includes non-humans. The author then clarifies that by 'definition' here he means any defining expression. The main point is that a proper definition must have two characteristics: ittarad (comprehensiveness) and in'ikas (exclusiveness). Al-ittarad means the definition applies to every individual of the defined. Al-in'ikas means it applies only to the defined. These two together ensure the definition is both comprehensive and exclusive, which are necessary for a correct definition.

Key Terms
الاطراد
الانعكاس
الحد
المحدود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Defining 'animal' as 'rational'
C) Defining 'human' as 'white'
D) The definition that is equal to the definiendum

Key Takeaway
Ittarad and in'ikas are the two conditions that together guarantee a definition is both comprehensive and exclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Test the definition of 'wudhu' (ablution) as 'washing the face, hands, wiping the head, and washing the feet' against ittarad and in'ikas. Does it cover all obligatory acts? Does it include any non-obligatory acts?
?
Provide a corrected definition if needed.

Condition 3: Definien Must Be Clearer Than Definiendum
Author states the third condition: definien must be clearer than definiendum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a given definition satisfies the condition that the definiens is clearer than the definiendum.

Core Idea
A definition using a term more obscure than the defined term is no definition at all.

This lesson introduces the third condition for a valid definition: the term used to define (al-mu'arif) must be better known and clearer to the hearer than the term being defined (al-mu'arraf). This ensures the definition actually clarifies rather than confuses. We will explore examples of valid and invalid definitions and learn how to apply this condition in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiens and definiendum
In any definition, locate the term being defined (al-mu'arraf) and the term(s) used to define it (al-mu'arif). The definiens may be a single word or a phrase.
In Arabic, al-mu'arif (active participle) is the definer; al-mu'arraf (passive participle) is the defined.
2
Assess relative clarity
Compare how well-known each term is to the intended hearer. Consider common knowledge vs. technical jargon. The definiens must be more apparent (azhar) and clearer (awdah).
3
Determine the relationship
Decide if the definiens is clearer, equal in clarity, or more obscure. If equal or more obscure, the definition fails the condition.
Examples of equality: defining 'spouse' as 'what is not single' (both equally unclear). Examples of obscurity: defining 'fire' as 'a body like the soul' (soul is more abstract).
4
Apply to Islamic science definitions
Take a definition from fiqh or usul al-fiqh and check if the definiens is clearer. For instance, define 'gharar' (uncertainty) using clear terms like 'risk' or 'deception' rather than obscure synonyms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الثالث: أن يكون المعرف - باسم الفاعل - أظهر وأوضح عند السامع من المعرَّف - باسم المفعول ، كتعريف الغضنفر بالأسد ؛ فإن الأسد أظهر وأوضح عند السامع من الغضنفر، وهكذا. ولا يجوز أن يكون المعرّف - باسم الفاعل ـ مساويا للمعرَّف في الظهور أو أخفى منه . ومثال المساوي : تعريف الزوج بما ليس فردا، كعكسه، وتعريف الساكن بماليس بمتحرك، كعكسه. ومثال ماهو أخفى منه : تعريف النار بأنها «جسم كالنفس، فالنفس أخفى من النار عند العقل، وكتعريف الذهب بالتضار أو العسجد، وتعريف القمر بأنه الزبرقان .
The third condition: that the definer (active participle) is more apparent and clearer for the hearer than the defined (passive participle). For example, defining 'ghadanfar' by 'asad' (lion), for 'assad' is more apparent and clearer for the hearer than 'ghadanfar'. Similarly. And it is not permissible that the definer is equal to the defined in clarity or more hidden than it. An example of equality: defining 'spouse' as 'what is not single', or its converse, and defining 'stationary' as 'what is not moving', or its converse. An example of what is more hidden: defining 'fire' as 'a body like the soul', for the soul is more hidden than fire with respect to the intellect; and like defining 'gold' by 'al-tidhar' or 'al-'asjad', and defining the moon as 'al-zuburqan'.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents the third condition for a valid definition: the definiens (المعرف — the term used to define) must be better known and clearer to the hearer than the definiendum (المعرف — the term being defined). The author uses the example of defining the rare word 'ghadanfar' (meaning lion) with the common word 'asad'; since 'asad' is well-known, the definition succeeds. Conversely, if the definer is equal in obscurity, like defining 'spouse' as 'what is not single' — here both terms are equally unclear because 'single' (fard) needs definition — or defining 'stationary' as 'not moving', which gives no new clarity. If the definer is even more obscure than the defined, like defining 'fire' as 'a body like the soul' — the soul is more abstract than fire — or using rare synonyms for gold or the moon, the definition fails to clarify and instead increases confusion. Therefore, the definition must always proceed from the known to the unknown.

Key Terms
المعرف
المعرف
الظهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Defining 'gold' as 'al-'asjad' (an obscure synonym)
C) Defining 'animal' as 'sentient moving being'
D) Defining 'sun' as 'the rising star'

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must move from the known to the unknown; the definiens must always be more apparent and clearer than the definiendum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Evaluate the following definition from fiqh: 'Salah is a specific set of actions and utterances beginning with takbir and ending with taslim.' Is the definiens clearer than 'Salah' for a beginner? Explain why or why not.
?
Find a definition from usul al-fiqh (e.g., 'qiyas' or 'ijma') and check if the definiens satisfies the third condition. Revise it if necessary.

Condition 4: Metaphor in Definitions Requires a Clear Indicator
Author explains that metaphorical definiens is only allowed if accompanied by a clear context specifying the intended meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to assess the acceptability of a metaphorical definiens by verifying the presence and clarity of a contextual indicator (qarina) that specifies the intended meaning.

Core Idea
A metaphorical definition without a clear qarina is as useless as a locked door with no key.

Definitions aim for clarity. Using metaphorical language can obscure meaning. But if a metaphor is accompanied by a specific indicator (qarina) that pinpoints the intended sense, it can still work. This lesson unpacks the fourth condition for valid definitions, showing why the qarina in definitions must be stronger than the qarina for ordinary metaphor and illustrating with the example of defining 'stupid' as 'a donkey that writes'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth condition
Read the opening statement: the definiens should not be metaphorical unless there is a clear qarina determining the intended meaning. Note the exception: metaphor is allowed with a qarina.
The condition is not an absolute ban on metaphor, but a requirement for explicit context.
2
Examine the example of 'stupid as a donkey that writes'
The author gives this as a permissible metaphorical definition. 'Donkey' is a metaphor for ignorance, but 'that writes' provides the qarina, specifying the intended meaning as a foolish person who pretends to write without skill.
Without 'that writes', the metaphor 'donkey' would be too vague.
3
Understand the objection and response
An objector says every metaphor already has a qarina diverting from literal meaning. The response: that qarina only indicates the word is used figuratively, not the exact intended meaning in the definition. The qarina in definitions must be explicit and clear because the purpose of definition is clarification.
The general metaphorical qarina may be subtle; the definitional qarina must be obvious.
4
Apply the condition to a new example
Consider a definition of 'patience' as 'a bitter medicine'. The metaphor 'bitter medicine' suggests something unpleasant but beneficial. Is there a clear qarina specifying the intended attribute? Without further context, it fails the condition. To fix it, add a qarina like 'that cures anxiety'.
The qarina must be part of the definiens or immediate context, not external.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الرابع : أن لا يكون المعرّف - باسم الفاعل - مجازاً، إلا قرينة تعيّن المقصود بالتعريف، فإن وُجدَتْ قرينة تدل على ذلك جاز التعريف به كتعريف البليد بأنه حمار يكتب . فإن قيل : المجاز لابد له من قرينة صارفة عن قصد المعنى الحقيقي إلى المعنى المجازي، فإنهم يجيبون بأن قرينة المجاز لا تكفي وحدها هنا ؛ لأن قرينة المجاز إنما تدل على استعمال اللفظ في غير ما وضع له، والقرينة المشترطة هنا الدالة على تعيين المراد بالتعريف . هي ومن الفوارق بينهما أن قرينة المجاز من حيث هو قد تكون خفية، وقرينته في التعريف لابد أن تكون واضحة؛ لأن المقصود بالتعريف الإيضاح .
The fourth condition: that the definer (active participle) should not be metaphorical, unless there is an indicator that determines the intended meaning in the definition. If an indicator exists that indicates that, then definition by it is allowed, like defining 'stupid' as 'a donkey that writes'. So if it is said: Metaphorical expression inevitably has an indicator that diverts from intending the literal meaning to the metaphorical meaning; then they answer that the indicator of metaphor alone is not sufficient here, because the indicator of metaphor only indicates the use of the word in other than what it is coined for, while the indicator required here is the one indicating the determination of what is intended by the definition. And among the differences between them is that the indicator of metaphor as such may be hidden, while its indicator in a definition must be clear, because the purpose of definition is clarification.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage outlines the fourth condition: the definiens should not be used metaphorically unless there is a clear contextual indicator (qarina) that specifies the intended meaning in the definition. The author gives an example: defining a stupid person as 'a donkey that writes'—here 'donkey' is metaphorical for ignorance, but the phrase 'that writes' serves as a clear indicator that the intended meaning is the foolish person who tries to write without skill, distinguishing it from the literal animal. Then an objection is raised: doesn't every metaphor already require a qarina to divert from the literal meaning? The response is that the qarina required for metaphor in general is different from the qarina required in definitions. The general metaphorical qarina only indicates that the word is used figuratively, but it may not specify the exact intended meaning in the definition. In definitions, the qarina must be explicit and clear because the goal of definition is to clarify (idah). A hidden or ambiguous qarina fails to achieve clarity. Thus, the condition demands that the definition remain straightforward and unambiguous.

Key Terms
المجاز
القرينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the general qarina is reserved for poetry.
D) Because definitions are not real metaphors.

Key Takeaway
A metaphorical definiens is only valid if accompanied by an explicit contextual indicator that clearly specifies the intended attribute, ensuring the definition fulfills its purpose of clarification.

The Fifth Condition: No Circularity in Definition
Author explains the fifth condition: no antecedent circularity (dawr sabqi) in definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a definition contains antecedent circularity (dawr sabqi) and explain why it fails.

Core Idea
A definition that presupposes what it defines teaches nothing new.

This lesson examines the fifth condition in the art of definition: the definiens must not depend on prior knowledge of the definiendum. When it does, the definition is circular and fails to achieve its purpose of producing new knowledge. The author warns against this faulty structure, and we will learn how to detect it in theological and juristic definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Locate the term being defined (mahdud) and the words used to define it (hadd). In a valid definition, the definiens should be better known than the definiendum.
If the definiens contains a term that itself requires the definiendum for its own definition, that is a red flag.
2
Check for dependency between definiens and definiendum
Ask: does understanding the definiens require prior understanding of the definiendum? If yes, there is circularity (dawr sabqi). This means the definition is useless because it presupposes what it is supposed to explain.
The author states: the purpose (gharad) is that knowledge of the defined depends on the definition; if the reverse occurs, it is circular.
3
Test with a simple Islamic example
Take a definition like 'Wine is a drink that intoxicates' and 'Intoxication is a state caused by wine.' This is circular because each term relies on the other. The definiens of wine uses intoxication, which itself is defined by wine.
Circular definitions can be subtle, especially when different words are interdefined. The key is the epistemic dependency.
4
Confirm the violation of the condition
Once you have identified the circular dependency, you can confidently assert that the definition violates the fifth condition and is therefore invalid for imparting new knowledge.
This condition is closely related to the requirement that the definiens be more known; circularity makes both terms equally unknown.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الخامس : أن لا يكون التعريف فيه دور سبقي، ومعناه أن تكون معرفة الحد يشترط لها سبقية معرفة بعض ألفاظ المحدود؛ لأن الغرض توقف معرفة المحدود على معرفة الحد، فإن توقفت معرفة الحد على معرفة المحدود كان دورًاً سبقيًا ؛ لأن معرفة كل منهما
The fifth condition: that the definition should not involve antecedent circularity. Its meaning is that knowledge of the definition requires prior knowledge of some words of the defined, because the purpose is that knowledge of the defined depends on knowledge of the definition. Then if knowledge of the definition depends on knowledge of the defined, it will be circular, because knowledge of each of them...
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth condition: the definition must avoid dawr sabqi (antecedent circularity). Circularity occurs when understanding the definition (hadd) presupposes understanding of the definiendum (mahdud). For instance, if we define 'fire' as 'the cause of smoke' and define 'smoke' as 'the effect of fire', neither term is clarified. The author explains that the purpose (gharad) of definition is to make the defined known; consequently, knowledge of the defined should depend on the definition. But if knowledge of the definition depends on prior knowledge of the defined, a vicious circle results. The text cuts off, but the idea is clear: circular definitions fail to provide new knowledge and thus are invalid. This condition is closely related to the earlier condition that the definiens must be better known; circularity makes the definiens equally unknown.

Key Terms
الدور السبقي
معرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Defining 'book' as 'a collection of pages'

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be epistemically independent: knowledge of the definiendum must depend on the definition, not the other way around.
