Priority Circularity: The Forbidden Loop in Definitions
Author defines priority circularity and gives a concrete example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify priority circularity in a definition and explain why it invalidates the definition.

Core Idea
Priority circularity traps knowledge in an impossible loop; a valid definition must break the cycle.

The fifth condition for a sound definition is that it avoids priority circularity. In this passage, the author defines this condition and illustrates it with the example of defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'knowledge of the known as it is'. This lesson unpacks why such circularity makes a definition unacceptable.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition
The author states the fifth condition: the definition must not involve priority circularity. Note the key term 'daur sabqi'.
2
Grasp the mechanism of circularity
Knowledge of the definiendum depends on the definition, but if the definition itself requires prior knowledge of some term from the definiendum, a vicious circle arises: each depends on the other sequentially, making knowledge impossible.
The author says 'knowing each requires priority of the other'.
3
Analyze the example
The definition 'al-'ilm is ma'rifat al-ma'lum 'ala ma huwa bihi' is used. Identify why it is circular: 'al-ma'lum' is derived from 'al-'ilm', so knowing 'al-ma'lum' presupposes knowing 'al-'ilm'.
Derived term (mushtaqq) cannot be known without its root.
4
Recognize the consequence
Such a definition fails to provide new knowledge; it assumes what it ought to explain. Therefore, it is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الخامس : أن لا يكون التعريف فيه دور سبقي، ومعناه أن تكون معرفة الحد يشترط لها سبقية معرفة بعض ألفاظ المحدود؛ لأن الفرض توقف معرفة المحدود على معرفة الحد، فإن توقفت معرفة الحد على معرفة المحدود كان دورًاً سبقيًا ؛ لأن معرفة كل منهما تتوقف على سبق معرفة الآخر، فلا يمكن الإدراك، كتعريف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم على ماهو به ؛ لأن المعلوم مشتق من العلم، والمشتق لا يعرف إلا بعد معرفة المشتق منه . أما الدور المَعِيُّ فلا مُحال فيه ككون ما يسمونه (الجرم) متصفاً بما يسمونه (العرض)؛ إذ لا يعقل جرم خالٍ من جميع الأعراض، كالحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق واللون ونحو ذلك، كما لا يُعقل عندهم عرَضَ قائم بنفسه دون جرم فمعرفة كل منهما تتوقف على معرفة الآخر، إلا أنه لا يُشترط سبق أحدهما للآخر، بل يُعلمان معاً في وقت واحد، وذلك هو معنى كونه دورًا مَعِيًّا . هي والأربع الأول التي هي الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق المعروفة عند المتكلمين بالأكوان . ويدخل في الدور الممنوع إدخال الأحكام في الحدود؛ لأن الحكم على الشيء فرع تصوره.
The fifth condition: that the definition not involve priority circularity. Its meaning is that the knowledge of the definition (hadd) requires prior knowledge of some words of the definiendum; because the assumption is that knowledge of the definiendum depends on knowledge of the definition. If knowledge of the definition depends on knowledge of the definiendum, then there is priority circularity; because the knowledge of each one depends on the prior knowledge of the other, so cognition is impossible. An example is defining knowledge ('ilm) as 'knowledge of the known as it is'; because 'the known' is derived from 'knowledge', and the derived term is not known except after knowing the root term. As for simultaneous circularity (al-daur al-ma'i), it is not impossible. Like what they call 'jism' (body) being characterized by what they call 'arad' (accident); because a body devoid of all accidents is not conceivable, such as motion, rest, conjunction, separation, color, and so on; similarly, an accident subsisting by itself without a body is not conceivable according to them. So knowledge of each depends on knowledge of the other, but it is not required that one precede the other; rather, they are known together at one time. That is the meaning of it being simultaneous circularity. He says: The first four, which are motion, rest, conjunction, separation, known to the mutakallimun as 'akwan'. And entering rulings into definitions is included in the prohibited circularity; because a ruling about something is a branch of its conception.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth condition for definition: avoiding circularity (daur). The author first defines 'priority circularity' (daur sabqi): when knowledge of the definition is said to depend on prior knowledge of the definiendum, but the definiendum itself depends on the definition, creating a vicious circle. An example is defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'knowledge of the known as it is' – 'the known' (al-ma'lum) is derived from 'ilm, so one cannot know 'the known' without already knowing knowledge. This is unacceptable. The author then distinguishes 'simultaneous circularity' (daur ma'i), which is not impossible. The example is the relationship between a body (jism) and its accidents ('arad). According to the mutakallimun, a body is never free from accidents, and an accident cannot exist without a body. Yet they are known together simultaneously, not sequentially. This is permissible. The author notes that the first four accidents (motion, rest, conjunction, separation) are called 'akwan' by the theologians. Finally, the author adds that including rulings (ahkam) in definitions is prohibited because ruling on something presupposes its conception – if the definition includes a ruling, circularity arises.

Key Terms
الدور السبقي
المشتق
المشتق منه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is defining 'ilm as 'ma'rifat al-ma'lum' an example of priority circularity?
A) Because 'ma'lum' is derived from 'ilm, requiring prior knowledge of 'ilm
B) Because it is too brief
C) Because it uses a synonym
D) Because it involves an accident

Key Takeaway
Priority circularity in definition renders the definition invalid because it presupposes what it ought to explain, making knowledge acquisition impossible.

Simultaneous Circularity: When Interdependence Is Acceptable
Author distinguishes simultaneous circularity from priority circularity and gives the body-accident example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simultaneous circularity from priority circularity and identify cases where it is permissible in definitions.

Core Idea
Simultaneous circularity is natural and acceptable; knowledge can emerge together without priority.

Having explained the prohibition of priority circularity, the author now introduces simultaneous circularity as a harmless form of interdependence. Using the example of body (jism) and accident ('arad), he shows that some things are known together simultaneously. This lesson explores how to recognize permissible circularity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction
Contrast priority circularity (forbidden) with simultaneous circularity (allowed). Note the key difference: priority circularity requires sequential dependency, while simultaneous involves mutual dependency without sequence.
2
Examine the example
The author gives the example of jism (body) and 'arad (accident). A body cannot exist without accidents, and an accident cannot subsist without a body. They are known together simultaneously.
Accidents include motion, rest, conjunction, separation (akwan), and others like color.
3
Understand the theological context
The mutakallimun use 'akwan' for the first four accidents. This highlights that simultaneous circularity is accepted in kalam because it reflects real ontological interdependence.
4
Apply the principle to rulings
The author adds that including rulings (ahkam) in definitions is prohibited because a ruling about something presupposes its conception, leading to priority circularity.
This extends the condition beyond terms to the relationship between definition and ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الخامس : أن لا يكون التعريف فيه دور سبقي، ومعناه أن تكون معرفة الحد يشترط لها سبقية معرفة بعض ألفاظ المحدود؛ لأن الفرض توقف معرفة المحدود على معرفة الحد، فإن توقفت معرفة الحد على معرفة المحدود كان دورًاً سبقيًا ؛ لأن معرفة كل منهما تتوقف على سبق معرفة الآخر، فلا يمكن الإدراك، كتعريف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم على ماهو به ؛ لأن المعلوم مشتق من العلم، والمشتق لا يعرف إلا بعد معرفة المشتق منه . أما الدور المَعِيُّ فلا مُحال فيه ككون ما يسمونه (الجرم) متصفاً بما يسمونه (العرض)؛ إذ لا يعقل جرم خالٍ من جميع الأعراض، كالحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق واللون ونحو ذلك، كما لا يُعقل عندهم عرَضَ قائم بنفسه دون جرم فمعرفة كل منهما تتوقف على معرفة الآخر، إلا أنه لا يُشترط سبق أحدهما للآخر، بل يُعلمان معاً في وقت واحد، وذلك هو معنى كونه دورًا مَعِيًّا . هي والأربع الأول التي هي الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق المعروفة عند المتكلمين بالأكوان . ويدخل في الدور الممنوع إدخال الأحكام في الحدود؛ لأن الحكم على الشيء فرع تصوره.
The fifth condition: that the definition not involve priority circularity. Its meaning is that the knowledge of the definition (hadd) requires prior knowledge of some words of the definiendum; because the assumption is that knowledge of the definiendum depends on knowledge of the definition. If knowledge of the definition depends on knowledge of the definiendum, then there is priority circularity; because the knowledge of each one depends on the prior knowledge of the other, so cognition is impossible. An example is defining knowledge ('ilm) as 'knowledge of the known as it is'; because 'the known' is derived from 'knowledge', and the derived term is not known except after knowing the root term. As for simultaneous circularity (al-daur al-ma'i), it is not impossible. Like what they call 'jism' (body) being characterized by what they call 'arad' (accident); because a body devoid of all accidents is not conceivable, such as motion, rest, conjunction, separation, color, and so on; similarly, an accident subsisting by itself without a body is not conceivable according to them. So knowledge of each depends on knowledge of the other, but it is not required that one precede the other; rather, they are known together at one time. That is the meaning of it being simultaneous circularity. He says: The first four, which are motion, rest, conjunction, separation, known to the mutakallimun as 'akwan'. And entering rulings into definitions is included in the prohibited circularity; because a ruling about something is a branch of its conception.

The Sixth Condition: Avoid Ambiguity in Definitions
Author states the sixth condition: the definiendum must not be a homonym unless a clarifying context specifies the meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a definition uses a homonym (mushtarak) and determine whether a clarifying context (qarina) is present to make the definition valid.

Core Idea
A homonym in a definition requires a clarifying context; otherwise it is invalid.

This passage presents the sixth condition for valid definitions: the definiendum must not be ambiguous, i.e., a homonym, unless a contextual indicator specifies the intended meaning. Ambiguity defeats the purpose of definition. We will explore the rule, examine an invalid definition (defining the sun as 'ayn' without context), and see how adding a qualifier resolves the ambiguity. This condition is essential in all types of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the homonym (mushtarak) in the definition
Look for a word in the definiens that has multiple distinct meanings. In the example, 'ayn' can mean 'eye', 'spring', or 'self'.
2
Check for a clarifying context (qarina)
Determine if the definition includes additional words that specify which meaning is intended. Without such qualifiers, the definition is ambiguous.
3
Assess the definition's validity
If no qarina is present, the definition violates the condition and is invalid. If a qarina exists, the definition is acceptable.
4
Apply the condition to another homonym
Consider a homonym from an Islamic science, e.g., 'wajib' in fiqh (can mean obligatory or necessary). Check if a definition uses it with or without context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط السادس : أن لا يكون المعرَّف مشتركًا بين معنيين فصاعدا؛ لأن الاشتراك مانع من فهم المراد بالتعريف، مالم توجد قرينة تعين المراد، فإن وُجدت جاز التعريف به . فمثال التعريف بالمشترك دون القرينة تعريف الشمس بأنها عين . ومثال القرينة المعينة للمراد تعريف الشمس بأنها «عين تضيء جميع آفاق الدنيا» . فهذه الشروط تشترط في كل تعريف من أنواع التعاريف
The sixth condition: that the definiendum not be a homonym (mushtarak) between two or more meanings; because homonymy prevents understanding the intended meaning in the definition, unless a contextual indicator specifying the intended meaning exists. If it exists, it is permissible to define with it. An example of defining with a homonym without contextual indicator is defining the sun as 'spring (or source)'. An example of the indicator specifying the intended meaning is defining the sun as 'a spring (source) that illuminates all regions of the world'. So these conditions are required in every definition from among the types of definitions.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth condition for definition: the definiendum (the term being defined) must not be ambiguous (mushtarak, i.e., a homonym having multiple meanings) unless there is a clear contextual indicator (qarina) that specifies which meaning is intended. The author gives an example: if one defines the sun (al-shams) simply as 'ayn (which can mean 'spring' or 'source' or 'eye'), without any context, the definition is ambiguous and fails. However, if one adds 'that illuminates all regions of the world', the ambiguity is removed and the definition becomes valid. The passage concludes by stating that these six conditions apply to every type of definition.

Forbidden vs. Concurrent Circularity in Definitions
Distinguishes forbidden from concurrent circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between forbidden circularity (dawr mamnu') and concurrent circularity (dawr ma'i) and evaluate definitions for circularity using examples from Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Not all circularity is forbidden: concurrent dependency is permissible when mutual knowledge is simultaneous.

Definitions in Islamic logic must be rigorous and avoid vicious circles. This passage presents two types of circularity: one that makes knowledge impossible and one that is acceptable. Understanding this distinction is critical for constructing sound definitions in kalam, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of circularity
Find the Arabic terms for forbidden and concurrent circularity in the passage. Note that 'mamnu' means forbidden and 'ma'i means concurrent.
Look for 'الدَّوْر الممنوع' and 'الدَّوْر المَعِي'.
2
Extract the logical condition for each
For forbidden circularity, knowledge of one term requires prior knowledge of the other, which is impossible. For concurrent circularity, the two terms are known simultaneously without priority.
The key is whether priority (سبق) is required.
3
Examine the forbidden example
Analyze why defining 'knowledge' as 'knowing the known as it is' fails. Because 'the known' derives from 'knowledge', so you need to know 'knowledge' first, creating a vicious circle.
This is a classic example of dawr mamnu'.
4
Examine the concurrent example
Consider the relationship between body (jirm) and accident ('arad) in kalam. A body is always qualified by accidents, and accidents cannot exist without a body. Yet we understand both together at once.
This mutual dependency is permissible because it is simultaneous.
5
Apply to rulings in definitions
The passage warns against including rulings (ahkam) in definitions, because a ruling presupposes the conception of the thing being ruled upon, creating forbidden circularity.
For example, defining 'wine' as 'what is prohibited' would be circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتوقف على سبق معرفة الآخر، فلا يمكن الإدراك، كتعريف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم على ماهو به ؛ لأن المعلوم مشتق من العلم، والمشتق لا يعرف إلا بعد معرفة المشتق منه . أما الدور المَعِيُّ فلا مُحال فيه ككون ما يسمونه (الجرم) متصفاً بما يسمونه (العرض)؛ إذ لا يعقل جزم خالٍ من جميع الأعراض، كالحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق واللون ونحو ذلك، كما لا يُعقل عندهم عَرَضَ قائم بنفسه دون جرم فمعرفة كل منهما تتوقف على معرفة الآخر، إلا أنه لا يُشترط سبق أحدهما للآخر، بل يُعلمان معاً في وقت واحد، وذلك هو معنى كونه دورًا مَعِيًّا . والأربع الأول التي هي الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق المعروفة عند المتكلمين بالأكوان . ويدخل في الدور الممنوع إدخال الأحكام في الحدود؛ لأن الحكم على الشيء فرع تصوره.
It depends on prior knowledge of the other, so perception is impossible, like defining knowledge as knowing the known as it is; because the known is derived from knowledge, and the derived is not known except after knowing the source. As for concurrent circularity, it is not impossible, like the existence of what they call (the body) being qualified by what they call (the accident); for one cannot conceive a body devoid of all accidents, like motion and rest and conjunction and separation and color and the like, just as they do not conceive an accident subsisting by itself without a body. So knowledge of each depends on knowledge of the other, but priority of one over the other is not required; rather, they are both known together at one time. That is the meaning of its being concurrent circularity. The first four, which are motion, rest, conjunction, and separation, known among the theologians as the al-akwan. And included in the forbidden circularity is the inclusion of rulings in definitions, because ruling on a thing depends on its conception.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines two types of circular definitions in logical methodology. It begins by describing the forbidden circular definition (al-dawr al-mamnu'), where knowledge of one term depends on prior knowledge of another, itself dependent on the first, making understanding impossible. An example given is defining 'knowledge' ('ilm) as 'knowing the known as it is', since 'the known' (al-ma'lum) is derived from 'knowledge', and a derived term cannot be known without prior knowledge of its source. Then it introduces the permissible concurrent circularity (al-dawr al-ma'i), where two concepts are mutually dependent but known simultaneously without priority. This is illustrated by the relationship between substance (jirm) and accident ('arad) in Islamic theology: a body cannot be conceived without accidents like motion, rest, etc., and an accident cannot subsist independently of a body. Yet both are understood together at once. The passage then lists the four primary accidents (al-akwan) of the theologians: motion, rest, conjunction, and separation. Finally, it notes that including rulings (ahkam) in definitions constitutes forbidden circularity, because a ruling presupposes the conception of the subject being ruled upon.

الدَّوْر المَعِي
الجرم
العرض
الأكوان

B) Because it includes a ruling in the definition.
C) Because it is ambiguous.
D) Because it is a definition by property.

Key Takeaway
Forbidden circularity makes knowledge impossible by requiring prior knowledge, while concurrent circularity allows simultaneous mutual understanding.

The Sixth Condition: Avoiding Ambiguity in Definitions
Author states the sixth condition (no ambiguity) and exemplifies with 'eye' for sun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify ambiguous terms (mushtarak) in definitions and determine whether a contextual indicator (qarina) resolves the ambiguity.

Core Idea
Ambiguity in definition destroys clarity unless a contextual indicator pinpoints the intended meaning.

A definition must be clear. If the term used to define is itself ambiguous, the definition fails. The sixth condition forbids using an ambiguous definiens unless a contextual indicator (qarina) specifies the meaning. Here we learn how to spot ambiguity and when a qarina saves the definition, using the example of defining the sun as 'eye' — ambiguous without a qarina, clear with one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
In the example 'defining the sun as eye', the definiendum (term being defined) is 'al-shams' (the sun), and the definiens (defining expression) is 'ayn' (eye). Notice that the definiens is a single ambiguous word.
2
Check the definiens for ambiguity
Ask: Does the definiens have more than one distinct meaning in Arabic? 'Ayn' can mean eye, spring, source, essence, etc. This is al-mushtarak — ambiguity that blocks understanding.
The condition states the definiendum should not be ambiguous, but here the definiens is ambiguous. The principle applies to the defining expression.
3
Look for a contextual indicator (qarina)
If no qarina is present, the definition is invalid. The author gives a valid example: 'an eye that illuminates all horizons of the world'. The phrase 'illuminates all horizons' specifies that the intended meaning is 'sun' (the celestial body), not a water spring or physical eye.
The qarina must be sufficient to uniquely determine the intended meaning.
4
Apply the rule to other definitions
Test any definition from Islamic sciences: if the defining expression contains an ambiguous term, check for a qarina. For example, in fiqh, defining 'gharar' as 'uncertainty' is ambiguous unless specified by context (e.g., 'gharar in sales').
Without a qarina, the definition fails the sixth condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط السادس : أن لا يكون المعرَّف مشتركًا بين معنيين فصاعدا؛ لأن الاشتراك مانع من فهم المراد بالتعريف، مالم توجد قرينة تعين المراد، فإن وُجدت جاز التعريف به . فمثال التعريف بالمشترك دون القرينة تعريف الشمس بأنها عين . ومثال القرينة المعينة للمراد تعريف الشمس بأنها «عين تضيء جميع آفاق الدنيا . فهذه الشروط تشترط في كل تعريف من أنواع التعاريف المذكورة. فإن قيل : قد عرفتم لنا المعرّف - باسم الفاعل - بأنه الجامع المانع، أي المنعكس المطرد ، ثم ذكرتم لنا الجمع والمنع من شروط المعرفات - باسم الفاعل ، ومعلوم أن الشرط خارج عن الماهية؟ . فالجواب أن ذلك التعريف رسم؛ لأنه تعريف بالخاصة، وهي خارجة عن الماهية. والواحد بالشخص له جهتان فالخاصة من جهة تعريف الماهية بها فهي معرّف - باسم الفاعل ، وقد تكون شرطاً في التعريف إذا عُرِّف بشيء آخر غيرها كالفصل، ونحن راعينا كليهما من جهة فعرَّفنا ثم جعلناها شرطًا بالاعتبار الآخر . ومقصودنا بالخاصة هنا هو كل من الجمع والمنع، فمجموعهما خاصة للمعرّف - باسم الفاعل ، والله - تعالى - أعلم .
The sixth condition: that the definiendum not be ambiguous between two or more meanings; because ambiguity prevents understanding the intended meaning of the definition, unless there is a contextual indicator that specifies the intended meaning, and if it exists, definition by it is permissible. An example of definition by an ambiguous term without a contextual indicator is defining the sun as 'eye'. And an example of a contextual indicator specifying the intended meaning is defining the sun as 'an eye that illuminates all horizons of the world'. So these conditions apply to every definition from the types of definitions mentioned. Then if it is said: You have defined for us the definer – as an active participle – as the comprehensive and exclusive, i.e., the reflective and universal, and then you mentioned comprehensiveness and exclusiveness as conditions of the definers – as active participles – and it is known that the condition is external to the essence? So the answer is that that definition is a rasm, because it is a definition by property, and the property is external to the essence. And the one thing in person has two aspects: the property, from the aspect of defining the essence by it, is a definer – as an active participle, and it may be a condition in the definition if something other than it is defined, like the differentia. And we considered both aspects, so we defined (by it) then we made it a condition by the other consideration. And our intent by 'property' here is both comprehensiveness and exclusiveness, so their aggregate is a property of the definer – as an active participle. And Allah – Most High – knows best.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth condition of definition: the definiendum must not be ambiguous (mushtarak) between two or more meanings. Ambiguity obstructs understanding of the intended meaning of the definition unless a contextual indicator (qarina) specifies which meaning is meant. The author provides two examples: defining 'the sun' simply as 'eye' is ambiguous because 'eye' could mean the organ of sight or a well (in Arabic). Defining it as 'an eye that illuminates all horizons of the world' uses a contextual indicator to clarify the intended meaning. After listing this condition, the author states that all these conditions apply to every type of definition mentioned. Then an objection is raised: the author had earlier defined 'the definer' (al-mu'arraf, active participle meaning the definition itself) as 'the comprehensive and exclusive' (al-jami' al-mani'), but then listed comprehensiveness and exclusiveness as conditions of definitions, whereas conditions are external to the essence being defined. The response is that the earlier definition was a rasm (definition by property), not a definition by essence. A property is external to the essence. Moreover, the same thing can have two aspects: from the perspective of defining the essence, the property acts as a definer; from the perspective of a definition using something else, the same property can be a condition. The author explains that they considered both aspects: first defining the definer by that property, then treating the property as a condition when considering other definitions. Here, 'property' specifically refers to the aggregate of comprehensiveness and exclusiveness, which together form the distinctive property (khassa) of the definer.

Key Terms
المُشْتَرَك
القَرِينَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because 'sun' is an ambiguous word in Arabic.
B) Because 'eye' (ʿayn) has multiple meanings and no qarina specifies which one is meant.
C) Because the definition is too short.
D) Because the definition uses a property instead of an essence.

Key Takeaway
The sixth condition ensures definitions are unambiguous; if a defining term is mushtarak, it must be accompanied by a qarina that pinpoints the intended meaning.

When a Property Serves Both as Definition and as Condition
Author defends earlier definition against objection by distinguishing rasm and dual aspect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the same property (khassa) can act as a definition in one context and as a condition in another, using the author's resolution of the objection about 'comprehensiveness and exclusiveness'.

Core Idea
A property can both define an essence and be a condition for other definitions, depending on the aspect considered.

The author earlier defined 'the definer' (al-muʿarraf) as 'the comprehensive and exclusive' (al-jāmiʿ al-māniʿ). But then he lists comprehensiveness and exclusiveness as conditions of definitions, which seems contradictory because conditions are external to the essence. The objection is resolved by clarifying that this definition is a rasm (definition by property) and that the same property can be viewed from two aspects: as a definer when it identifies the essence, and as a condition when we are defining something else.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the apparent contradiction
The author defined 'the definer' as 'the comprehensive and exclusive' (al-jāmiʿ al-māniʿ). But later he calls comprehensiveness and exclusiveness 'conditions' of definitions. Since conditions are external to essence, how can they also be the definition of the definer?
2
Understand the author's classification: rasm vs. hadd
The author admits the definition is a rasm, not a hadd. A rasm defines by a property (khassa), which is external to the essence. So it is acceptable for a property to be used in a definition even though it is not part of the essence.
A hadd would use genus and differentia; a rasm uses unique properties.
3
Grasp the dual aspect of a property
The same thing (here, the property of being comprehensive and exclusive) can be considered from two angles. When it defines the essence of 'definer', it functions as a definer (muʿarraf). When we are defining something else (e.g., a definition) and we require this property to hold, it becomes a condition (shart).
The author says: 'al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ lahu jihaān' – the one thing in person has two aspects.
4
Apply the distinction to other examples
In usul al-fiqh, 'the ability to distinguish between good and evil' (al-tamyīz) is a property of the mukallaf (legally responsible person). It can be part of the definition of 'mukallaf' and also a condition for the validity of acts of worship.
This illustrates the same principle: a property can serve both roles depending on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط السادس : أن لا يكون المعرَّف مشتركًا بين معنيين فصاعدا؛ لأن الاشتراك مانع من فهم المراد بالتعريف، مالم توجد قرينة تعين المراد، فإن وُجدت جاز التعريف به . فمثال التعريف بالمشترك دون القرينة تعريف الشمس بأنها عين . ومثال القرينة المعينة للمراد تعريف الشمس بأنها «عين تضيء جميع آفاق الدنيا . فهذه الشروط تشترط في كل تعريف من أنواع التعاريف المذكورة. فإن قيل : قد عرفتم لنا المعرّف - باسم الفاعل - بأنه الجامع المانع، أي المنعكس المطرد ، ثم ذكرتم لنا الجمع والمنع من شروط المعرفات - باسم الفاعل ، ومعلوم أن الشرط خارج عن الماهية؟ . فالجواب أن ذلك التعريف رسم؛ لأنه تعريف بالخاصة، وهي خارجة عن الماهية. والواحد بالشخص له جهتان فالخاصة من جهة تعريف الماهية بها فهي معرّف - باسم الفاعل ، وقد تكون شرطاً في التعريف إذا عُرِّف بشيء آخر غيرها كالفصل، ونحن راعينا كليهما من جهة فعرَّفنا ثم جعلناها شرطًا بالاعتبار الآخر . ومقصودنا بالخاصة هنا هو كل من الجمع والمنع، فمجموعهما خاصة للمعرّف - باسم الفاعل ، والله - تعالى - أعلم .
The sixth condition: that the definiendum not be ambiguous between two or more meanings; because ambiguity prevents understanding the intended meaning of the definition, unless there is a contextual indicator that specifies the intended meaning, and if it exists, definition by it is permissible. An example of definition by an ambiguous term without a contextual indicator is defining the sun as 'eye'. And an example of a contextual indicator specifying the intended meaning is defining the sun as 'an eye that illuminates all horizons of the world'. So these conditions apply to every definition from the types of definitions mentioned. Then if it is said: You have defined for us the definer – as an active participle – as the comprehensive and exclusive, i.e., the reflective and universal, and then you mentioned comprehensiveness and exclusiveness as conditions of the definers – as active participles – and it is known that the condition is external to the essence? So the answer is that that definition is a rasm, because it is a definition by property, and the property is external to the essence. And the one thing in person has two aspects: the property, from the aspect of defining the essence by it, is a definer – as an active participle, and it may be a condition in the definition if something other than it is defined, like the differentia. And we considered both aspects, so we defined (by it) then we made it a condition by the other consideration. And our intent by 'property' here is both comprehensiveness and exclusiveness, so their aggregate is a property of the definer – as an active participle. And Allah – Most High – knows best.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth condition of definition: the definiendum must not be ambiguous (mushtarak) between two or more meanings. Ambiguity obstructs understanding of the intended meaning of the definition unless a contextual indicator (qarina) specifies which meaning is meant. The author provides two examples: defining 'the sun' simply as 'eye' is ambiguous because 'eye' could mean the organ of sight or a well (in Arabic). Defining it as 'an eye that illuminates all horizons of the world' uses a contextual indicator to clarify the intended meaning. After listing this condition, the author states that all these conditions apply to every type of definition mentioned. Then an objection is raised: the author had earlier defined 'the definer' (al-mu'arraf, active participle meaning the definition itself) as 'the comprehensive and exclusive' (al-jami' al-mani'), but then listed comprehensiveness and exclusiveness as conditions of definitions, whereas conditions are external to the essence being defined. The response is that the earlier definition was a rasm (definition by property), not a definition by essence. A property is external to the essence. Moreover, the same thing can have two aspects: from the perspective of defining the essence, the property acts as a definer; from the perspective of a definition using something else, the same property can be a condition. The author explains that they considered both aspects: first defining the definer by that property, then treating the property as a condition when considering other definitions. Here, 'property' specifically refers to the aggregate of comprehensiveness and exclusiveness, which together form the distinctive property (khassa) of the definer.

Key Terms

الرَّسْم
الخاصَّة

B) The definition is a rasm and the property has two aspects.
C) He made a mistake and corrected it.
D) Conditions are internal to the essence.

Key Takeaway
A property can serve both as a definer (in a rasm) and as a condition for other definitions, depending on whether it is being used to define an essence or to set a requirement.

Objection and Answer on the Definer as Jami' Mani'
Author responds to objection that jam' and man' are conditions external to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential definition (hadd) and descriptive definition (rasm) and explain how the same property can serve both as a definer and a condition depending on the aspect considered.

Core Idea
A single attribute can be both definer and condition, viewed from different aspects.

In this passage, an objector points out a seeming contradiction: the author defined 'the definer' as 'comprehensive and exclusive' (jami' mani'), yet later lists comprehensiveness and exclusivity as mere conditions of definitions. If conditions are external to the essence, how can they be the definition? The author's answer reveals a crucial distinction between defining by essence (hadd) and defining by property (rasm), showing that the same attribute can play both roles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector notes that if jam' and man' are conditions, they are external to the essence of the definer. So defining the definer by them seems inconsistent.
The key is the distinction between essence and property.
2
Categorize the definition as rasm
The author immediately clarifies that the given definition is a rasm (description), not a hadd (essential definition), because it uses a special property (khassa) external to the essence.
Rasm is valid but does not capture the essence.
3
Understand the dual role of khassa
A special property can serve as a definer when it defines the essence by being distinctive, and it can be a condition when the definition is given through something else, like the differentia.
The same property viewed from different angles plays different logical roles.
4
Resolve the apparent contradiction
The author considered both aspects: he defined the definer by its property and also made that property a condition from another consideration. Thus, no contradiction arises.
The aggregate of jam' and man' together is a special property of the definer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد عرفتم لنا المعرّف - باسم الفاعل - بأنه الجامع المانع، أي المنعكس المطرد ، ثم ذكرتم لنا الجمع والمنع من شروط المعرفات - باسم الفاعل ، ومعلوم أن الشرط خارج عن الماهية؟ . فالجواب أن ذلك التعريف رسم؛ لأنه تعريف بالخاصة، وهي خارجة عن الماهية. والواحد بالشخص له جهتان فالخاصة من جهة تعريف الماهية بها فهي معرّف - باسم الفاعل ، وقد تكون شرطاً في التعريف إذا عُرِّف بشيء آخر غيرها كالفصل، ونحن راعينا كليهما من جهة فعرَّفنا ثم جعلناها شرطًا بالاعتبار الآخر . ومقصودنا بالخاصة هنا هو كل من الجمع والمنع، فمجموعهما خاصة للمعرّف - باسم الفاعل ، والله - تعالى - أعلم .
For it is said: You defined for us the definer (as an agent noun) as that which is comprehensive and exclusive, i.e., convertible and coextensive, then you mentioned to us that comprehensiveness and exclusivity are among the conditions of definers (as agent nouns), and it is known that the condition is external to the essence? The answer: That definition is a rasm because it is a definition by a special property, and it is external to the essence. And the one thing considered from two aspects: the special property from the aspect of defining the essence by it is a definer (as agent noun), and it can be a condition in definition if defined by something other than it, like the differentia. And we considered both from an aspect, then we defined, then we made it a condition from the other consideration. And our intention by 'special property' here is each of comprehensiveness and exclusivity, so their aggregate is a special property for the definer (as agent noun). And God Most High knows best.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange, presenting an objection and its answer regarding the definition of 'the definer' (al-mu'arraf bi-ism al-fa'il). The objector points out a seeming inconsistency: the author defined the definer as 'that which is comprehensive and exclusive' (al-jami' al-mani'), i.e., convertible and coextensive, but then listed comprehensiveness and exclusivity as conditions of definers. Since a condition is external to the essence, this would imply that the definition does not capture the essence. The author's answer clarifies that the definition given is a rasm (description), not a hadd (essential definition), because it uses a special property (khassa) which is indeed external to the essence. The author further explains that a single thing can be considered from two aspects: the special property can serve as a definer when defining the essence by it, and can be a condition when defining via something else, like the differentia. Thus, the seeming contradiction resolves by noting that the same attribute can be a definer in one sense and a condition in another. For example, in fiqh, 'comprehensiveness and exclusivity' are not part of the essence of a definition but are properties that a sound definition must possess; they are conditions from one viewpoint and a definition from another. The author concludes that their aggregate is a special property for the definer.

Key Terms
المعرّف
الجامع المانع
الخاصة
الرسم

C) Because it is a definition of a term, not a concept.

Key Takeaway
A definition by special property (rasm) is valid and can coexist with conditions, as the same property can serve different roles from different aspects.

Three Types of Hyperbole in Conditional Statements
Author classifies hyperbole into three types based on possibility of occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of hyperbole (mubalagha) used in conditional statements based on their possibility of occurrence by reason and habit.

Core Idea
Hyperbole is classified by whether the scenario is impossible by reason, habit, or neither.

In classical Arabic rhetoric, hyperbolic conditional statements are not always false; they serve specific purposes. Understanding the three types—tabligh, ighraq, and ghulu—helps you recognize permissible exaggeration from impossibility, a crucial skill when analyzing arguments in Islamic disciplines like balagha and munazara.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of classification
The author classifies hyperbole by examining two criteria: reason ('aql) and habit ('ada). Determine whether the statement is impossible by reason, by habit, both, or neither.
Reason refers to logical possibility; habit refers to what normally occurs.
2
Identify the first type: tabligh
Tabligh is a hyperbolic statement whose occurrence is improbable but not prevented by reason or habit. Example: Nabigha Dhubyan's verses. The statement is exaggerated but could theoretically happen.
Tabligh means 'exaggeration' in this context, not 'delivery'.
3
Identify the second type: ighraq
Ighraq is a statement that is impossible by habit but possible by reason. The example from Qays ibn al-Mulawwah: an echo after death is habitually impossible but rationally conceivable.
Ighraq is 'extravagance'—pushing beyond normal experience.
4
Identify the third type: ghulu
Ghulu is a statement impossible both by reason and by habit. The author notes that examples are well-known in balagha and refers to his own treatise on metaphor.
Ghulu is 'excess'—pure impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : نوع مستبعد الوقوع ، ولكنه لا يمنعه العقل ولا العادة، كالشرطية في بيتي نابغة ذبيان المذكورين . وهذا النوع من أنواع المبالغة هو المعروف عندهم بالتبليغ، وإنما ساغ مع عدم صدقه للغرض المذكور في قولهم : لئلا يُظن أنه غير متناه فيه) . النوع الثاني : هو المستحيل عادة وإن كان يجوز عقلاً. ومن الشرطية في بيتي قيس بن الملوّح المذكورين؛ لأن طرب صدى صوته بعد الموت لصدى صوت ليلى تُحيله العادة وإن أمكن عقلاً، وإنما جاز مع عدم صدقه للغرض المذكور، وهذا النوع من أنواع المبالغة هو المعروف عندهم بالإغراق . النوع الثالث : ما استحال عقلاً وعادة، وهو المعروف بالغلو، وأمثلته والتفصيلُ بين المقبول منه وغير المقبول معروفة في البديع من فن البلاغة ولسنا نريد تفاصيل ذلك هنا، وقد فصلناها في رسالتنا في منع المجاز في القرآن (۱) .
First: a type whose occurrence is improbable, but neither reason nor habit prevents it, like the conditional in the two verses of Nabigha Dhubyan mentioned earlier. This type of hyperbole is known among them as tabligh (exaggeration), and it is permissible despite its lack of truthfulness for the purpose mentioned in their saying: 'lest it be thought that he is not extreme in it.' Second: that which is impossible by habit though permissible by reason. From the conditional in the two verses of Qays ibn al-Mulawwah mentioned earlier; because the echoing of his voice after death responding to the echo of Layla's voice is impossible by habit though possible by reason, and it is permissible despite its lack of truthfulness for the purpose mentioned. This type of hyperbole is known among them as ighraq (extravagance). Third: that which is impossible both by reason and habit, known as ghulu (excess). Examples and the distinction between acceptable and unacceptable are well known in the badi' (rhetorical figures) in the discipline of balagha, and we do not intend to detail that here; we have detailed it in our treatise on preventing metaphor in the Quran (1).
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines three types of hyperbole (mubalagha) used in conditional statements, particularly in poetic contexts. The author explains each type in succession. The first type, tabligh, describes a scenario that is unlikely but not impossible either rationally or habitually. The example is from Nabigha Dhubyan's verses. The second type, ighraq, describes something impossible by habit but possible rationally. The example is from Qays ibn al-Mulawwah's verses, where the idea of an echo responding after death is habitually impossible. The third type, ghulu, is impossible both rationally and habitually. The author notes that the details of acceptable and unacceptable forms are covered in the science of balagha and refers to his own work on the rejection of metaphor in the Quran.

Key Terms
المبالغة
التبليغ
الإغراق
الغلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of hyperbole is impossible by habit but possible by reason?
A) Tabligh
B) Ighraq
C) Ghulu
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Hyperbole in conditional statements is classified into tabligh (possible but improbable), ighraq (habitually impossible but rationally possible), and ghulu (absolutely impossible), and each is permissible for rhetorical effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following conditional statements, classify each as tabligh, ighraq, or ghulu: (1) 'If I saw the caliph, I would give him my kingdom' (improbable but not impossible). (2) 'If the dead could speak, they would testify to his generosity' (impossible by habit but rationally possible). (3) 'If time could stand still, I would praise him forever' (impossible both by reason and habit).

Disjunctive Conditional: Opposition and the First Type (Man'at al-Jam' wal-Khuluw)
Author defines disjunctive conditional, divides opposition into three, and explains the first type (true exclusive disjunction).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of opposition in disjunctive conditionals and identify the true exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam' wal-khuluw).

Core Idea
True exclusive disjunction requires mutual exclusion in both truth and falsehood.

In the previous lessons we studied the conditional proposition in general. Now we turn to the disjunctive conditional, which depends on opposition between its two parts. This passage defines the disjunctive conditional, divides opposition into three types, and explores the first type—the strongest and most common in Islamic logical and legal reasoning. Understanding this division is essential for correctly evaluating disjunctive arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of disjunctive conditional
The author states that a disjunctive conditional must have opposition ('inad) between its two parts. He equates opposition with mutual exclusion and impossibility of conjunction.
Opposition is the defining feature; without it, the proposition is not truly disjunctive.
2
Identify the threefold division of opposition
The author divides opposition into three rational types: (1) in existence and non-existence together, (2) in existence only, (3) in non-existence only. This division is exhaustive according to reason.
These correspond to mutually exclusive and exhaustive possibilities: both cannot be true/false, cannot both be true, cannot both be false.
3
Analyze the first type: man'at al-jam' wal-khuluw
Here the parts cannot be true together nor false together; one must be true and the other false. This is the true disjunctive conditional, composed only from either two contradictories or a thing and its equivalent of a contradictory.
Examples: 'The number is either even or not even' (contradictories) and 'The number is either even or odd' (equivalent of contradictory).
4
Recognize the requirement of contradictories or equivalents
The author emphasizes that this strong disjunction can only be formed from two contradictories (direct opposites like 'even' and 'not even') or from a thing and a term that is equivalent to its contradictory (like 'odd' = 'not even').
This ensures that the disjunction is truly exhaustive and exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشرطية المنفصلة فضابطها أنها لابد أن يكون بين طرفيها عناد في الجملة . واعلم أن المراد بالعناد هنا والتنافرِ شيء واحد، وهو تنافي الطرفين، واستحالة اجتماعهما والعناد المذكور بين الطرفين هو معنى كونها منفصلة . والتقسيم العقلي الصحيح يحصر العناد المذكور في ثلاثة أقسام لا رابع لها : الأول: أن يكون في الوجود والعدم معاً. الثاني : أن يكون في الوجود فقط . الثالث : أن يكون في العدم فقط . بمعنى أن طرفيها لا يمكن اجتماعهما في الوجود ولا في العدم، فلا يوجدان معا ولا يُعدمان معًا، بل لابد من وجود أحدهما وعدم الآخر، فهي المعروفة بالشرطية المنفصلة الحقيقية، وتسمى مانعة الجمع والخُلُوِّ معا، ولا تتركب إلا من النقيضين أو من الشيء ومساوي نقيضه، ومثالها في النقيضين قولك : العدد إما زوج أو ليس بزوج)، ومثالها في الشيء ومساوي نقيضه : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد)؛ لأن لفظة (فرد) مساوية لـ ليس بزوج)، وهي نقيض (العدد زوج).
As for the disjunctive conditional, its rule is that there must be opposition between its two parts in general. Know that what is meant by opposition and incompatibility here is one thing: the mutual exclusion of the two parts and the impossibility of their conjunction. The mentioned opposition between the two parts is the meaning of its being disjunctive. The correct rational division confines the mentioned opposition to three divisions with no fourth: First, it is in existence and non-existence together. Second, it is in existence only. Third, it is in non-existence only. Meaning that its two parts cannot be combined in existence or in non-existence, so they do not exist together nor are they both absent together, but rather one must exist and the other be absent. This is the known true disjunctive conditional, called preventer of conjunction and disjunction together (man'at al-jam' wal-khuluw ma'an). It is composed only of two contradictories or of a thing and its equivalent of its contradictory. An example of two contradictories: 'The number is either even or not even.' An example of a thing and its equivalent of its contradictory: 'The number is either even or odd' because the word 'odd' is equivalent to 'not even', which is the contradictory of 'the number is even'.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines the disjunctive conditional (shartiyya munfasila) and explains its fundamental condition: the two parts must be in opposition ('inad). The author clarifies that 'inad means mutual exclusion, i.e., they cannot both be true. Then he presents a rational division of opposition into three types: (1) in existence and non-existence together, (2) in existence only, and (3) in non-existence only. He then elaborates on the first type, which is the strongest: when the opposition covers both existence and non-existence, the two parts cannot both be true nor both false; one must be true and the other false. This is the 'true' disjunctive conditional, also called man'at al-jam' wal-khuluw (preventer of conjunction and disjunction). It can only be formed from two contradictories (naqidān) or from a thing and the equivalent of its contradictory (musāwī naqīḍihi). Examples: using 'even' and 'not even' (direct contradictories) or 'even' and 'odd' (where 'odd' is equivalent to 'not even').

Key Terms
الشرطية المنفصلة
العناد
مانعة الجمع والخُلُوّ
النقيضان
مساوي النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must be the relation between the two parts of a true disjunctive conditional (man'at al-jam' wal-khuluw)?
A) They can both be true but not both false
B) They can both be false but not both true
C) They cannot be both true nor both false
D) They can be both true and both false

Key Takeaway
The true disjunctive conditional requires that its two parts be mutually exclusive in both truth and falsehood, and it can only be built from contradictories or their equivalents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Evaluate the statement 'The world is either created or eternal (i.e., not created)'. Is this a true exclusive disjunction? Explain why, referencing the conditions of man'at al-jam' wal-khuluw.
?
Task 2: Construct your own exclusive disjunction using a theological example (e.g., regarding divine attributes like 'God is either knowing or ignorant') and verify that it fits the definition of man'at al-jam' wal-khuluw.

How Definitions Implicitly Contain Propositions
Author explains the transformation of a definition into multiple claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the implicit declarative propositions contained within a given definition from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A definition is a single term in function but a bundle of claims in substance.

Definitions appear as single terms or incomplete composites, but the author reveals that they secretly contain complete sentences. Understanding this unlocks why definitions are disputable in munazara. This lesson unpacks the concept of the restrictive composite (murakkab taqyidi) and shows how it entails multiple propositions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the surface form of a definition
A definition like 'rational animal' is a restrictive composite (murakkab taqyidi). Look at its structure: it is a phrase that functions like a single term because it is equivalent (musawi) to the definiendum (e.g., 'human').
In Arabic logic, such composites are treated as terms for the purpose of discussion.
2
Understand the equivalence to a single term
The composite 'rational animal' is equivalent to 'human'. Since the definiendum is a single term, its equivalent is also considered a single term. This allows the definition to be used as a unit in arguments.
Equivalence (musawāt) is key: the definition and definiendum are interchangeable.
3
Unpack the definition into implicit claims
Despite being a single term in usage, the definition contains several complete declarative propositions. For example, 'human is rational animal' implies claims about its type (hadd vs rasm), its components (essential vs accidental), its scope (inclusion/exclusion), and its logical soundness (no circularity).
The author lists five such claims for the human definition.
4
Verify the claims from the passage
Review the passage to see the exact five claims: (1) it is a hadd, (2) composed of essential predicates, (3) prevents entry of non-definiendum, (4) encompasses all individuals, (5) entails no impossibility like circularity.
These claims are the basis for disputation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبلاغيين والمنطقيين، وما يسميه به كل منهم - فهو محل البحث ، والمناظرة، وعليه ترد اعتراضات المعترض وإجابة المجيب، كما ستری تفاصيله إن شاء الله تعالى . واعلم أن التعريفات والتقسيمات قد تجري فيها المناظرة، فإن قيل: كيف جرت فيها وهي لا تخلو أن تكون من قبيل المفرد أو المركب الناقص ؟ فالجواب أن المناظرة إنما جرت فيها من حيث إنها متضمنة مركبات خبرية تامة . وإيضاح ذلك في التعريف أنك لو عرفت الإنسان مثلاً بأنه هو الحيوان الناطق فهذا المركب التقييدي - الذي هو الحيوان الناطق الذي هو في قوة المفرد ؛ لأنه يساوي الإنسان، ومساوي المفرد مفرد - يتضمن مركبات خبرية تامة فكأنك ادعيت في ضمن ذلك التعريف الدعاوى الآتية : الأولى: أن هذا التعريف حدٌ لا رسم . والثانية : أنه مؤلف من الذاتيات، لا العرضيات . والثالثة والرابعة : أنه مانع من دخول غير المحدود، جامع لجميع أفراد المحدود. والخامسة : أنه لا يستلزم شيئًا من أنواع المحال، كالدور السبقي والتسلسل ونحو ذلك . فجريان المناظرة في التعريف باعتبار هذه القضايا الخبرية التامة التي تضمنها التعريف، وقد تقدم جميع إيضاحها في المقدمة المنطقية .
And the rhetoricians and logicians, and whatever each of them calls it - that is the subject of investigation and debate, and upon it objections of the objector and responses of the respondent are made, as you will see its details if Allah wills. Know that definitions and divisions may be the subject of disputation. If it is said: How can disputation occur in them while they are of the type of the single term or the incomplete composite? The answer is that disputation only occurs in them insofar as they contain complete declarative composites. The clarification of that concerning definition is that if you defined human, for example, as 'rational animal', this restrictive composite - which is 'rational animal' which is like a single term because it is equivalent to human, and the equivalent of a single term is a single term - contains complete declarative composites, such that you implicitly claimed within that definition the following claims: First: that this definition is a real definition (hadd) not a description (rasm). Second: that it is composed of essential predicates, not accidental ones. Third and fourth: that it prevents the entry of what is not the definiendum and encompasses all individuals of the definiendum. Fifth: that it does not entail any kind of impossibility, such as prior circularity and infinite regress and the like. So disputation in definitions occurs with respect to these complete declarative propositions that the definition contains, and all their clarification has preceded in the Logical Introduction.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage explains how disputation (munazara) can take place concerning definitions and divisions, even though they appear to be single terms or incomplete composites. The author first states that definitions and divisions may be subject to debate. A potential objection is raised: how can there be debate about something that is a single term (mufrad) or an incomplete composite (murakkab naqis)? The answer is that these definitions and divisions implicitly contain complete declarative sentences (murakkabat khabariyya tamma). The author then illustrates this with the example of defining 'human' as 'rational animal' (al-hayawan al-natiq). This restrictive composite (murakkab taqyidi) is like a single term because it is equivalent (musawi) to the definiendum, but it entails several claims. The author lists five implicit claims: 1) that the definition is a real definition (hadd) and not a mere description (rasm); 2) that it consists of essential predicates (dhatiyyat) and not accidental ones (aradiyyat); 3-4) that it is coextensive with the definiendum (mani' and jami'); 5) that it does not involve any kind of impossibility like circularity or infinite regress. Thus, disputation concerning definitions amounts to challenging these implicit claims. This explanation uses an example from logic (defining 'human') which is standard in Islamic logic textbooks. The author references an earlier part of the book (the Logical Introduction) where these points were detailed.

Disputing Definitions: The Five Implicit Claims
Author explains how definitions entail five claims and thus enable disputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to formulate objections against each of the five implicit claims in a given definition used in Islamic scholarly debate.

Core Idea
Every definition is a battleground of five claims.

The author reveals that a definition is not a simple term but a package of five disputable assertions. In munazara, each claim can be challenged: Is it really a hadd? Are the components essential? Does it include all? Exclude others? Does it involve circularity? Mastering these five objections is key to dialectical skill.

Guided Path
1
Recall the five implicit claims
From the passage, list the five claims: (1) it is a hadd not rasm; (2) composed of essential vs accidental; (3) prevents entry of non-definiendum; (4) encompasses all individuals; (5) entails no impossibility.
Write these down as a checklist.
2
Understand the logical significance of each claim
Each claim has a specific logical role: claim 1 concerns the type of definition; claim 2 concerns the ontological status of components; claims 3-4 concern coextensiveness; claim 5 concerns consistency.
These are evaluative criteria for definitions.
3
Formulate potential objections for each claim
For claim 1, one could object that the definition is merely a rasm (description) not a hadd. For claim 2, that one of the components is accidental. For claims 3/4, that it either fails to include all instances or includes non-instances. For claim 5, that it involves circularity.
Objections must be grounded in the actual definiendum and its properties.
4
Apply to an Islamic definition
Choose a definition from fiqh or kalam (e.g., 'the Quran is the uncreated speech of Allah'). Identify which of the five claims it makes, and then formulate one plausible objection per claim.
This builds practical debative skill.

Two Functions of Logical Division: Definition and Proof Component
Two functions of division: definition and proof component

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two roles of logical division (taqseem): as an independent definition for concepts and as a component of the proof method sabr wa-taqseem.

Core Idea
Logical division serves both as a complete definition and as the first pillar of a composite proof.

In this passage, the author explains that division (taqseem) is not merely a classificatory tool but can function as a complete definition in the domain of concepts (tasawwurāt) and as a crucial part of a proof in the domain of assents (taṣdīqāt). We will explore both functions using the example of defining knowledge and the method known in uṣūl al-fiqh as sabr wa-taqseem.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish tasawwur vs tasdiq
The author begins by separating the two realms: concepts (tasawwur) are mental representations, while assents (tasdiq) are judgments of truth. Division works differently in each.
2
Analyze the definition of knowledge via division
Look at the example: belief is divided into certain vs uncertain; certain is divided into conforming vs non-conforming; conforming is divided into stable vs not stable. The result is a definition: certain, conforming, stable belief is knowledge. Note how each division eliminates alternatives.
This shows division as an independent proof for a concept.
3
See how division yields a complete definition
The author states that this division alone provides the concept of knowledge. No external proof is needed. The definition includes genus (certain belief) and differentiae (conforming, stable, unquestionable).
This is a hadd haqiqi (true definition) achieved through division.
4
Understand division as a component of sabr wa-taqseem
In assents, division forms one pillar of a composite proof. The method sabr wa-taqseem has two parts: exhaustive division (taqseem) and testing (sabr). Division lists all possibilities; sabr eliminates the invalid ones.
Division alone does not yield assent; it must be combined with testing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل اعلم أن التقسيم قد يكون دليلاً مستقلاً في التصورات، وجزءً دليل في التصديقات . أما كونه دليلاً مستقلاً في التصورات فقد أشرنا إليه في المقدمة المنطقية في الكلام على المُعرِّفات، وبينا أن (القسمة) من المُعرِّفات التي تتميز بها الحقيقة المعرَّفة عن غيرها، ومرادنا بالقسمة التقسيم المذكور، ومثلنا لذلك بتعريف (العلم) بالتقسيم بأن نقول : الاعتقاد إما جازم أو غير جازم، والجازم إما مطابق أو غير مطابق، والمطابق إما ثابت لا يقبل التشكيك بحال وإما لا، فظهر من التقسيم : اعتقاد جازم مطابق ثابت لا يقبل التشكيك بحال، وهو (العلم)، كما قدمنا إيضاحه وما يحترز عنه بكل لفظ من ألفاظ التقسيم المذكورة، فهذا التقسيم صار دليلاً مستقلاً لتصور (العلم)؛ لأنه يحصل به تصور (العلم) بأنه اعتقاد جازم مطابق ثابت لا يقبل التشكيل بحال، كالعلم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، وأن الكل أكبر من الجزء . وأما كون التقسيم جزء دليل في التصديقات فلأن الدليل المعروف عند أهل الأصول بالسبر والتقسيم ، وعند الجدليين بالتقسيم والترديد، متركب من أصلين: أحدهما التقسيم وهو حصر جميع أقسام المقسَّم، والثاني هو سبر تلك الأقسام كلها، أي اختبارها وتمييز ما يصلح منها وما لا يصلح وبه تعلم أن التقسيم صار أحد ركني هذا الدليل التصديقي، وقسمه الثاني هو سبر الأقسام - أى اختبارها ، فإذا
Chapter: Know that division may be an independent proof in concepts (tasawwurat) and part of a proof in assents (tasdiqat). As for it being an independent proof in concepts, we have referred to it in the logical introduction in the discussion of definitions, and we explained that division (al-qisma) is among the definitions by which a defined reality is distinguished from others, and by division we mean the mentioned classification. We exemplified that by defining 'knowledge' through division by saying: Belief is either certain or non-certain; certain is either conforming or non-conforming; conforming is either stable and not subject to doubt at all or not. So from the division appears: a certain, conforming belief that is stable and not subject to doubt at all, which is 'knowledge', as we have previously clarified its explanation and what each word of the mentioned division guards against. So this division became an independent proof for the concept of 'knowledge' because it yields the concept of 'knowledge' as a certain, conforming belief that is stable and not subject to doubt at all, like the knowledge that one is half of two, and that the whole is greater than the part. As for division being part of a proof in assents, it is because the proof known to the scholars of usul as 'sabr wa-taqseem' (testing and division) and to the dialecticians as 'taqseem wa-tardid' (division and alternation) is composed of two principles: one is the division, which is the exhaustive enumeration of all divisions of the divided thing, and the second is the testing (sabr) of all those divisions, i.e., examining them and distinguishing what is suitable from what is not. And by this you know that division becomes one of the two pillars of this assent-based proof, and its second part is the testing of the divisions—i.e., examining them. So when...
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage introduces a chapter on the dual function of logical division (taqseem). The author states that division can serve two roles: first, as an independent proof for concepts (tasawwurat), meaning it can itself serve as a complete definition (mu'arraf) that differentiates the defined thing from others. He gives the example of defining 'ilm (knowledge) through a series of divisions: belief is either certain or not; certain belief is either conforming to reality or not; conforming belief is either stable and unquestionable or not. The result is the definition of knowledge as 'a certain, conforming belief that is stable and not subject to doubt.' This division alone provides the concept of knowledge. The second role is as a component of a proof for assents (tasdiqat). Here, division is part of the method known in usul al-fiqh as 'sabr wa-taqseem' (testing and division) and in dialectic as 'taqseem wa-tardid' (division and alternation). This method consists of two steps: first, exhaustively enumerating all possible divisions of the subject, and second, testing each division to see which one is valid. Division thus forms one pillar of this composite proof. The passage ends mid-sentence at the beginning of explaining further.

Key Takeaway
Logical division can serve as a complete definition in conceptual knowledge and as the exhaustive enumeration step in the sabr wa-taqseem proof method for assents.

Division as Independent Proof in Conceptualizations
Author explains how division serves as an independent definer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a definition by division (taqsim) and verify each division step exhausts the genus.

Core Idea
Division can define a concept without a separate proof.

In logic, division is not just a classificatory tool; it can serve as a complete definer. The author shows how a hierarchical division of belief leads to the precise definition of 'knowledge' (al-ilm). Understanding this method sharpens your ability to define concepts in Islamic sciences with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept to be defined
In the passage, the concept is 'knowledge' (العلم). Start with the broadest genus.
2
Perform a binary division
Divide the genus into two mutually exclusive and jointly exhaustive parts, e.g., belief into certain and not certain.
Each division must be exhaustive (thing and its negation).
3
Eliminate irrelevant branches
Continue dividing each remaining branch until you reach a single, uneliminated branch that captures the concept. E.g., certain belief is divided into corresponding and not corresponding; corresponding into stable and not stable.
The final uneliminated branch is the definition.
4
Verify exhaustiveness
Check that at each level, the division covers all possibilities and the eliminated branches truly do not apply to the concept.

Source Text

فصل اعلم أن التقسيم قد يكون دليلاً مستقلاً في التصورات، وجزء دليل في التصديقات . أما كونه دليلاً مستقلاً في التصورات فقد أشرنا إليه في المقدمة المنطقية في الكلام على المعرِّفات، وبينا أن (القسمة) من المعرِّفات التي تتميز بها الحقيقة المعرَّفة عن غيرها، ومرادنا بالقسمة التقسيم المذكور، ومثلنا لذلك بتعريف (العلم) بالتقسيم بأن نقول : الاعتقاد إما جازم أو غير جازم، والجازم إما مطابق أو غير مطابق، والمطابق إما ثابت لا يقبل التشكيك بحال وإما لا، فظهر من التقسيم : اعتقاد جازم مطابق ثابت لا يقبل التشكيك بحال، وهو (العلم)، كما قدمنا إيضاحه وما يحترز عنه بكل لفظ من ألفاظ التقسيم المذكورة، فهذا التقسيم صار دليلاً مستقلاً لتصور (العلم)؛ لأنه يحصل به تصور (العلم) بأنه اعتقاد جازم مطابق ثابت لا يقبل التشكيل بحال، كالعلم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، وأن الكل أكبر من الجزء . وأما كون التقسيم جزء دليل في التصديقات فلأن الدليل المعروف عند أهل الأصول بالسبر والتقسيم ، وعند الجدليين بالتقسيم والترديد، متركب من أصلين: أحدهما التقسيم وهو حصر جميع أقسام المقسَّم، والثاني هو سبر تلك الأقسام كلها، أي اختبارها وتمييز ما يصلح منها وما لا يصلح وبه تعلم أن التقسيم صار أحد ركني هذا الدليل التصديقي، وقسمه الثاني هو سبر الأقسام - أى اختبارها، فإذا صح التقسيم واستوفى شروطه المذكورة وصح سبرها وهو اختبار صحيحها من فاسدها، وصالحها من غيره كان الأمران المذكوران دليلاً تصديقيًا، والتقسيم جزء منه . وهذا الدليل الذي هو السبر والتقسيم يستعمله المنطقيون في مقاصد أخر ، وهو ما قدمنا أنهم يسمونه الشرطي المنفصل واعلم أنا قد بسطنا الكلام في هذا الدليل في كتابنا «أضواء البيان في إيضاح القرآن بالقرآن في سورة مريم، في الكلام على قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ أَطَّلَعَ الْغَيْبَ أَمِ اتَّخَذَ عِندَ الرَّحْمَنِ عَهْدًا، وبينا المراد به عند الأصوليين والجدليين والمنطقيين، وأوضحنا الفرق بين ما تريد به كل منهم وبين ما تريد به الأخرى، وذكرنا له أربعة أمثلة قرآنية، وبينا بعض آثاره التاريخية العقائدية والأدبية، ولذلك سنختصر الكلام عليه هنا، فنذكر له مثالاً في القرآن ومثالاً في الأصول، ومثالاً في الجدل. فمن أمثلته في القرآن قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ أَمْ خُلِقُوا مِنْ غَيْرِ شَيْءٍ أَمْ هُمُ الْخَالِقُونَ ﴾ [الطور / ٣٥] . وقد قدمنا أن التقسيم في هذه الآية عقلي، وإنما كانت الأقسام ثلاثة لأنهما تقسيمان عقليان قطعيان لأنا نقول : إما أن يكونوا خُلقوا بلا خالق أصلاً ، أو خلقهم خالق، وهذا تقسيم عقلي قطعي ؛ لحصره في الشيء ونقيضه، ثم نقسم القسم الثاني الذي هو قولنا : أو خلقهم خالق، فنقول : لا يخلو ذلك الخالق من أن يكون أنفسهم أو ليس بأنفسهم، وهذا أيضًا تقسيم عقلي قطعي ؛ لحصره في الشيء ونقيضه،
Chapter. Know that division may be an independent proof in conceptualizations, and a part of a proof in assents. As for its being an independent proof in conceptualizations, we have already pointed to it in the logical introduction in the discussion of definers, and we explained that (division) is among the definers by which the defined reality is distinguished from others, and we mean by division the aforementioned division, and we exemplified that by defining (knowledge) through division by saying: Belief is either certain or not certain; the certain is either corresponding or not corresponding; the corresponding is either stable and not subject to doubt at all or not. So from the division appears: a certain, corresponding, stable belief not subject to doubt at all, which is (knowledge), as we have previously clarified and what is guarded against by each word of the mentioned division. Thus this division became an independent proof for the conceptualization of (knowledge) because through it the conceptualization of (knowledge) is obtained as a certain, corresponding, stable belief not subject to doubt at all, like knowledge that one is half of two, and that the whole is greater than the part. As for division being part of a proof in assents, because the proof known among the scholars of usul as trial-and-division, and among the dialecticians as division-and-alternation, is composed of two principles: one of them is division, which is the exhaustive enumeration of all divisions of the divided thing; the second is the testing of all those divisions, i.e., examining them and distinguishing what is valid from what is not. And through this it is known that division became one of the two pillars of this assertoric proof, and its second part is the testing of the divisions – that is, their examination. So if the division is sound and fulfills its mentioned conditions, and its testing – which is distinguishing the sound from the unsound and the valid from the invalid – is sound, then the two mentioned matters constitute an assertoric proof, and division is part of it. And this proof, which is trial-and-division, is used by logicians for other purposes, and it is what we have previously stated they call the exclusive conditional. And know that we have expanded the discussion about this proof in our book 'Adwa' al-Bayan fi Idah al-Quran bil-Quran' in Surah Maryam, in the discussion of His – exalted – statement: {Did he have knowledge of the unseen or did he take a covenant with the Most Merciful?} And we explained what is intended by it among the usuliyyun, dialecticians, and logicians, and we clarified the difference between what each of them intends by it and what the other intends, and we mentioned four Quranic examples for it, and we explained some of its historical, creedal, and literary effects. Therefore we will abbreviate the discussion here, and we will mention an example from the Quran, an example from usul, and an example from dialectic. Among its examples in the Quran is His – exalted – statement: {Or were they created from nothing, or are they the creators?} [At-Tur 35]. And we have already stated that the division in this verse is rational, and that the divisions are three because they are two rational, certain divisions: for we say: Either they were created without any creator at all, or a creator created them. This is a rational, certain division because it exhausts the thing and its negation. Then we divide the second division, which is our statement: 'or a creator created them,' and we say: That creator is either themselves or not themselves. This too is a rational, certain division because it exhausts the thing and its negation.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage defines the logical function of division (taqsim). The author begins by asserting that division can serve two distinct roles: an independent proof in the domain of conceptualizations (tasawwurat) and a part of a proof in the domain of assents (tasdiqat). To illustrate the first role, he revisits a previous example of defining 'knowledge' (al-ilm) through a hierarchical division. Belief is first divided into certain and uncertain; certain belief into corresponding and non-corresponding; corresponding belief into stable and not stable. The final product – certain, corresponding, stable belief – is the definition of knowledge. This shows how division can produce a concept without needing a separate proof, hence it is an independent proof for conceptualization. The author then addresses the second role: division as a component of proof in assents. He identifies the well-known method 'sabr wa taqsim' (trial and division) used by usul scholars (fiqh methodology) and dialecticians. This method has two pillars: first, exhaustive division of the subject; second, testing (sabr) each division to determine which is valid. If both steps are properly executed, the result is a valid assertoric proof. The author notes that logicians also use this method, referring to it as the 'exclusive conditional' (sharti munfasil). He mentions his own expanded treatment in 'Adwa' al-Bayan' and promises to give three examples: from the Quran, from usul, and from dialectic. He then presents a Quranic example: {Or were they created from nothing, or are they the creators?} (52:35). He explains that the verse implies a rational division: either they were created without a creator, or a creator created them. Then the second alternative is further divided: either the creator is themselves or not themselves. These divisions are certain because they exhaust the thing and its negation. Thus, the overall structure illustrates how division functions as part of a dialectical proof.

Key Terms
التقسيم
تصور
تصديق
العلم

Checkpoint
In the example defining 'knowledge' through division, which division step eliminates non-corresponding beliefs?
A) Belief is either certain or not certain
B) Certain belief is either corresponding or not corresponding
C) Corresponding belief is either stable or not stable
D) Belief is either jāzim or ghayr jāzim

Key Takeaway
Division can function as a standalone definer in conceptualization by exhaustively narrowing down the genus to the intended concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Use division to define 'salah' (prayer). Start with the genus 'act of worship', then divide into bodily, financial, etc., and narrow down to the specific combination that defines salah.
?
In the passage's example of defining knowledge, identify which division eliminates the possibility of mere opinion (zann).

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
10 min
Division as Part of Proof in Assents: Sabr wa Taqsim
Author explains the structure of sabr wa taqsim and gives a Quranic example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two components of sabr wa taqsim and explain how they together form a valid assertoric proof.

Core Idea
Sabr wa taqsim is a two-pillar method: exhaustive division plus testing each division.

In assents (tasdiqat), division becomes part of a larger proof known as sabr wa taqsim (trial and division). This method is fundamental in usul al-fiqh and dialectic. The author explains its structure and provides a Quranic example that illustrates how rational division leads to a certain conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject to be divided
In the Quranic example, the subject is the origin of human creation: 'Were they created from nothing, or are they the creators?'
The subject is a question that requires a proof.
2
Perform exhaustive division (taqsim)
Divide the possibilities into mutually exclusive and jointly exhaustive parts. The first division: either created without a creator, or created by a creator. The second division of the second part: either the creator is themselves, or not themselves.
Each division is based on the law of excluded middle (thing and its negation).
3
Test each division (sabr)
Examine each possibility to see if it is valid. For example, they were not created from nothing (absurd), and they are not the creators (since they are created beings).
Testing eliminates the invalid divisions.
4
Draw the conclusion
The only remaining possibility (they were created by a creator other than themselves) is the valid one. This conclusion is certain because all other possibilities were eliminated through exhaustive division and testing.
